The Syllogism
intermediate
10 min
The Limitation of the First-Figure-like Compound Disjunctive Syllogism
Author discusses the shape resembling the first figure and shows why it does not yield universal conclusions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a compound disjunctive syllogism resembling the first figure fails to yield a necessary conclusion and state the condition required for it to produce a valid inference.

Core Idea
A compound disjunctive syllogism in first-figure form fails unless the disjuncts have a specific opposition.

The author examines a specific type of compound disjunctive syllogism where the common term is incomplete, creating a shape that resembles the first figure of categorical syllogisms. He shows that this shape does not automatically produce a valid conclusion. Through examples, we will see why transitivity fails and learn the precise condition under which a conclusion might follow.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim under critique
The passage targets the claim that a compound disjunctive syllogism with an incomplete common term, when structured like the first figure, yields a genuine necessary conclusion. Locate the phrase 'وقد قيل فيه' where this claim is introduced.
The claim is that the most natural form is two universal affirmative premises in the first conjunction.
2
Examine the example with mutually exclusive disjuncts
Analyze the given example: 'Always every number is either even or odd' and 'Always every even is either even-of-even, even-of-odd, or even-of-both'. The attempted conclusion would categorize all numbers, but the author says it is not genuine.
Note that even and odd are mutually exclusive (متباينان).
3
Analyze why transitivity fails
The author argues that from judging one disjunct (e.g., even) with a property, it does not necessarily apply to the other (odd), because the property might apply to both or only one. This lack of uniformity (لا اطراد) breaks the transitivity needed for a universal conclusion.
Transitivity (التعدية) here means carrying a judgment from one disjunct to another.
4
Test a modification with non-mutually exclusive disjuncts
Consider a changed example: 'Every even is either old or new', where 'old' and 'new' have no mutual exclusivity (لا معاندة). The author shows that even then, no valid conclusion follows, except possibly under a weaker condition.
The weaker condition is 'prevention of emptiness without prevention of combination' (المنع من الخلو دون الجمع).
5
Determine the only condition for validity
The passage concludes that the only scenario where a conclusion might emerge is when the disjuncts are opposed such that they cannot both be false (prevention of emptiness) but can both be true (no prevention of combination). This is insufficient for a universal necessary conclusion.
This condition does not guarantee a genuine categorical result.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إن كان الجزء المشترك غير تام فإما أن يكون محمولاً في أجزاء الانفصال في أحدهما وموضوعاً لأجزاء الانفصال في الأخرى، أو أنه محمول في أجزاء الانفصال فيهما، أو أنه موضوع لأجزاء الانفصال فيهما، فإن كان الأول فهو على هيئة الشكل الأول، وقد قيل فيه أن القريب من الطبع منه ما كان من كليتين موجبتين من الاقتران الأول منه كقولنا: (دائماً كل عدد إما زوج وإما فرد) و(دائماً كل زوج إما زوج الزوج أو زوج الفرد أو زوج الزوج والفرد معاً) وليس بحق إن قيل أن النتيجة حقيقية، فإن الزوج والفرد متباينان ولا يلزم من الحكم على أحدهما بحكم أن يكون حكماً على الآخر؛ لجواز أن يعمها فتلزم التعدية، أو يخص أحدهما فلا تلزم التعدية، فلا اطراد ولهذا فإنا لو غيرنا المادة في المثال المذكور بقولنا: (وكل زوج فإما قديم وما حادث وبما لا معاندة بينه وبين الجزء الآخر، فإنه لا ينتج العدد إما فرد قديم وإما حادث) إذ لا عناد بين هذه الأجزاء، إلا بالمنع من الخلو دون الجمع، وعلى هذا فلا يخفى الحكم في باقي اقتراناته.
And if the common part is incomplete, then either it is a predicate in the disjunctive parts of one [premise] and a subject for the disjunctive parts of the other, or it is a predicate in the disjunctive parts of both, or it is a subject for the disjunctive parts of both. If it is the first, then it is in the form of the first figure, and it has been said concerning it that what is close to nature among them is what consists of two universal affirmatives from the first conjunction, like our saying: 'Always every number is either even or odd' and 'Always every even is either even of even or even of odd or even of both even and odd'. But it is not correct if it is said that the result is genuine, because even and odd are mutually exclusive, and from judging one of them with a judgment, it does not necessitate that it be a judgment on the other, because it is possible that it applies to both, then transitivity is necessary, or it applies to one of them, then transitivity is not necessary, so there is no uniformity. For this reason, if we change the matter in the mentioned example by saying: 'And every even is either old or new, but with no contradictoriness between it and the other part', then it does not produce 'the number is either an old odd or a new odd', because there is no opposition between these parts, except by prevention of emptiness without prevention of combination. So on this basis, the rule in the rest of its conjunctions is not hidden.
Source pages p. 297

Commentary
This passage discusses the case where the common part (the term shared between the two premises) is 'incomplete', meaning it does not appear fully as a subject or predicate. The author classifies three possibilities: (1) the common part is a predicate in one premise's disjunctive parts and a subject in the other's; (2) predicate in both; (3) subject in both. He then focuses on the first possibility, which resembles the first figure of categorical syllogisms. A claim is mentioned that the most natural form is when both premises are universal affirmative in the 'first conjunction' (the first type of compound disjunctive). The author criticizes this: the conclusion from such a syllogism is not necessarily genuine. He gives the example: 'Every number is either even or odd' and 'Every even is either even-of-even, even-of-odd, or even-of-both'. The supposed conclusion would be that every number is either odd or one of these subdivisions, but this is not valid because the predicates 'even' and 'odd' are mutually exclusive, and there is no guarantee that the judgment on one applies to the other without transitivity issues. He then modifies the example to use 'every number is either even or odd' and 'every even is either old or new' (with no opposition between the parts), showing that no valid conclusion emerges except by the weaker condition of prevention of emptiness rather than both prevention of emptiness and combination. The point is that such syllogisms do not produce a necessary categorical conclusion universally.

Key Terms
الجزء المشترك غير تام (al-juz' al-mushtarak ghayr tamm): The common term between two premises in a compound disjunctive syllogism that is not fully specified or appears in different roles (subject/predicate) across the premises. [Note: No significant shift; a technical term in compound syllogisms.]
التعدية (al-ta'diya): Transitivity of a judgment from one term to another; here, it means the inference that a property of one disjunct applies to another disjunct. [Note: Stable in logical contexts as 'transitivity'.]
المعاندة (al-mu'anada): Mutual exclusivity or opposition between disjuncts, such that they cannot be simultaneously true or false in a contradictory manner. [Note: Consistent meaning in dialectical and logical contexts.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, under what condition might a compound disjunctive syllogism resembling the first figure yield a conclusion?
A) When the disjuncts are mutually exclusive and exhaustive.
B) When the common part is complete.
C) When the disjuncts are opposed by prevention of emptiness without prevention of combination.
D) When both premises are universal affirmative.

Key Takeaway
A compound disjunctive syllogism in the first-figure-like shape does not necessarily produce a valid conclusion; transitivity fails unless the disjuncts have a specific opposition of prevention of emptiness without prevention of combination.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the following compound disjunctive syllogism: 'Every act is either wajib or mubah. Every wajib is either fard 'ayn or fard kifayah. Therefore, every act is either mubah or fard 'ayn or fard kifayah.' Using the lesson's analysis, identify why this conclusion is not universally valid, and state what condition would be needed for the inference to hold.
?
Construct a compound disjunctive syllogism on a juristic topic where the disjuncts have the specific opposition of prevention of emptiness without prevention of combination. Explain whether a valid conclusion follows and of what type.

The Syllogism

Conditions for Valid Conjunctions in Disjunctive Syllogisms (Common Part as Predicate)
Conditions and first four conjunctions for disjunctive syllogisms with common part as predicate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three conditions for a valid conjunction in a compound disjunctive syllogism and determine whether a given pair of disjunctive premises meets those conditions.

Core Idea
Disjunctive syllogisms can yield categorical conclusions when premises meet three specific conditions.

In this passage, the author moves from simple disjunctive syllogisms to compound ones where the common part appears as predicate. He lays down three precise conditions that guarantee a necessary conclusion. Understanding these conditions is essential for constructing valid arguments in legal reasoning and theological debate, where disjunctive premises are common.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Common Part
Locate the term that appears in both disjunctive components of the premises; here it is the predicate in the disjunctive parts (e.g., 'C or D' in the example).
The common part is not one of the variables but the disjunction itself.
2
State the Three Conditions
Extract the three conditions: (1) the two premises must differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative); (2) one premise must be universal; (3) the disjunctive parts in the major premise must be universal (apply to all parts of the subject).
These conditions ensure the premises interact to yield a necessary conclusion.
3
Parse the First Conjunct Example
Examine the first conjunction: minor premise 'Always: either every B is C or D' (universal affirmative), major premise 'Always: it is not that either every A is C or D' (universal negative). Both disjunctive parts are universal. The conclusion is 'No B is A' (universal negative categorical).
The conclusion is categorical, not disjunctive.
4
Generalize the Pattern
Note that all valid conjunctions in this list produce a universal negative categorical conclusion when the conditions are met, regardless of the specific content of the disjunctive parts.
The author will continue listing all twelve conjunctions; each follows the same pattern.

الاقتران الأول من كليتين الصغرى موجبة والكبرى سالبة، وأجزاء الانفصال فيهما كلية، كقولنا: دائماً إما كل "ب" "ج" وإما "د") و(دائماً ليس إما كل "أ" "ج" وإما "د" ونتيجته كليّة حَمْلية سالبة وهي لا شيء من "ب" "أ") لما عرف في الاقترانات الحملية.

And if the common part is a predicate in the disjunctive parts, then its condition is that the two premises differ in quality, that one of the two premises be universal, and that the disjunctive parts in the major be universal, i.e., their relation[?] to all parts of its subject. Its productive conjunctions are twelve conjunctions. The first conjunction: from two universals, the minor affirmative and the major negative, and the disjunctive parts in both are universal, like our saying: 'Always: either every B is C or D' and 'Always: it is not that either every A is C or D', and its conclusion is a universal negative categorical, namely 'No B is A', as known in categorical conjunctions. The second conjunction: from a universal affirmative minor and a particular negative major, and the disjunctive parts in both are universal, like our saying: 'Always: either every B is C or D' and 'Not always: every A is C or D', and its conclusion is like the previous. The third conjunction: from two universals, the minor negative, and the disjunctive parts in both are universal, like our saying: 'Always: it is not that either every B is C or D' and 'Always: either every A is C or D', and its conclusion is like the previous. The fourth conjunction: from a universal negative minor and a particular affirmative major, and the disjunctive parts

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration of valid conjunctions (iqtiqanat) for compound disjunctive syllogisms where the common part (al-juz' al-mushtarak) serves as the predicate in both premises' disjunctive components. The author first states three conditions: (1) the two premises must differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative); (2) one premise must be universal; (3) the disjunctive parts in the major premise must be universal, meaning they apply to all parts of the subject. There are twelve productive conjunctions. The author then begins listing them: First conjunction: both premises universal, minor affirmative, major negative, disjunctive parts universal in both. Example: 'Always: either every B is C or D' and 'Always: it is not that either every A is C or D'. The conclusion is a universal negative categorical ('No B is A'). Second conjunction: minor universal affirmative, major particular negative. Conclusion same as first. Third conjunction: both universal, minor negative. Conclusion same. Fourth conjunction: minor universal negative, major particular affirmative (text cuts off). Each conjunction corresponds to a pattern from categorical syllogisms, but applied to disjunctive premises. The author uses the variables ب، ج، أ (B, C, A) as placeholders for terms. The examples show that the disjunction introduces complexity, but the resulting conclusion is categorical. The reader is expected to infer the remaining eight conjunctions by analogy.

Key Takeaway
The three conditions for a valid conjunction in a compound disjunctive syllogism with common part as predicate are: differing quality, at least one universal premise, and universal disjunctive parts in the major.

The Syllogism

Validity Conditions for First-Figure Disjunctive Syllogisms
Author explains validity of first-figure disjunctive syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate whether a first-figure disjunctive syllogism yields a valid conclusion by checking the mutual exclusivity of its disjuncts.

Core Idea
Not every first-figure disjunctive syllogism is valid; disjuncts must be exclusive.

In conjunctive syllogisms where both premises are disjunctive, the shared part can be the subject, placing the syllogism in the first figure. However, a common claim that any such syllogism with universal affirmative premises yields a real conclusion is false. The author demonstrates this using the example of numbers and parity. To understand why, we must analyze the relationship between the disjuncts—whether they are mutually exclusive or not. This lesson guides you through that analysis and helps you identify the condition for validity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the shared part and figure
In the author's example, the shared part (al-juz' al-mushtarak) is 'even' (زوج). It appears as a disjunct in the first premise ('every number is either even or odd') and as the subject of the second premise ('every even is either even-even, even-odd, or even-even-odd'). Since the shared part is the subject of the second premise, the syllogism has the form of the first figure.
2
Extract the two disjunctive premises
Premise 1: 'Every number is either even or odd.' (دائماً كل عدد إما زوج وإما فرد) Premise 2: 'Every even number is either even-even, even-odd, or even-even-odd together.' (دائماً كل زوج إما زوج الزوج أو زوج الفرد أو زوج الزوج والفرد معاً) The claimed conclusion is that the number (subject) is one of the subcategories of even, but the author critiques this.
3
Check mutual exclusivity of disjuncts in Premise 1
The disjuncts 'even' and 'odd' are mutually exclusive (متباينان) – a number cannot be both. This exclusivity prevents a judgment that applies to one disjunct from automatically transferring to the other. The author states: 'لا يلزم من الحكم على أحدهما بحكم أن يكون حكماً على الآخر' (it is not necessary that a judgment upon one of them becomes a judgment upon the other).
4
Analyze the transitivity (ta'diyya) condition
The author introduces the concept of transitivity (التعدية). If a judgment applies to both disjuncts universally, then transitivity holds; in this example, the second premise's subcategories apply only to 'even', not to 'odd'. Therefore, the conclusion that 'every number is either even-even, etc.' does not follow uniformly for all numbers, because odd numbers are excluded from the second premise's scope.
5
Test with an altered example to see the condition
The author changes the second premise to: 'Every even is either old or originated' (وكل زوج فإما قديم وما حادث). Here 'old' and 'originated' are not mutually exclusive (لا معاندة بينه وبين الجزء الآخر). In this case, the syllogism fails to produce a valid conclusion. The only valid form, according to the author, is when the disjunction prevents emptiness (at least one true) but does not prevent combination (both can be true) – i.e., an inclusive disjunction. This shows that the type of disjunction (exclusive vs. inclusive) critically affects validity.
The exact phrasing 'بالمنع من الخلو دون الجمع' indicates a disjunction that is exclusive only in the sense of preventing emptiness (مانعة الخلو) but not preventing combination (مانعة الجمع). This is the inclusive 'or'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تتمة الفن الأول: في تعريف صور القياسات الاقترانية

الانفصال فيهما، أو أنه موضوع لأجزاء الانفصال فيهما، فإن كان الأول فهو على هيئة الشكل الأول، وقد قيل فيه أن القريب من الطبع منه ما كان من كليتين موجبتين من الاقتران الأول منه كقولنا: (دائماً كل عدد إما زوج وإما فرد) و(دائماً كل زوج إما زوج الزوج أو زوج الفرد أو زوج الزوج والفرد معاً) وليس بحق إن قيل أن النتيجة حقيقية، فإن الزوج والفرد متباينان ولا يلزم من الحكم على أحدهما بحكم أن يكون حكماً على الآخر؛ لجواز أن يعمها فتلزم التعدية، أو يخص أحدهما فلا تلزم التعدية، فلا اطراد ولهذا فإنا لو غيرنا المادة في المثال المذكور بقولنا: (وكل زوج فإما قديم وما حادث وبما لا معاندة بينه وبين الجزء الآخر، فإنه لا ينتج العدد إما فرد قديم وإما حادث) إذ لا عناد بين هذه الأجزاء، إلا بالمنع من الخلو دون الجمع، وعلى هذا فلا يخفى الحكم في باقي اقتراناته.
Continuation of the first art: On defining the forms of conjunctive syllogisms. The disjunction in both [premises], or that [the shared part] is the subject of the parts of the disjunction in them. If it is the first, then it is in the form of the first figure, and it has been said therein that the one close to nature among them is that which consists of two universal affirmative [premises] from the first conjunction thereof, like our saying: 'Always every number is either even or odd' and 'Always every even [number] is either even-even or even-odd or even-even-and-odd together'. But it is not correct if it is said that the result is real, for the even and the odd are mutually exclusive, and it does not follow from the judgment upon one of them by a judgment that it be a judgment upon the other; because it is possible that [the judgment] applies to both, so transitivity follows, or it applies to one of them, so transitivity does not follow. Thus there is no uniformity. And for this reason, if we change the matter in the aforementioned example by our saying: 'And every even [number] is either old or originated, and with no opposition between it and the other part, then it does not produce: number is either old odd or originated odd', since there is no opposition between these parts, except by prevention of emptiness without combination. Upon this, the ruling in the rest of its conjunctions is not hidden.
Source pages p. 298

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of a specific type of conjunctive syllogism (qiyas iqtirani) where the premises are disjunctive (munfasil). The author first sets up the context: the shared part—the term common to both premises—is either the subject or the predicate of the disjunctive parts. Here the shared part is the subject. This makes the syllogism belong to the first figure. The author then critiques a claim that the most natural example of such a syllogism is one with two universal affirmative premises, such as: 'Every number is either even or odd; every even number is either even-even, even-odd, or even-even-odd.' The claim that the conclusion of this syllogism is 'real' (haqiqiyya) is incorrect because the terms 'even' and 'odd' are mutually exclusive. A judgment that applies to one does not necessarily apply to the other; it could apply to both (requiring transitivity) or to only one (failing transitivity), so there is no uniform rule. To illustrate, the author alters the example to show that if the disjuncts are not mutually exclusive (e.g., 'old' and 'originated' are not opposed in the sense of being simultaneously false; they can both be true or both false? Actually 'old' and 'originated' in Islamic theology: the world is originated, so they are not mutually exclusive? The text says 'with no opposition between it and the other part' meaning the two parts are not contradictory, so the syllogism fails. The only valid form is when the disjunction is exclusive in the sense of preventing emptiness but not combination (mani'at al-khuluw la al-jam'). The author concludes that based on this, the ruling for the remaining conjunctions is clear.

Key Terms
الجزء المشترك
الانفصال
التعدية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, why does the syllogism 'Every number is either even or odd; every even is either even-even or even-odd' not produce a 'real' conclusion?
A) Because the major premise is particular.
B) Because 'even' and 'odd' are mutually exclusive.
C) Because the shared part is not distributed.
D) Because the conclusion would be a disjunction, not a categorical statement.

Key Takeaway
The validity of a first-figure disjunctive syllogism depends on whether the disjuncts in the first premise are mutually exclusive; inclusive disjunctions are required for a valid conclusion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Then evaluate whether the conclusion 'Every action is either wajib or recommended or permissible' is valid by checking the mutual exclusivity of wajib and mubah.

Conditions and Enumeration of Productive Conjunctions
Conditions and enumeration of productive conjunctions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three conditions for a productive conjunction in the first figure when the shared part is a predicate, and identify the total number of such conjunctions.

Core Idea
A valid disjunctive syllogism in the first figure requires differing quality, one universality, and major's disjuncts universal.

In this passage from Daqaiq al-Haqaiq, the author lays out the precise conditions that make a disjunctive syllogism productive in the first figure when the shared term is a predicate. Understanding these conditions is essential for recognizing valid argument forms in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the shared part's role
The passage begins by specifying that the shared part (al-juz' al-mushtarak) is the predicate in the disjunctive parts. This sets the context for the figure.
2
Extract the three conditions
From the first sentence, list: (i) the two premises must differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative), (ii) at least one premise must be universal, (iii) the disjunctive parts in the major premise must be universal (i.e., apply to all subjects).
The major premise is the one containing the shared part as predicate.
3
Note the total number of conjunctions
The author states there are twelve productive conjunctions (qarā'in muntija) under these conditions.
4
Distinguish the two sets of six
The first six have disjunctive parts universal in both premises. The second six have disjunctive parts universal only in the major, and particular in the minor, yielding a particular negative conclusion.
The second set is only briefly mentioned; the author says 'so consider it'.

Source Text

وأما إن كان الجزء المشترك محمولاً في أجزاء الانفصال فشرطه اختلاف المقدمتين في الكيفية، وأن تكون إحدى المقدمتين كليّة، وأن تكون أجزاء الانفصال في الكبرى كليته أي نسبتها إلى جميع أجزاء موضوعها، وقرائنه المنتجة اثنا عشر اقتراناً
الاقتران الأول من كليتين الصغرى موجبة والكبرى سالبة، وأجزاء الانفصال فيهما كلية، كقولنا: (دائماً إما كل "ب" "ج" وإما "د") و(دائماً ليس إما كل "أ" "ج" وإما "د") ونتيجته كليّة حَمْلية سالبة وهي (لا شيء من "ب" "أ") لما عرف في الاقترانات الحملية.
الاقتران الثاني : من كلية صغرى موجبة وجزئية سالبة كبرى وأجزاء الانفصال فيهما كليّة، كقولنا: (دائماً إما كل "ب" "ج" وإما "د") و(ليس دائماً كل "أ" "ج" وإما د) ونتيجته كالتي قبله.
الاقتران الثالث من كليتين والصغرى سالبة، وأجزاء الانفصال فيهما كلية، كقولنا: (دائماً ليس إما كل "ب" "ج" وإما "د") و(دائماً إما كل "أ" "ج" وإما "د") ونتيجته كالتي قبله.
الاقتران الرابع من كلية سالبة صغرى وجزئية موجبة كبرى، وأجزاء الانفصال فيهما كليّة، كقولنا: (دائماً ليس إما كل "ب" "ج" وإما "د") و(قد يكون إما كل "ج" وإما "د") ونتيجته كالتي قبله.
الاقتران الخامس من جزئية صغرى موجبة، وكلية كبرى سالبة، وأجزاء الانفصال فيهما موجبة، كقولنا: (قد يكون إما كل "ب" "ج" وإما "د") و(دائماً ليس إما كل "أ" "ج" وإما "د") ونتيجته كالتي قبله.
الاقتران السادس من جزئية صغرى سالبة، وكليّة كبرى موجبة، وأجزاء الانفصال فيهما كليّة، كقولنا: (ليس دائماً إما كل "ب" "ج" وإما "د") و(دائماً إما كل "أ" "ج" وإما "د") ونتيجته كالتي قبله.
وإن كانت أجزاء الانفصال في الصغرى جزئية فقد يتهيأ منها ستة اقترانات أخرى موازية لهذه الستة، إلا أن النتيجة فيها جزئية سالبة حَمْلية، فاعتبرها.
And if the shared part is a predicate in the parts of the disjunction, then its condition is: the two premises differ in quality, one of the two premises must be universal, and the parts of the disjunction in the major must be universal, i.e., their relation to all parts of their subject. Its productive conjunctions are twelve. The first conjunction: from two universals, the minor affirmative and the major negative, and the parts of the disjunction in both are universal, like our saying: 'Always either every B is C or D' and 'Always not either every A is C or D', and its result is a universal categorical negative, namely 'No A is B', as known in categorical conjunctions. The second conjunction: from a universal affirmative minor and a particular negative major, and the parts of the disjunction in both are universal, like our saying: 'Always either every B is C or D' and 'Not always every A is C or D', and its result is like the previous. The third conjunction: from two universals, the minor negative, and the parts of the disjunction in both are universal, like our saying: 'Always not either every B is C or D' and 'Always either every A is C or D', and its result is like the previous. The fourth conjunction: from a universal negative minor and a particular affirmative major, and the parts of the disjunction in both are universal, like our saying: 'Always not either every B is C or D' and 'It may be that either every J is C or D', and its result is like the previous. The fifth conjunction: from a particular affirmative minor and a universal negative major, and the parts of the disjunction in both are affirmative [؟], like our saying: 'It may be that either every B is C or D' and 'Always not either every A is C or D', and its result is like the previous. The sixth conjunction: from a particular negative minor and a universal affirmative major, and the parts of the disjunction in both are universal, like our saying: 'Not always either every B is C or D' and 'Always either every A is C or D', and its result is like the previous. And if the parts of the disjunction in the minor are particular, then from them another six conjunctions parallel to these six may be prepared, except that the result in them is a particular categorical negative. So consider it.
Source pages p. 298

Commentary
This passage defines and enumerates the productive conjunctions (قرائن منتجة) for the first figure when the shared term (al-juz' al-mushtarak) serves as the predicate of the disjunctive parts. The author states three conditions: (1) the two premises must differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative), (2) at least one premise must be universal, and (3) the disjunctive parts in the major premise must be universal (i.e., the disjuncts apply to all individuals of the subject). Under these conditions, twelve conjunctions produce valid conclusions. The first six are listed with the disjunctive parts universal in both premises. Each conjunction specifies the quantity and quality of the minor and major premises, and yields a categorical negative conclusion (universal for the first six, particular for the second set). For instance, the first conjunction: minor universal affirmative, major universal negative, both disjunctive parts universal, gives 'No A is B'. The author uses variables (ب, ج, أ, د) to stand for terms. After listing these six, he notes that if the disjunctive parts in the minor premise are particular, six parallel conjunctions exist, resulting in a particular negative. He concludes with 'so consider it' (فاعتبرها), leaving the detailed application to the reader.

The First Six Productive Conjunctions with Universal Disjunctive Parts
Enumeration and structure of first six conjunctions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct any of the first six conjunctions and verify that each yields a universal categorical negative conclusion.

Core Idea
Each of the first six conjunctions differs in quantity/quality of premises but always produces a universal negative categorical conclusion.

After stating the conditions, the author lists six specific conjunctions, each combining a minor and major premise of specific quantity and quality. This lesson walks you through each to see the pattern and confirm the result.

Guided Path
1
Understand the form of a conjunction
Each conjunction is defined by the minor premise (first mentioned) and major premise, both with disjunctive parts universal. The result is always a universal categorical negative: 'No A is B'.
The variables: minor subject B, major subject A, shared term C and D are the disjuncts.
2
Analyze the first conjunction
Minor: universal affirmative (كل ب ج أو د), Major: universal negative (ليس كل أ ج أو د). Conclusion: No A is B. Check that premises differ in quality and one is universal.
3
Analyze the second conjunction
Minor: universal affirmative, Major: particular negative. Conclusion same as first.
Major is 'not always' (ليس دائماً).
4
Analyze the remaining four
Third: minor universal negative, major universal affirmative. Fourth: minor universal negative, major particular affirmative. Fifth: minor particular affirmative, major universal negative. Sixth: minor particular negative, major universal affirmative. All yield the same universal negative conclusion.
Note the pattern: one premise affirmative one negative, at least one universal.
5
Confirm the conclusion using categorical rules
The result is a universal categorical negative. This is justified by the rules of categorical syllogisms mentioned in 'as known in categorical conjunctions'.
The author relies on prior knowledge of categorical syllogisms.

Third Figure Disjunctive Syllogisms: First Three Conjunctions
Author begins enumeration of productive conjunctions for third figure disjunctive syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the conditions for a productive third-figure disjunctive syllogism and identify the first three conjunctions.

Core Idea
Third figure disjunctive syllogisms use a shared subject term and yield conclusions with a particular subject.

We now examine the third figure of disjunctive syllogisms, where the shared term is the subject of the disjunctive parts in both premises. The author states three conditions for production and then begins enumerating the eighteen productive conjunctions. We will parse the first three conjunctions in detail, noting how the quantity and quality of premises determine the conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the shared term's position
In the third figure, the shared term (al-juz' al-mushtarak) is the subject of the disjunctive parts in both premises. In the examples, 'B' is the shared term.
2
State the three conditions for production
Condition 1: At least one premise must be universal. Condition 2: The minor premise must be affirmative. Condition 3: The disjunctive parts must be universal in at least one premise (either minor or major).
These conditions ensure the conclusion follows.
3
Analyze the first conjunction
Both premises universal affirmative, disjunctive parts universal. Conclusion: universal affirmative disjunctive with a particular subject. Example: 'Always either every B is C or D' and 'Always either every B is A or D' gives 'Always either some C or some D is either A or D'.
Though universal, the conclusion's subject is particular (some C or some D).
4
Analyze the second conjunction
Minor universal affirmative, major universal negative, disjunctive parts universal. Conclusion: universal negative disjunctive with a particular subject. Example: 'Always either every B is C or D' and 'Always not either every B is A or D' gives 'Always not either some C or some D is either A or D'.
The negation in the major premise affects the quality.
5
Analyze the third conjunction
Minor universal affirmative, major particular affirmative, disjunctive parts universal. Conclusion: particular affirmative disjunctive with a particular subject. Example: 'Always either every B is C or D' and 'It may be that either every B is A or D' yields a particular conclusion, though the complete result is not given in the text.
The minor is universal, major particular, so conclusion is particular.

And if the shared part is a subject of the parts of the disjunction in both, then it is in the form of the third figure. Its condition for production is: universality of one of its two premises, affirmation of the minor, and universality of the parts of the disjunction in one of them, whichever it is. Its productive conjunctions are eighteen. The first conjunction: from two universals affirmative, and the parts of the disjunction in both are universal, like our saying: 'Always either every B is C or D' and 'Always either every B is A or D', and its result is a universal affirmative disjunctive, whose subject of the parts of the disjunction is particular: that is 'Always either some C or some D [is] either A or D'. The second conjunction: from a universal affirmative minor and a universal negative major, and the parts of the disjunction in both are universal, like our saying: 'Always either every B is C or D' and 'Always not either every B is A or D', and its result is a universal negative disjunctive, whose subject of the parts of the disjunction is particular: that is 'Always not either some C or some D [is] either A or D'. Commentary
This passage moves to the third figure of disjunctive syllogisms, where the shared term (al-juz' al-mushtarak) is the subject of the disjunctive parts in both premises. The author gives three conditions for production: (1) at least one premise must be universal, (2) the minor premise must be affirmative, and (3) the disjunctive parts must be universal in at least one premise (either minor or major). Under these, there are eighteen productive conjunctions. The author then begins enumerating them. The first conjunction: both premises universal affirmative, disjunctive parts universal, yields a universal affirmative disjunctive conclusion with a particular subject (some C or some D). Example: 'Always either every B is C or D; Always either every B is A or D' gives 'Always either some C or some D is either A or D'. The second conjunction: minor universal affirmative, major universal negative, yields a universal negative disjunctive conclusion. The third conjunction: minor universal affirmative, major particular affirmative, yields a particular affirmative disjunctive. The enumeration cuts off at the third conjunction due to the end of the page; the remaining fifteen conjunctions are not provided in this window.

The Syllogism

Conditions and Conjunctions of the Third Figure of Disjunctive Syllogisms
Author enumerates the 18 productive conjunctions for the third figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to verify the validity conditions of a third‑figure disjunctive syllogism and construct the conclusion for any of the 18 productive conjunctions.

Core Idea
Third‑figure disjunctive syllogisms require a universal premise, an affirmative minor, and universal disjunctive parts.

This passage enumerates the 18 valid conjunctions (moods) of the third figure of disjunctive syllogisms, where the common term (middle) is the subject of the disjuncts in both premises. The author lays down three conditions for a productive syllogism and then illustrates the first four conjunctions in detail. Understanding these conditions and the pattern of conclusion formation is essential for constructing and evaluating complex disjunctive arguments in Islamic logic, particularly in legal and theological debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the common term and its position
In the third figure, the common part (الجزء المشترك) is the subject of the disjunctive parts in both premises. Check that the same term (e.g., 'B') is subject of both disjunctions.
2
Verify the three conditions for production
The passage gives three conditions: 1) One premise must be universal (kulliyya), 2) The minor premise must be affirmative (ijab al-sughra), 3) The parts of the disjunction (ajza' al-infisal) must be universal in at least one premise. Ensure these hold.
3
Classify the premises by quantity and quality
Determine whether each premise is universal or particular, affirmative or negative. For example, in the first conjunction both are universal affirmative.
4
Formulate the conclusion
The conclusion is a disjunctive proposition where the subject parts are particularized. For a universal affirmative minor and universal affirmative major, the conclusion is universal affirmative with particular subject parts: 'Always, either some C or some D is A or D.' Practice adjusting for changes in quality and quantity.
The remaining conjunctions follow the same pattern with variations in the modality of the premises.

Source Text

وأما إن كان الجزء المشترك موضوعاً لأجزاء الانفصال فيهما فهو على هيئة الشكل الثالث فشرط إنتاجه كليّة إحدى مقدمتيه، وإيجاب الصغرى، وكلية أجزاء الانفصال في إحداهما أيهما كانت، وقرائنه المنتجة ثمانية عشر اقتراناً:

And as for when the common part is a subject for the parts of the disjunction in both, then it is in the form of the third figure. Its condition for production is that one of its two premises is universal, the minor premise affirmative, and the parts of the disjunction universal in one of them whichever it may be. Its productive conjunctions are eighteen conjunctions:
The first conjunction: from two universal affirmative premises, and the parts of the disjunction in both are universal, like our saying: (Always every B is either C or D) and (Always every B is either A or D), and its conclusion is a universal affirmative disjunctive proposition whose subject parts of the disjunction are particular, namely: Always either some C or some D is A or D.
The second conjunction: from a universal affirmative minor and a universal negative major, and the parts of the disjunction in both are universal, like our saying: (Always every B is either C or D) and (Always not every B is either A or D), and its conclusion is a universal negative disjunctive proposition whose subject parts of the disjunction are particular, namely: Always not either some C or some D is A or D.
The third conjunction: from a universal affirmative minor and a particular affirmative major, and the parts of the disjunction in both are universal, like our saying: (Always every B is either C or D) and (Sometimes every B is either A or D), and its conclusion is a particular affirmative disjunctive proposition whose subject parts of the disjunction are particular, namely: Sometimes either some C or some D is A or R.
The fourth conjunction: from a universal affirmative minor and a particular negative major, and the parts of the disjunction in both are universal, like our saying: (Always every B is either C or D) and (Not always every B is either A or R), and its conclusion is a particular negative disjunctive proposition, whose subject parts are particular, namely: Not always either some C or some D is A or R. And four others may be composed on this pattern, with the parts of the disjunction in the major being particular, and four others with the parts of the disjunction in the minor being particular without the major, and six other conjunctions parallel to these conjunctions, with the minor premise particular affirmative. So take them into consideration. And the judgment regarding the modality of the conclusion and its absoluteness in pure and mixed conjunctions, and following them in the premises of its syllogism, follows what was previously mentioned in categorical conjunctions in all these figures.

Commentary
This passage defines the productive conjunctions for the third figure of disjunctive syllogisms where the common term (the middle term) is the subject of the disjunctive parts in both premises. The author first states the condition for a valid syllogism in this figure: one premise must be universal, the minor premise must be affirmative, and the parts of the disjunction must be universal in at least one of the premises. Then he lists the eighteen possible conjunctions, explicitly detailing the first four. The first conjunction combines two universal affirmative premises, both with universal disjunctive parts. The conclusion is a universal affirmative disjunctive with particular subject parts, e.g., "Always, either some C or some D is A or D." The second conjunction has a universal affirmative minor and a universal negative major, yielding a universal negative conclusion. The third has a universal affirmative minor and a particular affirmative major, giving a particular affirmative conclusion. The fourth has a universal affirmative minor and a particular negative major, producing a particular negative conclusion. The author then notes that four more conjunctions arise when the major premise has particular disjunctive parts, and another four when the minor has particular disjunctive parts, and six more when the minor premise is particular affirmative, totaling eighteen. He concludes by reminding that the modality (e.g., necessary, absolute) of the conclusion and its determination in pure or mixed conjunctions follows the rules established for categorical syllogisms. This passage demonstrates the systematic enumeration of valid forms in disjunctive syllogisms, a key topic in Islamic logic for analyzing complex arguments.

The Fourth Conjunction of Disjunctive Syllogisms
Author describes the fourth conjunctive mood of disjunctive syllogisms and enumerates variant forms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the fourth conjunction of a disjunctive syllogism by parsing its minor and major premise types and constructing its conclusion pattern.

Core Idea
The fourth conjunction uses a universal affirmative minor and particular negative major to produce a particular negative disjunctive conclusion.

In the study of disjunctive syllogisms, each mood has specific premise combinations. The fourth conjunction is one of many moods that arise when both premises are disjunctive propositions with universal or particular quantification. This lesson breaks down the author's description so you can recognize the pattern and apply it in Islamic logical analysis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the minor premise
Find the minor premise in the description: it is a universal affirmative disjunctive proposition. The text says 'من موجبة كلية صغرى' (universal affirmative minor). In the example, it is 'دائماً إما كل ب ج وإما د' (Always either every B is J or D). Note that the disjunction parts (أجزاء الانفصال) are universal.
Universal affirmative means the proposition holds for all instances of the subject.
2
Identify the major premise
The major premise is a particular negative disjunctive proposition: 'وجزئية سالبة كبرى' (particular negative major). The example is 'ليس دائماً إما كل ب أ وإما ر' (Not always either every B is A or R). The disjunction parts are also universal here.
Particular negative means the proposition is denied for some instances.
3
Derive the conclusion pattern
The conclusion is a particular negative disjunctive proposition. The text gives: 'ليس دائماً إما بعض ج وإما بعض د إما أ وإما ر' (Not always either some J and some D, either A or R). Notice the subject parts become particular (some J, some D) and the predicates are A and R.
The conclusion mirrors the structure of the premises but with particularized subject parts.
4
Recognize the variant forms
The author enumerates variants: four more conjunctions where the disjunction parts in the major are particular; four where the disjunction parts in the minor are particular; and six parallel conjunctions with a particular affirmative minor. Study how changing the quantification of disjuncts alters the mood.
Consider these variants as exercises in pattern recognition.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Supplement to the first art: On defining the forms of conjunctive syllogisms. Particular, and it is (it may be either some J and some D, either A or R). The fourth conjunction: from a universal affirmative minor premise and a particular negative major premise, and the parts of the disjunction in it are universal, as in our saying: (Always either every B is J or D) and (Not always either every B is A or R), and its conclusion is a particular negative disjunctive; the parts of the subject in it are particular, namely: Not always either some J and some D, either A or R. Commentary
This passage is a classification of disjunctive syllogism moods, specifically focusing on the fourth conjunction. The author begins by defining a particular disjunctive proposition (جزئي وهي) which has two possible forms: either some J and some D, or A or R. Then he describes the fourth conjunction: it consists of a universal affirmative minor premise and a particular negative major premise, and the disjunctive parts (أجزاء الانفصال) in it are universal. An example is given using variables: 'Always either every B is J or D' (the universal affirmative minor) and 'Not always either every B is A or R' (the particular negative major). The conclusion is a particular negative disjunctive proposition where the subject parts are particular: 'Not always either some J and some D, either A or R'. The author then enumerates variant forms: four other conjunctions on this pattern with the disjunction parts in the major being particular; four others with the disjunction parts in the minor being particular (and the major universal); and six other parallel conjunctions where the minor is particular affirmative. He instructs the reader to consider them. Finally, he notes that the rule for the direction (جهة) and absoluteness (إطلاق) of the conclusion in pure and mixed contexts follows the same principles as in categorical syllogisms across all figures. This passage is dense and requires careful parsing of the technical terms for the disjunctive moods.

Key Terms
الاقتران الرابع (al-iqtirān al-rābi‘): The fourth conjunctive mood of disjunctive syllogisms, characterized by a universal affirmative minor and a particular negative major with universal disjunction parts.
أجزاء الانفصال (ajzā’ al-infiṣāl): The parts that make up the disjunction (i.e., the disjuncts) in a disjunctive proposition, which can be universal or particular.
القرائن الصرفة والمختلطة (al-qarā’in al-ṣirfa wa-l-mukhtaliṭa): Pure and mixed contexts referring to the nature of the premises (all categorical vs. mixed categorical and conditional) in determining the modality of the conclusion.

Mixed First-Figure Syllogisms: Categorical Major and Conditional Minor
Rules for mixed first-figure syllogisms with categorical major

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify valid mixed syllogisms in the first figure where the categorical is the major premise and the conditional is affirmative and shares its consequent, and verify the condition that the predicate in the consequent relates affirmatively to its subject.

Core Idea
A mixed first-figure syllogism requires not just an affirmative conditional, but an affirmative relation within the consequent.

This passage from Daqaiq al-Haqaiq explains how to combine a categorical major premise with a conditional minor premise in the first figure. The author expands on the conditions necessary for validity, which go beyond the purely categorical case. We will walk through the key rules and the example provided, noting the crucial condition about the consequent's internal relation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises
Determine which premise is categorical (major) and which is conditional (minor). In the first figure, the categorical must be the major premise. The conditional minor can be affirmative or negative; here we consider affirmative.
2
Locate the sharing term
Look at the consequent of the conditional and the categorical. The middle term must be the predicate of the consequent and the subject of the categorical. This establishes the first figure.
The author lists four possibilities for the middle term configuration; only one matches the first figure.
3
Check the affirmative condition
Even if the conditional is affirmative, it is not sufficient. The relation of the predicate in the consequent to its subject must be affirmative. If the predicate is negated of the subject in the consequent, the syllogism fails.
The author states: 'لابد من أن تكون نسبة المحمول في التالي إلى موضوعه إيجابية'
4
Determine the conclusion form
The conclusion is always a conditional (متصل) not a categorical. Its antecedent is the antecedent of the original conditional; its consequent is the result of combining the original consequent with the categorical via inference.
In the example: from 'if every B is J then every H is R' and 'every R is D', we conclude 'if every B is J then every H is D'.

Chapter Three: On conjunctions formed from categoricals and conditionals. And the conjunctions formed from a conditional and categoricals are either such that the categorical is the major or minor. If it is the major, then the conditional is either affirmative or negative. If it is affirmative, then the sharing (common term) is either between the categorical and the consequent of the conditional, or between the categorical and the antecedent of the conditional. If the sharing is between the categorical and the consequent of the conditional, then the configuration of the middle term between them does not exceed the four figures; because it is either the predicate of the consequent and the subject of the categorical, or the predicate of the consequent and the categorical, or the subject of the consequent and the categorical, or the subject of the consequent and the predicate of the categorical. If the configuration of the middle term between them is such that it is the predicate of the consequent and the subject of the categorical, then it is the first figure. Its properties and conditions for production, and the quantity of its conjunctions, follow what preceded regarding the first figure among categoricals, except that its conclusion is only a conditional conjunctive (conditional statement). And since the condition for production in this figure among categoricals is the affirmation of its minor, it is not sufficient here to affirm the conditional; rather, it is necessary that the relation of the predicate in the consequent to its subject be affirmative, because from judging it negatively about the subject of the consequent of the conditional by something, it does not follow that it is judged about the subject or not, even if the conditional is affirmative, for what preceded. And at that, what is judged in the categorical may not be necessary for the antecedent of the conditional. And at this, the conditional is either universal or particular. In both cases, the four conjunctions of the first figure are obtained from it, because the consideration in that is only of the consequent of the conditional and the categorical, not of the conditional and the categorical. As for when the conditional is universal, the example of the first conjunction, in which the consequent and the categorical are both universal affirmative, is our saying: (Every B is J and every H is R) and (Every R is D). Its conclusion is a universal affirmative conditional, its antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is a universal affirmative which is the result of the conjunction of the consequent with the categorical if they were alone, namely (every
Source pages p. 301

Commentary
This passage opens Chapter Three on mixed syllogisms composed of categorical and conditional premises. The author first divides such syllogisms based on whether the categorical premise is the major or minor. Here, he treats the case where the categorical is major. Then the conditional major can be affirmative or negative. If affirmative, the shared term (the middle term) can be between the categorical and either the consequent or the antecedent of the conditional. When the sharing is with the consequent, the configuration of the middle term falls into one of the four figures. The first figure occurs when the middle term is the predicate of the consequent and the subject of the categorical. The properties, conditions for validity, and quantity of conjunctions follow those of the categorical first figure, with one difference: the conclusion is always a conditional (متصل) rather than a categorical. The author then specifies a crucial condition: in the categorical first figure, the minor must be affirmative. In this mixed case, it is not enough for the conditional to be affirmative; rather, the relation of the predicate in the consequent to its subject must be affirmative. This is because if the consequent's predicate is negated of its subject, it does not follow that the categorical's judgment applies to the antecedent. Thus, even if the conditional is affirmative, the categorical's judgment may not be necessary for the antecedent. The author notes that the conditional can be universal or particular, and in both cases the four conjunctions of the first figure can be formed. The consideration is based on the consequent and the categorical, not the whole conditional and the categorical. He then gives an example of the first conjunction when the conditional is universal affirmative: 'Every B is J and every H is R' and 'Every R is D' (note: the antecedent of the conditional is presumably 'Every B is J' and the consequent is 'Every H is R'? The example seems to have a conditional missing; likely the conditional is 'if every B is J then every H is R' or similar). The conclusion is a universal affirmative conditional whose antecedent is the antecedent of the original conditional and whose consequent is the result of combining the consequent with the categorical (here, 'Every R is D' with the consequent producing 'Every H is D'). The text cuts off before completing the example. This passage requires careful attention to the conditions for validity in mixed syllogisms.

Key Terms
الشرطية المتصلة
الحد الأوسط
تالي الشرطية

Key Takeaway
A mixed first-figure syllogism requires that the predicate in the consequent relates affirmatively to its subject, not merely that the conditional itself is affirmative.

First-Figure Compound Syllogisms: Categorical Major with Conditional Minor
Author explains compound syllogisms with categorical major and conditional minor, first figure conditions and examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the middle term in a compound syllogism with a categorical major and conditional minor, and verify the conditions for a valid first-figure inference.

Core Idea
A compound syllogism's validity depends on the middle term's position and the affirmative relation in the conditional's consequent.

In the previous lessons, we studied categorical syllogisms and their figures. Now, we extend that to compound syllogisms where one premise is categorical and the other is a conditional (if...then...). The author explains how to combine them by focusing on the common term between the categorical and the consequent of the conditional. The first figure is the most straightforward: the middle term is the predicate of the consequent and the subject of the categorical. However, there is a crucial additional condition: the predicate in the consequent must be affirmatively related to its subject, or the inference fails. Let's work through the logic step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the common term between the categorical and the conditional's consequent
Look for a term that appears in both the categorical premise and the consequent clause of the conditional premise. This term will serve as the middle term (al-hadd al-awsat). In the author's example, the consequent is 'kull H R' and the categorical is 'kull R D', so the common term is 'R'.
If the common term is with the antecedent, the figures are different; here we focus on the case where it is with the consequent.
2
Determine the position of the common term in each premise
For the first figure, the common term must be the predicate of the consequent and the subject of the categorical. In our example, 'R' is the predicate of 'kull H R' and the subject of 'kull R D'. That confirms it is first figure. The other positions (predicate-predicate, subject-subject, subject-predicate) correspond to second, third, and fourth figures.
The author states that the four figures are the same as in categorical syllogisms, but the conclusion is always a conditional.
3
Check the affirmative condition on the predicate in the consequent
Even if the conditional premise itself is affirmative, the relation between the predicate and its subject in the consequent must be affirmative. That is, the consequent clause must be a positive statement (affirmative). In the example, 'kull H R' is affirmative, so the condition is met. If the consequent were negative (e.g., 'no H is R'), the inference would not produce a valid conclusion, because a negative judgment about the consequent does not necessarily transfer to the antecedent.
This is a special requirement for mixed syllogisms that does not appear in pure categorical syllogisms.
4
Form the conditional conclusion
The conclusion is a conditional proposition whose antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional premise, and whose consequent is the result of combining the categorical with the consequent using the rules of the categorical syllogism. In our example, inner categorical syllogism: 'kull H R' + 'kull R D' gives 'kull H D'. So the conclusion is: 'If B is J, then every H is D' (i.e., 'kull ma B J fa kull H D').
The conclusion is always a conditional, and its consequent follows from the inner categorical syllogism.

Chapter Three: On compound syllogisms consisting of categoricals and conditionals. The compound syllogisms consisting of a conditional and a categorical may have the categorical as major or minor. If it is major, then the conditional is either affirmative or negative. If it is affirmative, then the common term is either between the categorical and the consequent of the conditional, or between the categorical and the antecedent of the conditional. If the common term is between the categorical and the consequent, then the form of the middle term between them does not go beyond the four figures; because it is either (1) the predicate of the consequent and the subject of the categorical, or (2) the predicate of the consequent and the predicate of the categorical, or (3) the subject of the consequent and the subject of the categorical, or (4) the subject of the consequent and the predicate of the categorical. If the form of the middle term is that it is the predicate of the consequent and the subject of the categorical, then it is the first figure, and its specific properties, conditions for production, and the quantity of its syllogisms are as previously stated for the first figure of categoricals, except that its conclusion is only a conditional conjunctive. And since the condition for production in this figure in categoricals is the affirmation of its minor, it is not sufficient here to affirm the conditional; rather it is necessary that the relation of the predicate in the consequent to its subject be affirmative, because the judgment of it as negative concerning the subject of the consequent of the conditional by something does not necessitate that it be judged about the subject or not even if the conditional is affirmative, as previously. And in that case, what is judged in the categorical may not be necessary for the antecedent of the conditional. And with this, the conjunctive conditional is either universal or particular. In both cases, the four syllogisms of the first figure are obtained from it, because the consideration in that is only the consequent of the conditional and the categorical, not the conditional and the categorical. As for when the conditional is universal: An example of the first syllogism, in which the consequent and the categorical are both universal affirmatives: our saying: 'Every (ب) is (ج) and every (هـ) is (ر)' and 'Every (ر) is (د)' and its conclusion is a universal affirmative conditional, its antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is a universal affirmative which is the result of combining the consequent with the categorical if they were alone: 'Every (ب) is (ج) so every (هـ) is (د).' An example of the second syllogism, in which the consequent is a universal affirmative and the categorical is a universal negative: our saying: 'Every (ب) is (ج) so every (هـ) is (ر)' and 'Nothing of (ر) is (د)' and its conclusion is a universal affirmative conditional, its antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is a universal negative which is the result of combining the consequent with the categorical: 'Every (ب) is (ج) so nothing of (هـ) is (د).' An example of the third syllogism, in which the consequent and the categorical are both affirmative, except the consequent is particular: our saying: 'Every (ب) is (ج) so some (هـ) is (ر)' and 'Every (ر) is (د)' and its conclusion is a universal affirmative conditional, its antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is a particular affirmative which is the result of combining the consequent with the categorical. An example of the fourth syllogism, in which the consequent is a particular affirmative and the categorical is a universal negative: our saying: 'Every (ب) is (ج) so some (هـ) is (ر)' and 'Nothing of (ر) is (د)' and its conclusion is a universal affirmative, its antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is a particular negative which is the result of combining the consequent with the categorical. And as for when the conditional is particular: An example of the first syllogism: our saying: 'It may be that if (ب) is (ج) then every (هـ) is (ر)' and 'Every (ر) is (د)' and its conclusion is a particular affirmative conditional, its antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is a universal affirmative, like our saying: 'It may be that if (ب) is (ج) then every (هـ) is (د).' An example of the second syllogism: 'It may be that if (ب) is (ج) then every (هـ) is (ر)' and 'Nothing of (ر) is (د)' and its conclusion is a particular affirmative, its antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is a universal negative, like our saying: 'It may be that if (ب) is (ج) then nothing of (هـ) is (د).' An example of the third syllogism: 'It may be that if (ب) is (ج) then some (هـ) is (ر)' and 'Every (ر) is (د)' and its conclusion is a particular affirmative, its antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is a particular affirmative, like our saying: 'It may be that if (ب) is (ج) then some (هـ) is (د).' An example of the fourth syllogism: 'It may be that if (ب) is (ج) then some (هـ) is (ر)' and 'Nothing...'

Commentary
This passage is a detailed treatment of compound syllogisms where the major premise is categorical and the minor is a conditional conjunctive proposition. The author, likely from the Islamic logical tradition, explains how to form valid syllogisms of this mixed type. The passage begins by stating the general case: the categorical may be the major or minor premise. Here, the focus is when the categorical is major. Then, depending on whether the conditional is affirmative or negative, and whether the common term (the middle) is shared with the consequent or the antecedent of the conditional, the author outlines four possible figures of the middle term. These correspond exactly to the four figures of categorical syllogisms: first figure (middle is predicate of consequent and subject of categorical), second (middle is predicate of both), third (middle is subject of both), and fourth (middle is subject of consequent and predicate of categorical). The first figure is then examined in detail. Its properties, conditions for production, and quantity are the same as for categoricals, except that the conclusion is always a conditional conjunctive. However, a key additional condition is highlighted: mere affirmation of the conditional is insufficient; the relation of the predicate to the subject in the consequent must be affirmative. This is because a negative judgment about the consequent does not necessarily transfer to the antecedent. The author emphasizes that the consideration is solely on the relation between the consequent and the categorical, not on the conditional as a whole. Then, examples are provided for both universal and particular conditional premises. For each type, four syllogisms of the first figure are given, corresponding to combinations of universal/particular and affirmative/negative. The examples use abstract letters (ب, ج, هـ, ر, د) as placeholders. In an Islamic context, one could substitute familiar terms: for instance, 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' (كلي موجب) and 'Every nabidh is intoxicant' to conclude 'Every nabidh is forbidden' if structured properly. The author's abstract examples illustrate the logical forms without distraction.

Checkpoint
In a first-figure compound syllogism with a categorical major 'Every R is D' and a conditional minor 'If B is J, then every H is R', what is the condition for the inference to be valid?
A) The conditional must be universal.
B) The categorical must be affirmative.
C) The relation in the consequent 'every H is R' must be affirmative.
D) The middle term must be the predicate of the categorical.

Key Takeaway
A valid first-figure compound syllogism with a categorical major and conditional minor requires the consequent clause to be affirmative, and the conclusion is a conditional formed from the original antecedent and the result of the inner categorical syllogism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given the premises: 'If an animal is carrion (mayta), then it is impure (najis)' and 'Every impure thing is forbidden to eat', construct the first-figure compound syllogism and write the conclusion. Verify that the consequent 'it is impure' is affirmative.
?
Examine the following argument: 'If wine (khamr) is intoxicating, then it is forbidden.' and 'No forbidden thing is permissible to trade.' What is the compound conclusion? Check if the conditions for the first figure are satisfied. If not, explain why.

The Syllogism
advanced
15 min
Four Moods of Conjunctive Syllogism with Universal Conditional
Four conjunctions with universal conditional (first figure).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four conjunctions (اقترانات) of a universal conditional with a categorical premise in the first figure and derive the correct conditional conclusion.

Core Idea
The four conjunctions transform the consequent using the categorical to produce a universal conditional conclusion.

This lesson examines the four valid combinations of a universal conditional premise with a categorical premise in the first figure of conjunctive syllogisms. The author systematically presents each conjunction based on the quantity and quality of the consequent and the categorical, showing how the conclusion is always a universal conditional with the same antecedent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional premise
Locate the conditional (شرطية متصلة) which is universal affirmative: 'Every B is J, so (some proposition about H and R)'. Note the antecedent (B is J) and the consequent (proposition about H and R).
The universal conditional is introduced with 'كل ما كان...فـ'.
2
Identify the categorical premise
Find the categorical (حملیة) premise. It is either universal affirmative or universal negative, and it relates the middle term R to the term D.
The categorical is always universal (كل or لا شيء) when the conditional is universal.
3
Match to one of the four conjunctions
Check the quantity and quality of the consequent (the proposition about H and R) and the categorical (about R and D). There are four possibilities: (1) both universal affirmative; (2) consequent universal affirmative, categorical universal negative; (3) consequent particular affirmative, categorical universal affirmative; (4) consequent particular affirmative, categorical universal negative.
The author labels these as 'الاقتران الأول', 'الثاني', etc.
4
Derive the conclusion
Form a new conditional with the same antecedent (B is J) and a new consequent that combines the subject of the original consequent (H) with the predicate of the categorical (D) using the same quantity and quality as the categorical applied to H. For conjunction 1: 'كل هـ د' (universal affirmative). For conjunction 2: 'لا شيء من هـ د' (universal negative). For conjunction 3: 'بعض هـ د' (particular affirmative). For conjunction 4: 'ليس كل هـ د' (particular negative).
The conclusion is always a universal conditional because the original conditional is universal.

Continuation of the first art: on defining the forms of conjunctive syllogisms. Example of the second conjunction: in which the consequent is universal affirmative and the categorical is universal negative, our saying (Every B is J, so every H is R) and (No R is D) and its result is a universal affirmative conditional, its antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is a universal negative which is the result of conjoining the consequent with the categorical, namely (Every B is J, so no H is D). Example of the third conjunction: in which the consequent and the categorical are both affirmative, except that the consequent is particular, as our saying (Every B is J, so some H are R) and (Every R is D) and its result is a universal affirmative conditional, its antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is a particular affirmative which is the result of conjoining the consequent with the categorical. Example of the fourth conjunction: in which the consequent is particular affirmative and the categorical is universal negative, as our saying (Every B is J, so some H are R) and (No R is D) and its result is a universal affirmative conditional, its antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is a particular negative which is the result of conjoining the consequent with the categorical. And if the conditional is particular: Example of the first conjunction: our saying (It may be that if B is J then every H is R) and (Every R is D) and its result is a particular affirmative conditional, its antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is a universal affirmative, as our saying (It may be that if B is J then every H is D). Example of the second conjunction: (It may be that if B is J then every H is R) and (No R is D) and its result is a particular affirmative conditional, its antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is a universal negative, as our saying (It may be that if B is J then no H is D). Example of the third conjunction: (It may be that if B is J then some H are R) and (Every R is D) and its result is a particular affirmative conditional, its antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is a particular affirmative, as our saying (It may be that if B is J then some H are D). Example of the fourth conjunction: (It may be that if B is J then some H are R) and (No R is D) and its result is a particular affirmative conditional, its antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is a particular negative, as our saying (It may be that if B is J then not every H is D). The production from these conjunctions is clear; for whenever R is predicated of H, whatever is predicated of R affirmatively or negatively is predicated of H, and (H is R) was necessary for (B is J); so H, which is the subject of what is predicated of R, is necessary for (B is J) in any case considered, considering the difference in quantity and quality as previously. And if the form of the middle term is that it is a predicate in the consequent and the categorical, then it is the second figure, and its production is accomplished by considering in the conjunction of the categorical with the consequent what was considered in the second figure of categoricals, whether the conditional is universal or particular, the four conjunctions also occur. If it is universal: Example of the first conjunction, in which the consequent and the categorical are both universal, but the consequent is affirmative and the categorical is negative, as our saying (Every B is J, so every H is R) and (No D is R) and its result is a universal affirmative conditional, its antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is a universal negative which is the result of conjoining the consequent with the categorical or its conversion, namely (Every B is J, so no H is D); because if it is predicated of H and denied of every D by conversion and contraposition, then if (Every B is J, so every H is R) and (No D is R), then (Every B is J, so no H is D). Example of the second conjunction: in which the consequent and the categorical are both universal, except that the consequent is negative and the categorical is affirmative, as our saying (Every B is J, so no H is R) and (Every D is R) and its result is a universal affirmative conditional, its antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is a universal negative which is the conjunction of the consequent with the categorical, namely (Every B is J, so no H is D); and its proof: if R is denied of every H and affirmed of every D, it is known from categoricals by conversion and contraposition that no H is D, so it must be that every B is J, so no H is D.
Source pages p. 302

Commentary
This passage is an exposition of the forms of conjunctive syllogisms (قياسات اقترانية) where the major premise is a conditional (شرطية متصلة) and the minor premise is a categorical (حملیة). The author systematically presents four conjunctions (اقترانات) for the case where the conditional is universal, then four for when it is particular, and finally discusses the second figure where the middle term (الحد الأوسط) is predicate in both the consequent and the categorical. The examples use variable letters (ب, ج, هـ, د, ر) to illustrate the logical forms. The key idea is that the middle term (ر) mediates between the subject of the conditional's consequent (هـ) and the predicate of the categorical (د). The author shows how the result (نتيجة) is always a conditional with the same antecedent as the original conditional and a new consequent derived from combining the original consequent with the categorical premise. For the first figure (when the middle term is subject in the categorical and predicate in the consequent), the four conjunctions are distinguished by the quantity and quality of the consequent and the categorical: (1) both universal affirmative, (2) consequent universal affirmative and categorical universal negative, (3) consequent particular affirmative and categorical universal affirmative, (4) consequent particular affirmative and categorical universal negative. The results are universal if the conditional is universal, and particular if the conditional is particular. For the second figure (middle term predicate in both), the conjunctions are again four, with the categorical and consequent both universal but differing in quality. The author explains that the production (إنتاج) relies on the principle: whatever is predicated of the middle term (ر) is predicated of the subject of the consequent (هـ), because the middle term is predicated of that subject, and the middle term's predication is necessary given the antecedent (ب,ج). Thus, the result is a conditional linking the antecedent to the predication about هـ. The discussion of conversion and contraposition (بالعكس والخلف) is used to justify the inference when the middle term is predicate in both. This passage is a detailed technical treatment of the forms of conjunctive syllogisms, providing a complete classification for the connected conditional (متصل) and the categorical.

شرطیة متصلة

The Syllogism
