Second Figure of Compound Syllogisms: Universal Conditional Conjunctions
Author defines the second figure and illustrates four conjunctions for universal conditional.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the second figure of a compound syllogism with a universal conditional and determine the conclusion's consequent from the four conjunctions.

Core Idea
The second figure's conclusion is always a universal affirmative conditional, with the consequent derived by categorical inference.

Having mastered the first figure of compound syllogisms, we now turn to the second figure, where the middle term is the predicate in both the consequent of the conditional and the categorical premise. The author outlines the four possible conjunctions of the consequent and categorical premise when the conditional is universal, each producing a specific conclusion. Understanding these patterns is essential for constructing and evaluating compound syllogisms in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
1. Identify the second figure structure
In the second figure, the middle term (al-hadd al-awsaṭ) is the predicate in both the consequent of the conditional and the categorical premise. Check that the shared term between the two premises occupies the predicate position.
The middle term does not appear in the conclusion.
2
2. Recognize the inference rules
The inference from the consequent and the categorical premise follows the rules of the second figure of categorical syllogisms. Recall that valid moods in the second figure are Cesare, Camestres, Festino, and Baroco.
The author uses conversion and contraposition (al-‘aks wa al-khalf) to derive the conclusion.
3
3. Parse the four conjunctions for universal conditional
For each conjunction, note the quality (affirmative/negative) and quantity (universal/particular) of the consequent and the categorical premise. The four cases are: (1) universal affirmative consequent + universal negative categorical, (2) universal negative consequent + universal affirmative categorical, (3) particular affirmative consequent + universal negative categorical, (4) particular negative consequent + universal affirmative categorical.
The conditional is universal affirmative in all cases.
4
4. Derive the consequent of the conclusion
Apply categorical reasoning to each conjunction to find the resulting consequent. For example, in the first conjunction: from 'Every H is R' and 'No D is R', we conclude 'No H is D' by Cesare. The conclusion is a universal affirmative conditional: 'Every that is B is J → no H is D'.
The antecedent of the conclusion remains the same as the antecedent of the original conditional.
5
5. Verify the pattern across all four
Confirm that in each case the conclusion is a universal affirmative conditional, and the consequent matches the categorical result. Practice by constructing the conclusions for the other three conjunctions.
The third and fourth conjunctions involve particular premises and yield a particular negative consequent ('not every H is D' or 'some H is not D').

For it has been known by contraposition and supposition that if R is denied of some H and affirmed of every D, then D is denied of some H, so if every that is B is J, then not every H is D.

Commentary
This passage defines the second figure of compound syllogisms (where the conditional is connected, and the categorical premise is attached). The author explains that in this figure, the middle term (i.e., the term shared between the consequent of the conditional and the categorical premise) serves as the predicate in both. The condition for valid inference is the same as in the second figure of purely categorical syllogisms: the categorical premise must be of the appropriate quantity and quality. The author then provides examples for all four possible conjunctions when the conditional is universal. In each, the conclusion is a universal affirmative conditional with the same antecedent, and the consequent is derived by applying categorical inference to the consequent and the categorical premise. The first conjunction has a universal affirmative consequent and a universal negative categorical premise, yielding a universal negative consequent. The second is opposite: universal negative consequent and universal affirmative categorical premise, also yielding universal negative. The third has particular affirmative consequent and universal negative categorical, giving particular negative consequent. The fourth has particular negative consequent and universal affirmative categorical, giving a negative universal consequent (not every H is D). The proofs rely on known categorical conversions and contraposition.

Particular Conditional Syllogisms: The Four Conjunctions
Author details the four conjunctions for particular conditional

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to derive the conclusion of any of the four conjunctions when the conditional premise is particular.

Core Idea
A particular conditional yields a particular conclusion; the antecedent is preserved.

In compound syllogisms where the conditional premise is particular (indicated by 'قد يكون'), the author identifies four conjunctions based on the quality and quantity of the consequent and the categorical premise. Understanding these patterns allows you to construct valid syllogisms even with particular conditionals.

Guided Path
1
Identify the outer quantifier
Notice that the conditional begins with 'قد يكون' (it may be that), indicating the conditional is particular. This means the conclusion will also be a particular conditional, regardless of the inner proposition's quantity.
The outer quantifier is always 'قد يكون' in these examples.
2
Extract the antecedent and consequent
The antecedent is the part after 'إذا كان' (if/when) up to 'ف' (then). The consequent is the proposition after 'ف'. In the author's notation, the antecedent is 'ب ج' and the consequent is a categorical proposition about H and R.
The antecedent is preserved in the conclusion.
3
Identify the categorical premise
The second premise is a categorical proposition about D and R. In all four examples, it is universal (either universal affirmative 'كل د ر' or universal negative 'لا شيء من د ر').
In the universal conditional case, the categorical premise could be particular, but here it is always universal.
4
Determine the conjunction number
The four conjunctions correspond to the combination of the consequent's quality and quantity and the categorical premise's quality. The author lists them in order: 1) consequent universal affirmative, categorical universal negative; 2) consequent universal negative, categorical universal affirmative; 3) consequent particular affirmative, categorical universal negative; 4) consequent particular negative, categorical universal affirmative.
Memorize the pattern: the categorical premise alternates between negative and affirmative.
5
Form the conclusion
The conclusion is a particular conditional with the same antecedent. The new consequent is derived by combining the original consequent and the categorical premise using categorical syllogism rules. For example, from 'كل هـ ر' and 'لا شيء من د ر' we infer 'لا شيء من هـ د' (universal negative). The conclusion's consequent may be universal or particular depending on the premises.
The conclusion's outer quantifier is 'قد يكون', making it a particular conditional even if the inner consequent is universal.

Second Arrangement: Middle Term as Subject in Both Premises
Introduces arrangement where middle term is subject in both premises

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to check whether a compound syllogism with the middle term as subject in both premises satisfies the condition for valid inference.

Core Idea
When the middle term is the subject in both premises, validity requires the consequent to be affirmative and at least one premise universal.

The author introduces a new configuration where the middle term appears as the subject in both the consequent of the conditional and the categorical premise. This arrangement has specific conditions: the consequent must be affirmative, and either the consequent or the categorical premise must be universal. These conditions mirror those of the first figure in categorical syllogisms. The author begins illustrating with the universal conditional case, but the passage is incomplete. We can still learn the condition and apply it to similar examples.

Guided Path
1
Identify the position of the middle term
Check whether the same term is the subject in both the consequent of the conditional and the categorical premise. For example, if the consequent is 'كل هـ ر' and the categorical is 'كل هـ د', the middle term is 'هـ' (subject in both).
If the middle term is not the subject in both, this arrangement does not apply.
2
Check the condition: consequent must be affirmative
The consequent (the proposition in the conditional after 'ف') must be affirmative, i.e., not negative. For example, 'كل هـ ر' is affirmative, but 'لا شيء من هـ ر' is negative and would violate the condition.
Negative consequents are not allowed in this arrangement.
3
Check the condition: at least one premise must be universal
Either the consequent or the categorical premise (or both) must be universal. Universal propositions are 'كل' or 'لا شيء'. Particular propositions ('بعض') do not satisfy the condition.
If both are particular, the inference is invalid.
4
Determine validity
If both conditions are met, the inference is valid under this arrangement. Otherwise, it is invalid.
The actual conclusion form is not specified in the text, but the condition must hold for any valid inference.

Commentary
This passage first completes the discussion of the second figure by considering the case where the conditional is particular. The author gives four examples in which the conditional begins with 'قد يكون' (it may be that), meaning the conditional is particular (possible). The general form is the same as before, but the conclusion becomes a particular affirmative conditional (since the conditional is particular, the conclusion follows suit). The first and second conjunctions yield a universal negative consequent; the third and fourth yield a particular negative consequent. The explanation is said to be analogous to the universal case. The author then transitions to a new arrangement of the middle term: here the middle term is the subject in both the consequent and the categorical premise. The condition for valid inference is that the consequent must be affirmative and either the consequent or the categorical premise must be universal (as in the first figure of categorical syllogisms). The author begins to give examples for the case when the conditional is universal, starting with the first conjunction where both are universal affirmative, but the text is cut off.

The Syllogism
intermediate
10 min
Deriving Conclusions in Mixed Conditional-Categorical Syllogisms
Examples of four conjunctions with universal and particular conditionals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to derive the conditional conclusion from a universal conditional premise and a categorical premise by applying the rules of conjunctive syllogisms.

Core Idea
The conclusion keeps the antecedent and transforms the consequent using the categorical premise.

This lesson examines conjunctive syllogisms where one premise is conditional and the other is categorical. The author presents four moods for universal conditionals and four for particular conditionals. You will learn the pattern for deriving the conclusion: the antecedent stays the same, and the consequent results from conjoining the original consequent with the categorical premise using valid categorical inference.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two premises
Locate the conditional premise (shartiyya) and the categorical premise (hamliyya). The conditional has an antecedent (muqaddam) and a consequent (tali). The categorical is a simple declarative statement.
2
Determine the mood of the conjunction
Note the quantity (universal/particular) and quality (affirmative/negative) of the consequent and the categorical premise. There are four combinations: universal affirmative + universal affirmative, universal affirmative + universal negative, etc.
The source gives examples for moods where the consequent is particular and the categorical is universal or vice versa.
3
Derive the conclusion's consequent
Treat the original consequent and the categorical premise as premises of a categorical syllogism. Apply the rules of conversion and syllogistic inference to obtain a new statement that becomes the consequent of the conclusion.
For example, from 'some H is R' and 'no D is R', you derive 'not every H is D' (i.e., some H is not D).
4
Formulate the conclusion
Write the conclusion as a conditional with the same antecedent as the first premise and the newly derived consequent. Verify that the conclusion's quality and quantity match the expected mood.
The conclusion is always a conditional, never a categorical.

Source pages p. 304

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of conjunctive syllogisms (qiyāsāt iqtirāniyya), specifically those combining a conditional premise (sharṭiyya) and a categorical premise (ḥamliyya). The author defines the forms ('uṣūr) of such conjunctions (iqtrānāt). He gives examples for the third and fourth moods when the conditional is universal, and then for all four when it is particular. In each case, the middle term is between the antecedent (muqaddam) and the categorical premise. The conclusion is always a conditional with the same antecedent as the original, and its consequent results from combining the original consequent with the categorical premise. For instance, in the third universal conjunction: the consequent is 'some H is R' (particular affirmative) and the categorical is 'no D is R' (universal negative). The conclusion's consequent becomes 'not every H is D' (particular negative), derived by conjoining the two. The author notes that the reasoning is well-known through reductio (al-khalf) and assumption (al-iftirāḍ). The same patterns are shown for particular conditionals (qad yakūnu idhā...), where the conclusion is also particular.

Four Conjunctions with a Particular Conditional Premise
Author continues exemplifying conjunctive syllogisms when the conditional is particular (juz'iyya).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to derive the conclusion for each of the four conjunctions when the conditional premise is particular, and articulate the pattern that governs the conclusion's form.

Core Idea
A particular conditional yields a particular conclusion, preserving the antecedent while the consequent is derived from the premises.

In earlier lessons, you learned how conjunctive syllogisms function with universal conditional premises. Now the author turns to cases where the conditional is particular (juz'iyya), signalled by 'qad yakūnu' ('it may be that'). These patterns are essential for reasoning with non-universal norms in Islamic legal theory (usul al-fiqh) and theology (kalam).

Guided Path
1
Identify the marker of particularity
Look for the phrase 'qad yakūnu' at the beginning of the conditional premise. This indicates that the conditional is particular (juz'iyya), not universal.
In the Arabic text, this phrase appears in each example: 'قد يكون إذا كان...'.
2
Extract the antecedent and consequent
For each conjunction, note the antecedent (the part after 'if') and the consequent (the part after 'then'). The antecedent is preserved unchanged in the conclusion.
In the first example: antecedent = 'B is J', consequent = 'every H is R'.
3
Combine the categorical premise with the consequent
Take the categorical premise (e.g., 'no D is R') and combine it with the original consequent using the rules of categorical syllogisms. The result becomes the new consequent of the conclusion.
For the first conjunction: universal affirmative 'every H is R' plus universal negative 'no D is R' yields 'no H is D'.
4
Form the particular conditional conclusion
The conclusion is a particular conditional: 'qad yakūnu idhā [antecedent] fa [new consequent]'. The quantity of the new consequent depends on the premises; it may be universal or particular.
In the first example, the consequent is universal negative; in the third, it is particular negative.

Commentary
This passage provides examples for the four conjunctions when the conditional premise is particular (juz'iyya), denoted by 'qad yakūnu idhā...' (it may be that if...). The structure is the same as before: the conditional's antecedent is preserved in the conclusion, and the consequent of the conclusion is derived from combining the original consequent with the categorical premise. However, because the conditional is particular, the conclusion is also a particular conditional (juz'iyya muttaṣila). In the first example, the consequent is universal affirmative (every H is R) and the categorical is universal negative (no D is R), yielding a conclusion whose consequent is universal negative (no H is D). The second example has a different form (the consequent is 'nothing of some H is R') which is a particular negative reading, and the conclusion is the same as the first. The third and fourth examples follow similarly. The author states that the justification is as already known, referring to earlier principles of reduction and assumption.

The Syllogism

Middle Term Between Consequent and Categorical
Author defines middle term between consequent and categorical

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the configuration where the middle term lies as subject in both the consequent and the categorical premise, and derive the correct conclusion for each of the six conjunctions.

Core Idea
Middle term as subject in both consequent and categorical yields valid conclusions through conversion and reduction.

In this passage, the author extends the logic of conditional syllogisms to a new form where the middle term is the subject in both the consequent of the conditional and the categorical premise. This configuration, though more complex, follows familiar validity conditions: the consequent must be affirmative, and at least one premise universal. The author provides six examples for universal conditionals and two for particular ones, showing how to derive the conclusion by conjoining the two premises about the middle term.

Guided Path
1
Identify the configuration
Recognize that the middle term (H) is the subject in both the consequent (e.g., 'every H is R') and the categorical (e.g., 'every H is D'). The consequent is the second part of the conditional, and the categorical is a separate categorical premise.
The antecedent of the conditional is about B and J; it remains unchanged in the conclusion.
2
Check the validity conditions
The consequent must be affirmative (mūjab). At least one of the two premises (the consequent or the categorical) must be universal, similar to categorical syllogisms. This ensures the middle term is distributed properly.
If the consequent is negative or both are particular, no conclusion follows.
3
Derive the conclusion's consequent from the two premises about H
Take the two premises that share H: e.g., 'every H is R' and 'every H is D'. By conversion and reduction (al-‘aks wa al-khalf), infer 'some R is D'. This is a particular proposition because two universal affirmatives yield a particular in the third figure.
The author uses ‘aks (conversion) and khalf (reductio) to justify this step.
4
Form the full conclusion
The conclusion is a conditional with the same antecedent as the original conditional, and the newly derived consequent. For the first conjunction: 'Every B is J → some R is D'.
When the categorical is negative, the derived consequent is also negative: 'some R is not D'.
5
Adapt for particular conditionals
If the original conditional is particular (e.g., 'It may be that if B is J then every H is R'), the conclusion becomes a particular conditional: 'It may be that if B is J then some R is D'.
The same derivation of the consequent applies, but the modality changes from universal to particular.

1
ومثال الاقتران الخامس: وهو ما التالي فيه كليّ موجب، والحَمْلية جزئية سالبة، ونتيجته كالتي قبله.
ومثال الاقتران السادس وهو ما التالي فيه جزئي موجب، والحَمْلية كلية سالبة، ونتيجته أيضاً كالتي قبله، ولا يخفى عليك بيان الإنتاج في جميع هذه الاقترانات. وأما إن كانت الشرطية جزئية:
فمثال الاقتران الأول: قد يكون إذا كان "ب" "ج" فكل "هـ" "ر") و(كل "هـ" "د") ونتيجته جزئية موجبة متصلة، مقدمها مقدم الشرطية وتاليها جزئي موجب وهو (قد يكون إذا كان "ب" "ج" فبعض "ر" "د").
ومثال الاقتران الثاني: (قد يكون إذا كان "ب" "ج" فبعض "هـ" "ر") و(كل "هـ" "د" ونتيجته كالتي قبله.
And if the form of the middle term is between the consequent and the categorical, being the subject in both, then the condition for production is that the consequent must be affirmative, and it or the categorical must be universal, as previously in categoricals. Then the conditional is either universal or particular. If it is universal: Example of the first conjunction: in which the consequent and the categorical are both universal affirmative, as we say: 'Every 'B' is 'J' then every 'H' is 'R' and 'Every 'H' is 'D' and its conclusion is a universal affirmative conditional, its antecedent the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent a particular affirmative, which is the result of conjoining the consequent with the categorical if they were alone, namely: 'Every 'B' is 'J' then some 'R' is 'D' because it is known by conversion and reduction that if every 'H' is 'R' and every 'H' is 'D', then some 'R' is 'D', by means of 'H'. So if that is conditioned by 'Every 'B' is 'J' then some 'R' is 'D', it is also conditioned by it. And example of the second conjunction: in which the consequent and the categorical are both affirmative, except that the consequent is particular and the categorical is universal, as we say: 'Every 'B' is 'J' then some 'H' is 'R' and 'Every 'H' is 'D' and its conclusion is like the previous. And example of the third conjunction: in which the consequent and the categorical are both affirmative and the categorical is particular, as we say: 'Every 'B' is 'J' then every 'H' is 'R' and 'Some 'H' is 'D' and its conclusion is like the previous. And example of the fourth conjunction: in which the consequent and the categorical are both universal, and the categorical is negative, as we say: 'Every 'B' is 'J' then every 'H' is 'R' and 'No 'H' is 'D' and its conclusion is a universal affirmative conditional, its antecedent the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent a particular negative, which is the result of conjoining the consequent with the categorical if they were alone, namely: 'Every 'B' is 'J' then some 'R' is not 'D'. And example of the fifth conjunction: in which the consequent is universal affirmative and the categorical is particular negative, and its conclusion is like the previous. And example of the sixth conjunction: in which the consequent is particular affirmative and the categorical is universal negative, and its conclusion also like the previous, and the explanation of production in all these conjunctions is not hidden from you. And if the conditional is particular: Example of the first conjunction: 'It may be that if 'B' is 'J' then every 'H' is 'R' and 'Every 'H' is 'D' and its conclusion is a particular affirmative conditional, its antecedent the antecedent of the conditional and its consequent a particular affirmative, which is 'It may be that if 'B' is 'J' then some 'R' is 'D'. And example of the second conjunction: 'It may be that if 'B' is 'J' then some 'H' is 'R' and 'Every 'H' is 'D' and its conclusion is like the previous.
Source pages p. 305

Commentary
This passage introduces a new configuration where the middle term (al-ḥadd al-awsaṭ) is the subject in both the consequent of the conditional and the categorical premise. The condition for validity is that the consequent must be affirmative (mūjab) and at least one of the two premises (the consequent or the categorical) must be universal, analogous to the rules in categorical syllogisms. The author then gives six examples when the conditional is universal. The first three have affirmative categoricals of varying quantities; the fourth to sixth involve negative categoricals. In each case, the conclusion's consequent is derived by conjoining the original consequent (about H) with the categorical (also about H) to produce a conclusion about R and D, using the fact that H is the common term. For example, from 'Every H is R' and 'Every H is D' we infer 'Some R is D' via conversion and reduction. The conclusion is a conditional with the same antecedent. When the conditional is particular, the same patterns yield particular conditional conclusions. The author notes that the justification is well-known.

Six Conjunctions of a Universal Conditional with a Categorical
Author enumerates six conjunctions of a universal conditional with a categorical, giving their conclusions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the six types of conjunctions between a universal conditional and a categorical premise and determine the conclusion for each.

Core Idea
A universal conditional paired with a categorical always yields a conditional conclusion.

In this passage, the author systematically enumerates six forms of conjunction (iqtiran) where the major premise is a universal conditional and the minor premise is a categorical proposition. For each form, the conclusion is always a conditional with the same antecedent as the original, but the consequent is a particular proposition derived from the two premises. Understanding these patterns is crucial for analyzing complex syllogisms in Islamic jurisprudence and kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the structure
Recognize that each conjunction has a universal conditional (e.g., 'If every B is J, then every H is R') and a categorical premise (e.g., 'Every H is D').
The conditional is always universal affirmative; only the categorical varies in quantity and quality.
2
Classify the categorical premise
Determine whether the categorical is universal or particular, affirmative or negative. There are six possible combinations (AA, UA, UP, UN, etc.) as enumerated by the author.
The first three have affirmative categoricals; the last three have negative categoricals.
3
Derive the consequent of the conclusion
The conclusion's consequent is always a particular proposition (affirmative if the categorical is affirmative, negative if negative). It is obtained by conjoining the original consequent with the categorical using conversion and reductio.
For example, from 'Every H is R' and 'Every H is D', we get 'Some R is D'.
4
Recognize the conclusion pattern
The conclusion is a universal affirmative conditional: 'If the original antecedent, then the derived particular consequent'.
The antecedent remains unchanged; only the consequent changes.
5
Apply to an Islamic example
Take a conditional from fiqh: 'If every sale (B) is permitted (J), then every transaction (H) requires consent (R).' Add categorical: 'Every transaction (H) involves mutual agreement (D).' Conclude: 'If every sale is permitted, then some mutual agreements require consent.'
This matches the first conjunction.

If every B is J, then every H is R; and every H is D; its conclusion is a universal affirmative conditional, its antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is a particular affirmative that is the result of conjoining the consequent with the categorical if they were isolated, i.e., 'Every B is J, so some R is D' because it is known by conversion and reductio that if every H is R and every H is D, then some R is D, and that is by means of H. Commentary
This passage enumerates six types of conjunctions (اقترانات) when the conditional premise is universal. A conjunction here means a syllogism consisting of a conditional (شرطية) as the major premise and a categorical (حملية) as the minor premise. The author explains each type by specifying the quantities and qualities of the consequent (tali) of the conditional and the categorical premise, and then gives the resulting conclusion. The conclusion is always a conditional with the same antecedent as the original conditional, but its consequent is derived from conjoining the original consequent with the categorical. For example, in the first conjunction, when the conditional is 'If every B is J, then every H is R' and the categorical is 'Every H is D', the conclusion is 'If every B is J, then some R is D'. This is derived because from 'Every H is R' and 'Every H is D', we get 'Some R is D' by conversion (عكس) and reductio (خلف). The author then provides three more examples: the second conjunction where the consequent is particular and the categorical is universal, the third where the categorical is particular, the fourth where the categorical is negative, etc. The pattern shows that the conclusion's consequent is always a particular proposition, either affirmative or negative, depending on the premises. The author concludes by stating that the method of production (إنتاج) is clear for all these cases. To illustrate with an Islamic example, consider the conditional: 'If every prayer (B) is obligatory (J), then every purification (H) is required (R).' And the categorical: 'Every purification (H) is a condition (D) for prayer.' The conclusion would be: 'If every prayer is obligatory, then some required things are conditions for prayer.' Note that the original uses symbolic variables, but the pattern is general.

Six Conjunctions for Particular Conditionals
Author enumerates six conjunctions when the conditional is particular, with examples and conclusions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the six conjunctions when a conditional premise is particular and derive the corresponding conclusion in a mixed conditional-categorical syllogism.

Core Idea
Particular conditionals yield particular conclusions, mirroring the universal pattern with possibility.

The author has shown the six conjunctions for universal conditionals. Now he turns to particular conditionals, where the premise begins with 'قد يكون' (maybe if). Understanding these patterns is essential for constructing valid syllogisms when certainty is lacking but probability suffices in Islamic legal reasoning and theological arguments.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the marker of particularity
Notice that each conditional premise starts with 'قد يكون' (maybe if), indicating that it is a particular proposition (جزيئية). This is the key distinction from the universal case.
In Islamic logic, 'قد' often signals possibility or particularity.
2
Identify the two premises in each conjunction
For each of the six conjunctions, there is one conditional premise and one categorical premise. For example, the first conjunction: 'قد يكون إذا كان ب ج فكل هـ ر' (Maybe if B is J, then every H is R) and 'كل هـ د' (Every H is D).
The categorical premise can be universal affirmative, particular affirmative, universal negative, or particular negative.
3
Determine the conclusion pattern
The conclusion always has the same antecedent as the conditional premise, but its consequent is a particular proposition with the same quality (affirmative or negative) as the categorical premise's consequent. For universal affirmative categoricals, the consequent is 'بعض ر د' (some R is D); for universal negative, it is 'بعض ر ليس د' (some R is not D).
The consequent of the conclusion is always particular, never universal.
4
Verify with an Islamic example
Take a fiqh example: 'قد يكون إذا طهرت المرأة من الحيض فكل وقت صلاة' (Maybe if a woman becomes pure from menstruation, then every time is prayer time) and 'كل وقت صلاة هو وقت وجوب الصلاة' (Every prayer time is an obligatory prayer time). The conclusion: 'قد يكون إذا طهرت المرأة من الحيض فبعض وقت الصلاة هو وقت وجوب' (Maybe if she becomes pure, then some prayer time is obligatory).
This shows how the pattern applies to rulings derived from probable indicators.

Commentary
This passage continues the enumeration of conjunctions, but now for the case where the conditional premise is particular (جزئية). The author gives six examples parallel to the previous ones. In each, the conditional begins with 'قد يكون إذا...' (maybe if...), indicating possibility or particularity. The conclusions are also particular conditionals, with the same antecedent as the original, and a particular consequent. For instance, the first conjunction: 'Maybe if B is J, then every H is R' combined with 'Every H is D' yields 'Maybe if B is J, then some R is D'. The pattern mirrors the universal case but with the modality of possibility. In Islamic reasoning, one might use such a syllogism when the conditional is not certain but only possible, e.g., 'Maybe if the sun rises, then it is daytime' combined with 'Every daytime is a time for prayer' yields 'Maybe if the sun rises, then some time for prayer is daytime.' The author shows that the same six combinations apply, and the production method is analogous.

Mixed Syllogisms: Middle Term as Subject in Consequent, Predicate in Categorical
Author explains a new arrangement where the middle term is subject in the consequent and predicate in the categorical, giving examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three conjunctions of mixed syllogisms where the middle term is subject in the consequent and predicate in the categorical, and derive the correct conclusion for each.

Core Idea
The position of the middle term determines the mood and validity of a mixed syllogism.

In this passage, the author introduces a new configuration of mixed syllogisms where the middle term appears in different roles: subject in the consequent of a conditional premise and predicate in a categorical premise. This arrangement expands the types of valid syllogisms you can construct. By mastering these patterns, you will be able to derive conclusions from combinations of conditional and categorical statements commonly used in Islamic legal and theological reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the middle term's position
Notice that in this arrangement, the middle term (الحد الأوسط) is the subject (موضوع) in the consequent (تالي) of the conditional premise and the predicate (محمول) in the categorical premise (الحملية). This distinguishes it from other arrangements.
2
Understand the three conjunctions
The author presents three conjunctions: (1) consequent universal negative + categorical universal affirmative, (2) both universal affirmative, (3) consequent universal affirmative + categorical particular affirmative. Each yields a specific conclusion.
3
Derive the conclusion step by step
For the first example: from 'Every B is J, so no H is R' and 'Every D is H', the conclusion is 'Every B is J, so no R is D'. This follows from conversion and reductio: since R is negated from every H, and H is affirmed of every D, then no R is D.
4
Apply to Islamic examples
Map the variables to real concepts: B = obligatory act, J = required, H = invalid act, R = accepted, D = accepted act. The syllogism becomes: 'Every obligatory act is required, so no invalid act is accepted' and 'Every accepted act is valid' yields 'Every obligatory act is required, so no accepted act is invalid'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And if the form of the middle term between the consequent and the categorical is that it is subject in the consequent and predicate in the categorical, then its properties in production and its conditions are as previously mentioned in the operations, regardless of whether the conditional is universal or particular. If it is universal: Example of the first conjunction from it: it is where the consequent and the categorical are both universal and the consequent is negative, e.g., 'Every B is J, so no H is R' and 'Every D is H'; its conclusion is a universal affirmative conditional, its antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is a universal negative that is the result of conjoining the consequent with the categorical if they were isolated, i.e., 'Every B is J, so no R is D' because it is known by reductio and conversion that if R is negated from every H and H is affirmed of every D, then no R is D. So if 'Every B is J, so no H is R' and 'Every D is H', then 'Every B is J, so no R is D'. Example of the second conjunction [from it]: it is where the consequent and the categorical are both universal, like our statement: 'Every B is J, so every H is R' and 'Every D is H'; its conclusion is a universal affirmative conditional, its antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is a particular affirmative, i.e., 'Every B is J, so some R is D'. Example of the third conjunction [from it]: it is where the consequent is universal affirmative and the categorical is particular affirmative, like our statement: 'Every B is J, so every H is R' and 'Some D is H'; its conclusion is like the previous one.
Source pages p. 306

Commentary
This passage introduces a new arrangement of the mixed syllogism where the middle term (الحد الأوسط) appears as the subject (موضوع) in the consequent (تالي) of the conditional and as the predicate (محمول) in the categorical premise (الحملية). The author reminds that the properties and conditions for production follow what was previously established for categorical syllogisms. He then provides examples assuming the conditional is universal. In the first example, the consequent is universal negative, the categorical is universal affirmative: 'Every B is J, so no H is R' and 'Every D is H' yields 'Every B is J, so no R is D'. This is justified by conversion and reductio (عكس وخلف). The second example has both universal affirmative premises: 'Every B is J, so every H is R' and 'Every D is H' yields 'Every B is J, so some R is D' (particular affirmative). The third example has a universal affirmative consequent and a particular affirmative categorical, yielding the same kind of conclusion. This shows how the middle term's position affects the syllogism. To illustrate with an Islamic example: consider the conditional 'Every obligatory act (B) is required (J), so no invalid act (H) is accepted (R)' and the categorical 'Every accepted act (D) is valid (H)'; then we conclude 'Every obligatory act is required, so no accepted act is invalid.' Note that the variables are placeholders for concepts.

The Syllogism

Valid Moods with a Universal Conditional Premise
Enumeration of five valid moods for universal conditional mixed syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the five valid moods of mixed syllogisms when the conditional is universal and the middle term is subject in the consequent and predicate in the categorical.

Core Idea
When the conditional is universal, only five specific combinations of consequent and categorical yield a valid conclusion.

The passage presents a systematic inventory of valid moods for mixed conjunctive syllogisms where one premise is a universal conditional and the other is a categorical. The middle term's position is fixed: it is the subject in the consequent of the conditional and the predicate in the categorical. Five distinct combinations of quantity and quality are shown to produce valid results. Understanding these moods is essential for constructing rigorous demonstrations in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional premise
Locate the universal conditional proposition (e.g., 'Every thing such that when B is J then every H is R'). Note that the antecedent is 'B is J' and the consequent is about H and R.
Universal conditionals begin with 'كل ما كان' or similar.
2
Identify the categorical premise
Find the categorical proposition that relates to the consequent via a middle term (e.g., 'every D is H'). The categorical can be affirmative or negative, universal or particular.
3
Determine the middle term role
Check that the middle term (e.g., 'H') is the subject in the consequent and the predicate in the categorical. This is the only configuration covered in this section.
The author explicitly assumes this form.
4
Apply the known rules of categorical syllogisms to the consequent
Combine the consequent of the conditional (as a categorical proposition) with the categorical premise using first-figure syllogistic rules. The middle term links them.
The author refers to 'the previously mentioned operations' for the rules.
5
Form the final conclusion
The conclusion is a conditional with the same antecedent as the original, and its consequent is the result of the categorical syllogism performed on the original consequent and the categorical premise. The mood determines whether the consequent is universal or particular, affirmative or negative.
For example, if the consequent is 'every H is R' and the categorical is 'every D is H', the result is 'every D is R', then converted to 'some R is D' in some moods.

Continuation of the first art: On defining the forms of conjunctive syllogisms.

Commentary
This passage is a systematic enumeration of the valid moods (iqtiranāt) of mixed conjunctive syllogisms, where one premise is a conditional (shartiyya) and the other is a categorical (hamliyya). The author defines the form of each mood and gives examples using Arabic logical variables (ب, ج, د, هـ, ر).

The passage begins with examples of the third through sixth conjunctions when the middle term's position is unspecified, with the conditional being particular ('قد يكون'). These produce particular affirmative conclusions. Then, the author turns to the case where the middle term is subject in the consequent of the conditional and predicate in the categorical. He divides this into two main categories: when the conditional is universal (kulliyya) and when it is particular (juz'iyya).

For the universal conditional, five conjunctions are exemplified, varying the quantity and quality of the consequent and categorical premise. For example, the first conjunction uses a universal negative consequent and a universal affirmative categorical, yielding a universal negative conclusion. The second uses both universal affirmatives to yield a universal affirmative with a particular consequent. The third mixes a universal affirmative consequent with a particular affirmative categorical. The fourth uses a universal affirmative consequent with a universal negative categorical, giving a universal affirmative conclusion with a particular negative consequent. The fifth uses a particular affirmative consequent with a universal negative categorical.

When the conditional is particular, five conjunctions are also given, though the examples for the third, fourth, and fifth are less detailed. The first uses a particular negative conditional and a universal affirmative categorical, yielding a particular affirmative with a universal negative consequent. The second uses a particular affirmative conditional and universal affirmative categorical, yielding a particular affirmative conclusion. The third (with a universal affirmative conditional and universal negative categorical) repeats but with a particular conclusion. The fourth mirrors the third but with a different combination, and the fifth uses a particular affirmative conditional with a universal negative categorical.

The author does not provide detailed justifications for all moods, noting that the manner of production follows what was previously established. The passage is primarily an inventory of valid moods, serving as a reference for constructing mixed hypothetical syllogisms in demonstration (burhān).

Fourth and Fifth Iqtiran with Universal Conditional and Categorical Premises
Examples of fourth and fifth iqtiran

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to derive the conclusion of a fourth or fifth iqtiran given a universal affirmative conditional and a universal negative categorical premise, using Arabic logical variables or Islamic examples.

Core Idea
The iqtiran combines a conditional and a categorical through shared terms to yield new conditional knowledge.

This passage presents two concrete forms of iqtiran (conjunctive syllogism) that mix a universal affirmative conditional with a universal negative categorical premise. In the fourth iqtiran, the conditional's consequent is universal; in the fifth, it is particular affirmative. Both yield a universal affirmative conditional conclusion. We will dissect the logical steps—conversion of the categorical and syllogistic combination—so you can replicate the inference in any Islamic science context.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two premises
The first premise is a universal affirmative conditional. In the fourth iqtiran: 'Every B is J → every H is R.' In the fifth: 'Every B is J → some H is R.' The second premise is a universal negative categorical: 'No D is H.'
The terms B, J, H, R, D are placeholders; in Islamic examples they become specific concepts (e.g., 'action', 'wajib', 'part', 'required', 'prohibited').
2
Convert the categorical premise
Convert 'No D is H' (universal negative) to its simple converse 'No H is D'. This is always valid for universal negatives.
This step makes the subject of the categorical match the subject of the conditional's consequent.
3
Combine the converted categorical with the conditional's consequent
For the fourth iqtiran: from 'Every H is R' and 'No H is D', we infer 'Not every R is D' (since all H are R and no H is D, there is at least one R that is not D). For the fifth: from 'Some H is R' and 'No H is D', we also infer 'Not every R is D' (since some H are R and no H is D, those H are R and not D).
This step uses a first-figure syllogism (with H as middle term) plus existential import for universal premises.
4
Form the conclusion conditional
Attach the original antecedent 'Every B is J' to the derived consequent 'Not every R is D'. The final conclusion is 'Every B is J → not every R is D.'
The conclusion is a universal affirmative conditional with the same antecedent and a particular negative consequent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And an example of the fourth conjunction thereof: it is that wherein the consequent and the categorical are both universal, and the categorical is negative, as our saying: (Every thing that is 'B' is 'J' so every 'H' is 'R') and (Nothing of 'D' is 'H'), and its conclusion is a universal affirmative conditional, its antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is particular negative, and it is: (Every thing that is 'B' is 'J' so not every 'R' is 'D'). Commentary
This passage gives two specific examples of iqtiran (conjunctive syllogisms) where the conditional premise is universal affirmative and the categorical premise is universal negative. In the fourth iqtiran, both the consequent of the conditional and the categorical premise are universal, with the categorical being negative. The conclusion is a universal affirmative conditional, with its antecedent identical to that of the first premise, and its consequent a particular negative proposition: 'Not every R is D.' This is derived from the given premises. In the fifth iqtiran, the consequent is particular affirmative, and the categorical is universal negative, yielding a similar conclusion. The method of demonstrating validity (bayān al-intāj) is the same as previously explained, which involves checking the syllogistic figures and conditions.

Constructing Conjunctive Syllogisms with Particular Conditional Premises
Examples of iqtiran when the conditional premise is particular.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct the conclusion of a conjunctive syllogism (iqtiran) when the conditional premise is particular, given the categorical premise.

Core Idea
A particular conditional premise always yields a particular conclusion when combined with a categorical premise.

In this lesson, we examine how the pattern of iqtiran (conjunctive syllogism) changes when the conditional premise is particular (juz'iyya), marked by 'qad yakūn'. The author provides five examples to illustrate the resulting conclusions. By working through these patterns, you will master the rules for deriving conclusions in such syllogisms, which are essential in theological and juristic reasoning where claims are often not absolute.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional and categorical premises
In each example, there is a conditional premise (shartiyya) and a categorical premise (hamliyya). The conditional is particular because it begins with 'qad yakūn' (it may be that). Note the antecedent and consequent of the conditional.
2
Extract the terms and their quantifiers
The conditional is of the form 'If B then [categorical proposition about H and R]'. The categorical premise is about D and H. Identify the terms: B, J, H, R, D. Note whether the categorical premise is universal or particular, affirmative or negative.
3
Apply the categorical premise to the consequent of the conditional
Treat the consequent of the conditional as a categorical proposition. Then combine it with the categorical premise using standard categorical syllogistic rules to derive a new categorical conclusion about R and D.
4
Form the conclusion conditional
The conclusion is a conditional with the same antecedent as the first premise, and the derived categorical statement as the consequent. The conclusion will also be particular because the premise is particular.
5
Check the quantifier of the conclusion
Verify that the conclusion's quantifier matches the pattern: the conclusion is particular affirmative or particular negative, never universal.

Commentary
This passage presents examples of iqtiran (conjunctive syllogisms) when the conditional premise is particular (juz'iyya), indicated by the phrase 'qad yakūn' (it may be that). The author lists five iqtiran figures, though the text appears to repeat the fourth and the fifth is garbled. In the first example, the conditional is 'If B is J then nothing of H is R' (universal negative consequent) and the categorical is 'Every D is H' (universal affirmative). The conclusion is a particular affirmative conditional: 'If B is J then nothing of R is D'. In the second, with 'every H is R' as consequent and the same categorical, the conclusion is 'If B is J then some R is D'. The third and fourth repeat similarly, with the fourth yielding a particular negative consequent: 'not every R is D'. The fifth seems to have a missing consequent, but the conclusion is said to be like the previous. The pattern is that the conclusion always has the same antecedent as the first premise, and the consequent is derived by applying the categorical premise to the original consequent using categorical syllogistic rules.

The Syllogism

Compound Syllogism with Common Term Between Antecedent and Categorical
Author details four productive conjunctions when the common term links antecedent and categorical.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct the four productive iqtirans when the common term links the antecedent of a conditional with a categorical premise.

Core Idea
When the common term is in the antecedent, only negative antecedents and universal categoricals yield a conclusion.

We now examine a more intricate arrangement: the common term between the antecedent of the conditional and the categorical premise. The author shows that only when the middle term is predicated in the antecedent and subject in the categorical (first figure pattern) does the compound syllogism produce a valid conclusion, and even then the antecedent must be negative and the categorical universal. Let's walk through the four cases and their proofs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the arrangement of the middle term
Note that the middle term can be in four positions: predicated in antecedent & subject in categorical (case 1), predicated in both, subject in both, or subject in antecedent & predicated in categorical. Only case 1 is productive.
The author explicitly says 'إن كان محمولاً في المقدم وموضوعاً في الحَمْلية على نسق الشكل الأول'.
2
Note the conditions for productivity
For the productive case, the antecedent must be negative (sālib) and the categorical must be universal (kulliyya).
The passage: 'فشرطه أن يكون المقدم سالباً، وأن تكون الحَمْلية كلية'.
3
Examine the first iqtiran
Premises: conditional (every thing that is nothing of B is J so H is R) and categorical (every J is A). Conclusion: new conditional (every thing that is nothing of B is A so H is R). The proof uses a first-figure syllogism: from 'nothing of B is A' and 'every J is A' we infer 'nothing of B is J', which is the antecedent of the original conditional, giving 'H is R'.
Work through the letters: B, J, A, H, R are terms.
4
Analyze the second and third iqtirans
Second iqtiran uses universal negative categorical; third uses particular negative antecedent and universal affirmative categorical. Each produces a new conditional with a modified antecedent derived via categorical syllogisms.
Note the role of conversion and subsumption in the proofs.
5
Consider the fourth iqtiran
The fourth is cut off in the text. It would involve a particular negative antecedent and a universal negative categorical, likely following the same pattern.
Complete the pattern: conclusion would have a particular negative antecedent from subject of antecedent and predicate of categorical.

This is if the common ground is between the consequent of the conditional and the categorical. As for if it is between the antecedent of the conditional and the categorical, and the conditional is universal affirmative and the categorical is in the place of the major premise, then either the middle term is predicated in the antecedent and subject in the categorical, or predicated in both, or subject in both, or subject in the antecedent and predicated in the categorical. Source pages p. 308

Commentary
This passage transitions to a new arrangement: when the common term (al-ishtirāk) is between the antecedent (muqaddam) of the conditional and the categorical premise (hamliyya), rather than the consequent. The conditional is assumed universal affirmative and the categorical acts as the major premise. The author enumerates four possible positions of the middle term (al-ḥadd al-awsaṭ) in the antecedent and categorical: either (1) it is predicated in the antecedent and subject in the categorical (like first figure), (2) predicated in both, (3) subject in both, or (4) subject in the antecedent and predicated in the categorical. Only the first case yields productive iqtiran, and even then with conditions: the antecedent must be negative, and the categorical must be universal. Within that, four productive iqtiran are given. The first iqtiran: antecedent universal negative, categorical universal affirmative. Example: 'Everything that is nothing of B is J so H is R' and 'Every J is A' gives conclusion 'Everything that is nothing of B is A so H is R'. The reasoning: from the conclusion's antecedent (nothing of B is A) and the categorical (every J is A), by first figure we infer 'nothing of B is J', which is the antecedent of the original conditional, yielding the consequent 'H is R'. The second iqtiran: antecedent universal negative, categorical universal negative. Example: 'Nothing of B is J so H is B' and 'Nothing of J is A' gives conclusion 'Everything that is B is A so H is B'. The proof uses conversion and first figure. The third iqtiran: antecedent particular negative, categorical universal affirmative. Example: 'Some B is not J so H is B' and 'Every J is A' gives conclusion 'Some B is not A so H is B'. The reasoning: if some B is not A, and J is under A, then some B is not J (since J is included in A), which triggers the conditional. The fourth iqtiran is partially given, with antecedent particular negative and categorical universal negative, but the text cuts off before completing the example.

Key Terms
الاشتراك (al-ishtirāk): The common term between the conditional and categorical premise that enables the syllogism.
الحد الأوسط (al-ḥadd al-awsaṭ): The middle term that appears in both premises and is eliminated in the conclusion.
الملزوم (malzūm): The antecedent (or condition) that implies the consequent; here used in the proof to show that the new antecedent implies the original antecedent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It must be affirmative
B) It must be negative
C) It must be particular
D) It must be universal

Key Takeaway
The only productive arrangement when the common term links the antecedent of a conditional with a categorical is when the middle term is predicated in the antecedent and subject in the categorical, with the antecedent negative and the categorical universal.

Four Productive Conjunctions for Mixed First-Figure Syllogisms
Author enumerates four productive conjunctions for mixed syllogisms of first figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and construct the four productive conjunctions of mixed first-figure syllogisms given a universal affirmative conditional and a universal categorical premise.

Core Idea
In mixed syllogisms, the antecedent must be negative and the categorical universal.

This passage from al-Daqā'iq al-Ḥaqā'iq explains how to form valid mixed syllogisms in the first figure. The key is the position of the middle term—predicated in the antecedent and subject in the categorical—and the requirement that the antecedent be negative. The author enumerates four productive conjunctions, each with a specific combination of antecedent and categorical form. Understanding these rules is essential for using conditional reasoning in Islamic legal and theological arguments, where premises often mix conditional and categorical statements.

Guided Path
1
Identify the syllogism type
The passage describes mixed syllogisms: one premise is a universal affirmative conditional (if...then), and the other is a universal categorical (subject-predicate). The middle term is the term shared between the conditional's antecedent and the categorical.
The middle term is not the same term in both premises; it is the term linking the antecedent and categorical.
2
Determine the middle term position
For the first figure pattern, the middle term is predicated in the antecedent and subject in the categorical. This matches the standard first figure where the middle term is subject of the major premise and predicate of the minor.
The author mentions three other positions (predicated in both, subject in both, etc.) but focuses only on the first figure case.
3
Note the condition for validity
For the conjunction to be productive, the antecedent must be negative (either universal or particular) and the categorical must be universal (affirmative or negative). This ensures a valid categorical syllogism can be formed from the antecedent and categorical.
If the antecedent were affirmative, the middle term would not be distributed properly, and no conclusion would follow.
4
Enumerate the four conjunctions
The four conjunctions are: (1) universal negative antecedent + universal affirmative categorical; (2) universal negative antecedent + universal negative categorical; (3) particular negative antecedent + universal affirmative categorical; (4) particular negative antecedent + universal negative categorical. Each yields a conclusion that is a universal affirmative conditional with a new antecedent derived from the subject of the original antecedent and the predicate of the categorical.
The conclusion's antecedent is always derived from the subject of the original antecedent and the predicate of the categorical, with the same quantity and quality as the original antecedent but adjusted if needed.
5
Verify each conjunction via categorical syllogism
For each conjunction, the author provides a proof: the antecedent of the conclusion is equivalent to a categorical syllogism combining the original antecedent and the categorical. For example, in the first conjunction, from 'No B is A' (from the antecedent) and 'Every C is A', we deduce 'No B is C', which is the antecedent of the original conditional, so the conditional conclusion follows.
This step shows how the mixed syllogism reduces to a categorical syllogism, justifying the conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

هذا إن كان الاشتراك بين تالي المتصلة والحَمْلية، إما إن كان بين مقدم المتصلة والحَمْلية، والشرطية كليّة موجبة والحَمْلية مكان الكبرى فإما أن يكون الحد الأوسط محمولاً في المقدم موضوعاً في الحَمْلية، وإما محمولاً فيهما، وإما موضوعاً فيهما، وإما موضوعاً في المقدم ومحمولاً في الحَمْلية. فإن كان محمولاً في المقدم وموضوعاً في الحَمْلية على نسق الشكل الأول فشرطه أن يكون المقدم سالباً، وأن تكون الحَمْلية كلية، وقرائنه المنتجة أربعة:

Continuation of the first art on defining the forms of conjunctive syllogisms. 27 This if the sharing is between the consequent of the conditional and the categorical, or if it is between the antecedent of the conditional and the categorical. The conditional is universal affirmative and the categorical takes the place of the major premise. Then the middle term is either predicated in the antecedent and subject in the categorical, or predicated in both, or subject in both, or subject in the antecedent and predicated in the categorical. If it is predicated in the antecedent and subject in the categorical in the pattern of the first figure, then its condition is that the antecedent be negative and the categorical be universal, and its productive conjunctions are four: The first conjunction: from a universal affirmative conditional whose antecedent is universal negative, and a universal affirmative categorical, like our saying: 'Everything such that no B is C is H R' and 'Every C is A', and its conclusion is a universal affirmative conditional whose antecedent is universal negative from the subject of the antecedent in the conditional and the predicate of the categorical, and its consequent is the consequent of the conditional, which is 'Everything such that no B is A is H R' because if 'No B is A' and 'Every C is A', then 'No B is C', which is the implicans of 'H R'. The second conjunction: from a universal affirmative conditional whose antecedent is universal negative, and a universal negative categorical, like our saying: 'Everything such that no B is C is H B' and 'No C is A', and its conclusion is a universal affirmative conditional whose antecedent is universal affirmative from the subject of the antecedent of the conditional and the predicate of the categorical, and its consequent is the consequent of the conditional, which is 'Everything such that every B is A is H B' because if 'Every B is A' and 'No C is A', then 'No A is C', so 'No B is C', which is the implicans of 'H B'. The third conjunction: from a universal affirmative conditional whose antecedent is particular negative and a universal affirmative categorical, like our saying: 'Everything such that some B is not C is H B' and 'Every C is A', and its conclusion is a universal affirmative conditional whose antecedent is particular negative from the subject of the antecedent of the conditional and the predicate of the categorical, which is 'Everything such that some B is not A is H B' because if 'A' is denied of some B and C falls under A, then some B is not C, which is the implicans of 'H B'. The fourth conjunction: from a universal affirmative conditional whose antecedent is particular negative, and a universal negative categorical, like our saying: 'Everything such that some B is not C is H R' and 'No C is A', and its conclusion is a universal affirmative conditional whose antecedent is particular affirmative from the subject of the antecedent of the conditional and the predicate of the categorical, which is 'Everything such that B is A is H R' because if 'Some B is A' and 'No C is A', then 'No A is C', and 'Some B' falls under A, so 'Some B is not C', which is the implicans of 'H R'.
Source pages p. 308

Commentary
This passage defines the forms of mixed syllogisms where one premise is a universal affirmative conditional and the other is a categorical, and the common term is between the antecedent of the conditional and the categorical. The author first enumerates the possible positions of the middle term in the antecedent and categorical: it can be predicated in the antecedent and subject in the categorical (matching the first figure), predicated in both, subject in both, or subject in the antecedent and predicated in the categorical. Then he specifies the condition for the first figure pattern: the antecedent must be negative and the categorical must be universal. Four productive conjunctions follow, each with an example and proof. In the first conjunction, the conditional has a universal negative antecedent, the categorical is universal affirmative, and the conclusion is a new conditional with universal negative antecedent. The proof uses the categorical syllogism: from 'No B is A' and 'Every C is A' we get 'No B is C', which is the antecedent of the original conditional, so the conditional conclusion follows. The second conjunction uses a universal negative categorical, leading to a universal affirmative antecedent in the conclusion. The third conjunction uses a particular negative antecedent, and the fourth uses a particular negative antecedent with a universal negative categorical. Each is proven by reducing to a categorical syllogism whose conclusion is the antecedent of the original conditional, thus establishing the new conditional. The examples use the non-Arabic letters (B, C, A, H, R) as placeholders for terms, which is typical in logical works to avoid distraction from real meanings. The key condition that the antecedent be negative and the categorical universal ensures the syllogism is productive.

Author enumerates four conjunctions for second figure mixed syllogisms with conditions.

Core Idea
The second figure requires the categorical to be universal and match the antecedent's quality.

This passage extends the second figure to mixed syllogisms combining a conditional and a categorical. It lays out four productive conjunctions with conditions. Understanding these forms helps you analyse complex conditional arguments in Islamic logic, such as those used in fiqh and kalam.

Guided Path

Recognise that the syllogism has a universal affirmative conditional as the first premise, and a categorical as the second. The middle term is predicated in both the antecedent and the categorical.

2

The categorical must be universal, and its quality (affirmative or negative) must match the quality of the antecedent of the conditional.
If the antecedent is affirmative, the categorical must be affirmative; if negative, the categorical is negative.
3
Examine the proof for each conjunction
For each conjunction, the author provides a proof by converting the mixed syllogism into a categorical syllogism. Identify the intermediate categorical reasoning that yields the conditional conclusion.
The proof uses the subjects of the antecedent and categorical as terms in a new categorical syllogism.

Second Figure Mixed Syllogisms: Four Valid Moods
Author details the second figure's four productive conjunctions with conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four valid conjunctions of a second figure mixed syllogism and verify their conditions.

Core Idea
In the second figure, the categorical must be universal and match the antecedent's quality; only four moods yield valid conclusions.

This passage by al-Abhari explains the conditions for a mixed syllogism in the second figure, where the middle term is predicate in both premises. He lists four productive conjunctions, each with an example using logical variables. Understanding these patterns is essential for constructing valid arguments in Islamic sciences, especially in Kalam where such syllogisms are used.

Guided Path
1
Identify the figure
Check that the middle term (J in the examples) is the predicate in both the conditional antecedent and the categorical premise. This is the hallmark of the second figure.
2
Verify the condition for the categorical
The categorical must be universal, and its quality (affirmative/negative) must equal the quality of the conditional antecedent. If the antecedent is affirmative, the categorical must be affirmative; if negative, negative.
3
Walk through the first conjunction
Take the example: 'Everything that is B is J, so H is B' (universal affirmative conditional with universal affirmative antecedent) and 'Every A is J' (universal affirmative categorical). The conclusion is 'Everything that is B is A, so H is B'. Why? Because J is the middle term; from 'Every B is J' and 'Every A is J', we infer 'Every B is A' (a categorical syllogism in the second figure). Then the conditional's conclusion H is B follows from B being A.
Note that the conclusion is a conditional with antecedent combining B and A.
4
Extend to the other conjunctions
The same logic applies when the antecedent is particular or negative. In each case, the categorical is universal and matches quality. The conclusion's antecedent is formed by taking the subject of the conditional antecedent and the subject of the categorical.
The four conjunctions cover all combinations of universal/particular and affirmative/negative antecedents.

And if the middle term is a predicate in both the conditional premise and the categorical premise according to the second figure, then its condition is that the categorical be universal, and that it be affirmative if the conditional antecedent is affirmative, and negative if the conditional antecedent is negative. Its productive conjunctions are four: The first conjunction: from a universal affirmative conditional whose antecedent is universal affirmative, and a universal affirmative categorical, like our statement: 'Everything that is B is J, so H is B' and 'Every A is J', and its conclusion is a universal affirmative conditional whose antecedent is a universal affirmative from the subject of the conditional's antecedent and the subject of the categorical, namely 'Everything that is B is A, so H is B' because if 'Every B is A' and 'Every A is J', then 'Every B is J', and that necessitates 'H is B'. The second conjunction: from a universal affirmative conditional whose antecedent is particular affirmative, and a universal affirmative categorical, like our statement: 'Everything that is some B is J, so H is R' and 'Every A is J', and its conclusion is a universal affirmative conditional whose antecedent is particular affirmative from the subject of the conditional's antecedent and the subject of the categorical, namely 'Everything that is some B is A, so H is R' because A is included under J, so (some B is J) and that necessitates 'H is R'. The third conjunction: from a universal affirmative conditional whose antecedent is universal negative, and a universal negative categorical, like our statement: 'Everything that is nothing of B is J, so H is R' and 'Nothing of A is J', and its conclusion is an affirmative conditional whose antecedent is universal affirmative from the subject of the conditional's antecedent and the subject of the categorical, namely 'Everything that is every B is A, so H is R' because if 'Every B is A' and 'Nothing of B is J', and that necessitates 'H is R'. The fourth conjunction: from a universal affirmative conditional whose antecedent is particular negative, and a universal negative categorical, like our statement: 'Everything that is some B not J, so H is R' and 'Nothing of A is J', and its conclusion is a universal affirmative conditional whose antecedent is particular affirmative from the subject of the conditional's antecedent and the subject of the categorical, namely 'Everything that is some B is A, so H is R' because J is negated from all A, so it is negated from some B, and that necessitates 'H is R'.

Commentary
This passage explains the second figure of a mixed syllogism where one premise is a universal affirmative conditional (shartiyya muttasila) and the other is a categorical (hamliyya), with the middle term appearing as predicate in both premises. The condition for validity is that the categorical premise must be universal, and its quality (affirmative/negative) must match the quality of the conditional antecedent. The author then enumerates four productive conjunctions (iqitiranat), each with an example using letters as placeholders. In each case, the conclusion is a universal affirmative conditional whose antecedent combines the subjects of the original premises. The reasoning relies on categorical syllogistic principles: the middle term (J) is eliminated to derive a relation between the subjects (B and A). For instance, in the first conjunction, from 'Every B is J' and 'Every A is J', we infer 'Every B is A' because the middle term J is common. This is analogous to a categorical syllogism in the second figure (where the middle term is predicate in both premises) but applied to conditional propositions. The four conjunctions cover combinations of universal/particular and affirmative/negative antecedents, always ensuring the categorical is universal and matching in quality.

Third Figure Mixed Syllogisms: Conditions and Conjunctions
Conditions and first three conjunctions of the third figure

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions of the third figure mixed syllogism and derive the conclusion for any given valid conjunction.

Core Idea
The third figure mixed syllogism requires a negative antecedent and a universal categorical premise.

This lesson unpacks the third figure of mixed conditional-categorical syllogisms, where the middle term serves as subject in both premises. The author specifies two essential conditions: the conditional antecedent must be negative (universal or particular), and the categorical premise must be universal. We then explore the first three of four productive conjunctions, each showing how to transform the premises into a valid conclusion using conversion and inclusion reasoning. Although the text cuts off, the pattern is clear: by manipulating the terms through logical operations, we generate new necessary statements.

Guided Path
1
Identify the middle term and figure
In the third figure, the middle term is the subject of both the conditional antecedent and the categorical premise. For example, in 'Every B is A' and the antecedent 'Nothing of B is J', B is the middle term.
2
Check the two conditions
Verify that the conditional antecedent is negative (either 'nothing is' or 'some is not') and that the categorical premise is universal (affirmative or negative). Both conditions must hold for a productive conjunction.
3
Analyze the first conjunction
The first conjunction uses a universal affirmative conditional with a universal negative antecedent, and a universal affirmative categorical. Follow the derivation: from 'Nothing of J is A' (obtained by inclusion and conversion) and the categorical, reach 'Nothing of B is J' which necessitates the consequent.
Focus on how the terms are converted: 'Nothing of J is A' converts to 'Nothing of A is J' but here they use a different order.
4
Extract the conclusion form
The conclusion is a universal affirmative conditional with a universal negative antecedent formed from the predicate of the conditional's antecedent and the predicate of the categorical. For the first conjunction, it becomes 'Everything that is nothing of J is A, so H is R'.
The consequent remains the same (H is R) across all conjunctions.
5
Apply to remaining conjunctions
Repeat the pattern for the second conjunction (universal negative categorical) and the third (particular negative antecedent). Note how the categorical quality changes the conversion path.
The text cuts off during the third conjunction; the fourth is missing.

Commentary
This passage begins the third figure of mixed syllogisms, where the middle term is the subject in both the conditional antecedent and the categorical premise. For example, in the first conjunction, from 'Nothing of B is J' and 'Every B is A', we derive 'Nothing of J is A' (by converting the categorical? Actually, the explanation uses: if 'Nothing of J is A' and all B is A, then 'Nothing of J is B', then convert to 'Nothing of B is J'). The third conjunction involves a particular negative antecedent and a universal affirmative categorical; the conclusion is 'Nothing of J is A' derived from the negation. The text is incomplete, missing the fourth conjunction. Overall, this figure demonstrates how the middle term as subject generates valid moods by manipulating the terms.
