Third Figure Conjunctive Syllogisms: Conditions and Four Conjunctions
Author defines third figure conditions and enumerates four valid conjunctions with justifications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the condition for a valid third-figure conjunctive syllogism and reconstruct any of its four valid conjunctions with their conclusions.

Core Idea
Third figure requires a negative conditional premise and a universal categorical; it yields a universal affirmative conditional conclusion.

In this passage, the author defines the third figure of conjunctive syllogisms (qiyas iqtirani) where the middle term is subject in both premises. He first states the condition—the conditional premise must be negative and the categorical must be universal—then enumerates exactly four valid conjunctions (iqtiranat) that satisfy this condition. Each conjunction pairs a specific type of conditional premise with a categorical, and the author provides a logical justification for the conclusion using conversion (ʿaks) and entailment. Mastering these conjunctions trains you to construct and evaluate complex syllogistic arguments that appear in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Extract the general condition
Read the opening sentence: 'شرطه أن يكون المقدم سالباً، وأن تكون الحَمْلية كلية'. Note that the conditional premise (muqaddam) must be negative (salb), and the categorical (hamliyya) must be universal (kulliyya). This is the key to a productive third figure.
This condition is stated explicitly; memorize it as the rule for this figure.
2
List the four conjunctions
The author presents four iqtiranat. Identify each by: (1) universal negative conditional + universal affirmative categorical, (2) universal negative conditional + universal negative categorical, (3) particular negative conditional + universal affirmative categorical, (4) particular negative conditional + universal negative categorical.
Notice that the categorical is always universal, and the conditional premise always has a negative muqaddam.
3
Verify the conclusion for the first conjunction
Take the first iqtiran: universal negative conditional (if nothing of B is J then H is R) + universal affirmative categorical (every B is A). The conclusion is a universal affirmative conditional: if nothing of J is A then H is R. Follow the justification: if (nothing of J is A) and (every B is A), then by conversion and entailment, (nothing of B is J), which is the original muqaddam; therefore the conclusion holds.
The justification relies on conversion (ʿaks) of the categorical proposition. Practice this logic with each conjunction.

Continuation of the first art: On defining the forms of conjunctive syllogisms. And if the middle term is subject in the premise and the categorical on the pattern of the third figure, its condition is that the premise be negative, and that the categorical be universal, and its productive conjunctions are four: The first conjunction from a universal affirmative conditional, whose premise is a universal negative, and a universal affirmative categorical, like our saying: 'Everything such that nothing of B is J, then H is R' and 'Every B is A', and its conclusion is a universal affirmative conditional, whose premise is a universal negative from the predicate of the premise of the conditional and the predicate of the categorical, and it is 'Everything such that nothing of J is A, then H is R' because if (nothing of J is A) and every B is under A, then (nothing of J is B) and its converse (nothing of B is J) is a necessary condition for (H is R). The second conjunction: from a universal affirmative conditional, whose premise is a universal negative, and a universal negative categorical, like our saying: 'Everything such that nothing of B is J, then H is R' and 'Nothing of B is A', and its conclusion is a universal affirmative conditional, whose premise is a universal affirmative from the predicate of the premise of the conditional and the predicate of the categorical, and it is (Everything such that J is A, then H is R) because when J is under A, then (nothing of J is B) follows and its converse (nothing of B is J) is a necessary condition for (H is R). The third conjunction: from a universal affirmative conditional, whose premise is a particular negative, and a universal affirmative categorical, like our saying: 'Everything such that some B is not J, then H is R' and 'Every B is A', and its conclusion is a universal affirmative conditional, whose premise is a universal negative from the predicate of the premise of the conditional and the predicate of the categorical, and it is (Everything such that nothing of J is A, then H is R) because if A is negated from every J and B is under A, then the negation of B from every J follows, and the negation of J from some B follows by necessity of the truth of its negation from all of it, which is a necessary condition for (H is R). The fourth conjunction: from a universal affirmative conditional, whose premise is a particular negative, and a universal negative categorical, like our saying: 'Everything such that some B is not J, then H is R' and 'Nothing of B is A', and its conclusion is a universal affirmative conditional, whose premise is a universal affirmative from the predicate of the premise of the conditional and the predicate of the categorical, and it is (Everything such that J is A, then H is R) because if every (J is A) and A is negated from every B, then J is negated from every B, and the negation of J from some B follows, which is a necessary condition for (H is R).
Source pages p. 310

Commentary
This passage defines the third figure of conjunctive syllogisms (qiyas iqtirani) where the middle term appears as the subject in both the conditional premise (muqaddam sharti) and the categorical premise (hamliyya). The author states the condition: for the third figure to be productive, the conditional premise must be negative, and the categorical must be universal. He then enumerates the four valid conjunctions (iqtiranat) that meet this condition. Each conjunction pairs a universal affirmative conditional (with either a universal negative or particular negative premise) with a universal affirmative or universal negative categorical premise. The conclusions are always universal affirmative conditionals, derived by converting the relationship between the two terms. The justifications rely on conversion ('aks) of the categorical proposition and logical entailment. The first conjunction uses a universal negative conditional premise and a universal affirmative categorical (كل ب أ); the conclusion negates the predicate of the conditional premise with the predicate of the categorical. The second conjunction uses a universal negative conditional and a universal negative categorical, yielding a conclusion where the predicate of the conditional is affirmed of the predicate of the categorical. The third uses a particular negative conditional and an affirmative categorical, concluding with a universal negative. The fourth uses a particular negative conditional and a negative categorical, concluding with a universal affirmative. The explanations demonstrate how the logical relations (salb, luzum) together with conversion produce the required conclusion. This is a typical commentary on the matn of the Sullam or similar, expanding the compressed rules into explicit examples.

Fourth Figure Conditions and First Two Conjunctions
Author defines fourth figure conditions and enumerates three valid conjunctions with justifications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions of the fourth figure in conjunctive syllogisms and reconstruct the first two valid conjunctions with their justifications.

Core Idea
The fourth figure flips the middle term: predicate in the conditional, subject in the categorical.

The fourth figure of conjunctive syllogisms is a powerful but restricted form. The author lays out precise conditions to ensure validity, then provides three productive conjunctions. In this lesson, we will unpack these conditions and work through the first two conjunctions, seeing how conversion and term relations justify the conclusions. The third conjunction is incomplete in our source, so we focus on the complete ones.

Guided Path
1
Identify the middle term position in the fourth figure
Note that the middle term is predicate in the conditional premise (muqaddam) and subject in the categorical premise. This is the defining characteristic of the fourth figure.
The middle term here is 'ب' (B) in the examples.
2
Extract the three conditions
From the passage, list: (1) the conditional premise must not be universal affirmative, (2) the categorical must be universal, (3) the categorical's quality matches the premise's quality (negative if premise is negative, affirmative if affirmative).
These conditions ensure the syllogism is productive.
3
Analyze the first conjunction
Given: universal negative conditional premise (muqaddam is universal negative) + universal negative categorical. Use conversion ('aks) to transform the categorical and then deduce the conclusion.
The conversion: from 'Nothing of A is B' to 'Nothing of B is A'. Then combine with the conditional premise to derive the universal affirmative conditional conclusion.
4
Analyze the second conjunction
Given: particular affirmative conditional premise + universal affirmative categorical. Show that by subsumption and conversion, the conclusion follows.
If some J is A and every A is B, then some J is B. Convert to some B is J, which matches the antecedent of the conditional, thus H is R follows.
5
Note the incomplete third conjunction
Recognize that the source cuts off. The premises are particular negative conditional and universal negative categorical. Hypothesize the likely conclusion pattern based on the first two conjunctions.
The conclusion likely would be a universal affirmative conditional with a universal or particular premise relating J and A.

The pattern suggests the conclusion would involve a universal affirmative conditional with a particular or universal premise. Four Figures of Negative Conditional Syllogisms
Author defines four figures for negative conditionals based on shared term position.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the figure of a mixed syllogism with a negative conditional premise by identifying the position of the shared term in the categorical and the consequent.

Core Idea
The four figures depend on whether the shared term is subject or predicate in the categorical and the consequent.

In this passage, the author extends the classification of figures to mixed syllogisms where the major premise is a negative conditional. Understanding these figures is essential for checking the validity of such arguments in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises
Locate the conditional (shartiyya) major premise and the categorical (hamliyya) minor premise. Note which terms are shared.
The conditional is negative; the categorical can be affirmative or negative.
2
Check sharing location
Determine whether the shared term occurs in the consequent (tali) or the antecedent (muqaddam) of the conditional. The passage only defines figures for sharing with the consequent.
If sharing is with the antecedent, the same four figures apply analogously.
3
Locate the shared term in each premise
In the consequent, identify if the shared term is subject or predicate. In the categorical, identify the same term's role (subject or predicate).
Use the definitions: subject in the consequent and predicate in the categorical = first figure; predicate in both = second; subject in both = third; subject in consequent and predicate in categorical = fourth.
4
Assign the figure
Based on the roles, assign the figure according to the four cases given in the passage.
Double-check the mapping: it mirrors the categorical figures but with the conditional as major.
5
Apply the first-figure condition
If the figure is first, verify that the categorical is universal and that the consequent is negative (not affirmative), because in a negative conditional an affirmative consequent is actually negative and vice versa.
This condition is essential for the syllogism to be productive.

تتمة الفن الأول في تعريف صور القياسات الاقترانية وموضوع الحَمْلية، وكليّة سالبة حَمْلية، كقولنا كل ما كان كل "ج" "أ" فـ "هـ" "ر" : لأنه إذا كان (كل "ج") وكان (لا شيء من "أ" "ب") فـ (لا شيء من "ج" "ب") وعكسه (لا شيء من "ب" "ج") ويلزمه (بعض "ب" ليس "ج") وهو ملزوم ("هـ" "ر") وعلى هذا فالحكم عند كون الشرطية جزئية موجبة في شروط الإنتاج وكمية الاقترانات في كل شكل على ما عرف في الكلية الموجبة، غير أن النتيجة تكون تابعة لها في الحكم. وأما إن كانت الشرطية سالبة فإما أن تكون الشركة بين الحَمْلية والتالي، أو بين الحَمْلية والمقدم، فإن كانت الشركة بين الحَمْلية والتالي فإما أن يكون ما به الاشتراك محمولاً في التالي وموضوعاً في الحَمْلية فهو الشكل الأول، وإما محمولاً فيهما فهو الشكل الثاني، وإما موضوعاً فيهما فهو الشكل الثالث، وإما موضوعاً في التالي ومحمولاً في الحَمْلية فهو الشكل الرابع، فإن كان الأول فشرطه في الإنتاج كلية الحَمْلية، وأن يكون تالي المتصلة السالبة سالباً لا موجباً؛ إذ الموجب في نفس الأمر سالب والسالب موجب؛ لأنك إذا قلبت ليس البتة إذا كان "ب" "ج" فكل "هـ" "ر" وكان معناه ليس يلزم أن يكون كل "هـ" "ر" من كل "ب" "ج") وإذا قلبت ليس البتة إذا كان "ب" "ج" فلا شيء من "هـ" "ر" كان معناه ليس يلزم السلب، والأول ينقلب إلى السلب إذ يصح أن يقال: إذا كان "ب" "ج" فليس كل "هـ" "ر") والثاني ينقلب إلى الإيجاب إذ يصح أن يقال: إذا كان "ب" "ج" فبعض "هـ" "ر") وقد عرف أنه لا إنتاج في الشكل الأول من هذه الاقترانات مما تالي الشرطية فيه سالب
Continuation of the first art on defining the forms of conjunctive syllogisms. And the subject of the categorical, and a universal negative categorical, such as our saying: Every C is A, then H is R. Because if (every C) and (no A is B) then (no C is B) and its converse (no B is C) and it necessitates (some B is not C) and it is the implicatum of (H is R). And upon this, the judgment when the conditional is particular affirmative regarding the conditions of production and quantity of conjunctions in each figure is as known in universal affirmative, except that the conclusion is dependent on it in judgment. And if the conditional is negative, then either the sharing is between the categorical and the consequent, or between the categorical and the antecedent. If the sharing is between the categorical and the consequent, then either that by which the sharing [occurs] is predicate in the consequent and subject in the categorical — it is the first figure; or predicate in both — it is the second figure; or subject in both — it is the third figure; or subject in the consequent and predicate in the categorical — it is the fourth figure. If it is the first, then its condition for production is the universality of the categorical, and that the consequent of the negative conditional be negative, not affirmative; for the affirmative in reality is negative and the negative is affirmative; because if you convert 'It is not at all that if B is C then every H is R', its meaning is 'it is not necessary that every H is R from every B is C'; and if you convert 'It is not at all that if B is C then no H is R', its meaning is 'it is not necessary the negation', and the first converts to negation in that it is correct to say: 'If B is C then not every H is R'; and the second converts to affirmation in that it is correct to say: 'If B is C then some H is R'. And it is known that there is no production in the first figure from these conjunctions such that the consequent of the conditional in it is negative.
Source pages p. 312

Commentary
This passage defines the four figures for syllogisms where the major premise is a negative conditional (shartiyya saliba) and the minor premise is categorical (hamliyya). The author first explains the base case of affirmative conditionals and then transitions to negative ones. He states that when the conditional is negative, the sharing (al-shirka) between the categorical and the conditional can occur either with the consequent (tali) or the antecedent (muqaddam). If the sharing is with the consequent, then based on the position of the shared term as predicate or subject, the four figures are defined: first figure (predicate in consequent, subject in categorical), second (predicate in both), third (subject in both), fourth (subject in consequent, predicate in categorical). He then details the condition for the first figure: the categorical must be universal, and the consequent of the negative conditional must be negative, not affirmative, because an ostensibly affirmative consequent in a negative conditional is actually negative and vice versa. He illustrates with conversion of 'it is not at all that if B is C then every H is R' to show how it becomes negative. He concludes that from these conjunctions in the first figure when the consequent is negative, no direct production occurs without conversion. This passage is technical and requires careful parsing of the conditional and categorical terms.

Key Terms
الشرطية
الحَمْلية
التالي
المقدم
الشكل

A) First figure

D) Fourth figure

Key Takeaway
The four figures for negative conditionals are defined by the subject/predicate role of the shared term in the consequent and the categorical, with the first figure requiring a universal categorical and a negative consequent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the following mixed syllogism from kalam: Major (negative conditional): 'It is not true that if the world is temporally originated then it is not caused.' Minor: 'The world is temporally originated.' Identify the shared term and its position in the consequent and categorical. Determine the figure. Is it one of the four figures defined in the passage? If so, which one?

The Syllogism

First Figure Syllogisms with Universal Negative Conditionals
Author gives four examples of first figure conjunctions with universal negative conditionals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct the conclusion for any first figure mixed syllogism with a universal negative conditional and verify it using conditional conversion.

Core Idea
Universal negative conditionals pair with categoricals to yield new conditional conclusions.

This passage demonstrates how to handle mixed syllogisms where the conditional premise is universal and negative. By working through the four patterns, you will gain the skill to derive correct conclusions and understand the proof via conversion. These patterns are a cornerstone of logical argumentation in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional premise
Locate the universal negative conditional: it starts with 'ليس البتة' (not at all) and has an antecedent and a consequent.
Example: 'ليس البتة كل ما كان ب ج فبعض ه ليس ر'.
2
Identify the categorical premise
The second premise is a categorical proposition; note its quantity (universal) and quality (affirmative or negative).
Example: 'كل ر د' (universal affirmative).
3
Determine the pattern
Classify the consequent of the conditional (particular negative or universal negative) and the categorical (affirmative or negative). The author gives four cases.
The consequent's subject and predicate, and the categorical's predicate, determine the conclusion's consequent.
4
Construct the conclusion
The conclusion is a universal negative conditional with the same antecedent. The new consequent replaces the original consequent's predicate with the categorical's predicate.
For case 1: conclusion is 'ليس البتة إذا كان ب ج فبعض ه ليس د'.
5
Verify using qalb
Convert the universal negative conditional to an affirmative conditional (by removing the negation), apply the categorical to obtain the necessary consequent, then reform the negative conditional.
This shows why the conclusion follows logically.

Commentary
This passage provides detailed examples of the first figure conjunctions when the conditional is universal and negative. The author enumerates four types of conjunctions based on the quantity and quality of the consequent and the categorical premise. For each, he gives a formal example using variable letters, states the conclusion, and explains the proof by converting the conditional (qalb al-muttasila) to affirmation and taking the necessary consequent. He then adds a note that the same patterns apply when the conditional is particular, but the conclusion is particular. Finally, he begins the discussion of the second figure, stating its condition: the consequent and categorical must agree in quality (both negative or both affirmative?) and the categorical must be universal. He gives the first example of the second figure but the text breaks off. This is a teaching passage meant to demonstrate the mechanics of mixed conditional-categorical syllogisms.

Key Terms
الاقتران (al-iqtiran): A conjunction of a conditional and a categorical premise in a mixed syllogism. [Note: In some texts, iqtiran refers to conjunctive syllogisms; here it means the pairing of premises.]
قلب المتصلة (qalb al-muttasila): Conversion of a conditional proposition, typically to derive an equivalent form. [Note: Standard term in logic for converting conditional statements.]
لازم النتيجة (lazim al-natija): The necessary consequent or implication that follows from the conclusion. [Note: No significant shift.]

Third Figure of Conditional Syllogisms: Conditions and Examples
Author states the third figure's condition and gives three conjunction examples; text cuts off.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition for the third figure of conditional syllogisms and apply it to evaluate the validity of given conjunctions.

Core Idea
The third figure requires a negative consequent and at least one universal premise in reality.

In this passage, the author presents the specific condition for the third figure of conditional syllogisms: the consequent must be negative, and either the consequent or the categorical premise must be universal in fact (fi nafs al-amr). He then provides three examples to illustrate, though the third is incomplete. We will examine the condition and see how the conclusion is derived through conversion operations.

Guided Path
1
State the condition for the third figure
The condition is that the consequent (tali) is negative, and at least one of the consequent or the categorical premise (hamliyya) is universal in reality (not just in expression).
2
Identify the variables in the first example
In the first conjunction, the variables are: B, J, H, R, D. The conditional is 'Absolutely not every B is J so some H is not R' and the categorical is 'All H is D'.
3
Understand the derivation method
The author explains the conclusion is derived by (a) converting the negative conditional into an affirmative one, (b) converting the consequent (عكس التالي), and (c) taking the necessary consequence of the result.
The exact logical steps are complex and not fully detailed here.
4
Analyze the second example
The second conjunction has a universal negative consequent and a universal affirmative categorical. The result is similar to the first, indicating the method works when both the condition is satisfied.
5
Consider the incomplete third example
The third conjunction has a particular negative consequent and a particular affirmative categorical. Note that this violates the condition (neither is universal), so it likely does not yield a valid conclusion, though the text cuts off.
The text breaks off after stating the premises; no conclusion is given.

وأما إن كان الشكل الثالث فشرطه في الإنتاج أن يكون التالي سالباً، والتالي أو الحملية أيهما كان كلياً في نفس الأمر، وسواء كانت المتصلة كلية أو جزئية، لنفرض البيان في الكلية - وأنت تعرفه في الجزئية -. ومثال الاقتران الأول منه : وهو ما التالي فيه جزئي سالب والحملية كلية موجبة، كقولنا: ليس البتة كل ما كان "ب" "ج" فبعض "هـ" ليس "ر") و(كل "هـ" "د" ونتيجته ليس البتة إذا كان "ب" "ج" فلا شيء من "ر" "د") وبيانه بقلب المتصلة إلى الإيجاب، وعكس التالي، وأخذ لازم النتيجة. ومثال الاقتران الثاني منه : ما التالي فيه كلي سالب والحملية كليّة موجبة، كقولنا : ليس البتة كل ما كان "ب" "ج" فلا شيء من "هـ" "ر") و(كل "هـ" "د") ونتيجته كنتيجة ما قبله بالبيان السابق. ومثال الاقتران الثالث منه ما التالي فيه جزئي سالب والحملية جزئية موجبة.

Second Figure of Conjunctive Syllogisms: Conditions and Examples
Author explains second figure condition and provides four conjunct examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the condition for the second figure of conjunctive syllogisms and determine the valid conclusion from a given conditional and categorical premise.

Core Idea
The second figure of conjunctive syllogisms requires a negative conditional premise and a categorical premise of any quantity.

In this passage, the author lays out the rule for the second figure of mixed syllogisms (qiyas iqtirani) where one premise is a conditional and the other is a categorical. He gives four examples to show how different quantifiers in the consequent and the categorical affect the conclusion. Understanding this pattern helps you evaluate arguments in Islamic theology and jurisprudence where such combinations occur.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition for the second figure
The second figure requires a negative conditional (saliba muttasila). The categorical (hamliyya) can be either affirmative or negative.
This condition is stated at the beginning of the passage: 'wa kana (la al-muttasila al-saliba ila al-ijab...)'
2
Recognize the four conjuncts (iqtiranat)
The author presents four combinations based on the quantity of the consequent of the conditional and the categorical: first (implicitly) consequent universal affirmative + categorical universal negative; second consequent particular affirmative + categorical universal affirmative; third consequent universal negative + categorical universal negative; fourth consequent universal affirmative + categorical universal affirmative.
Each example is introduced by 'مثال الاقتران الثاني/ثالث/رابع منه'
3
Extract the conclusion for each conjunct
For each example, the author gives a conclusion that follows necessarily. Note that the conclusion is always a conditional (muttasila) with the same antecedent as the original conditional and a new consequent relating the terms of the two premises.
The pattern uses variables: ب, ج, هـ, د, ر. Identify them in each example.
4
Apply the method to particular conditionals
The author notes that even if the negative conditional is particular (juz'iyya), the same method of production (inti) applies. Check that the condition holds.
This extends the rule beyond the universal examples given.

وكان (لا المتصلة السالبة إلى الإيجاب كانت كل ما كان "ب" "ج" فكل "هـ" "ر") وكان (لا شيء من "د" "ر") فبيّن على ما عرف أنه كل ما كان "ب" "ج" فلا شيء من "هـ" "د" ويلزمه ليس البتة إذا كان "ب" "ج" فبعض "هـ" "د"). ومثال الاقتران الثاني منه وهو ما التالي فيه جزئي موجب، والحملية كلية موجبة، كقولنا: (ليس البتة كل ما كان "ب" "ج" فبعض "هـ" "ر") و(كل "د" "ر") ونتيجته كالتي قبله. ومثال الاقتران الثالث منه وهو ما التالي فيه كلي سالب والحَمْلية كليّة سالبة، كقولنا : ليس البتة كل ما كان "ب" "ج" فلا شيء من "هـ" "ر") و(لا شيء من "د" "ر") ونتيجته ليس البتة إذا كان "ب" "ج" فكل "هـ" "د"). ومثال الاقتران الرابع منه وهو ما التالي فيه كليّ موجب، والحملية كلية موجبة، كقولنا: ليس البتة كل ما كان "ب" "ج" فكل "هـ" "ر") و(كل "د" "ر") ونتيجته كنتيجة الذي قبله؛ للبيان السابق، وعلى هذا فلا تخفى كيفية الإنتاج، وإن كانت السالبة المتصلة جزئية.
And it was (the negative conditional to the affirmative was every what was 'B' 'J' then every 'H' 'R') and it was (nothing from 'D' 'R') so it was made clear according to what was known that every what was 'B' 'J' then nothing from 'H' 'D' and it entails not at all if was 'B' 'J' then some 'H' 'D'). And an example of the second conjunction of it, which is what its consequent is particular affirmative, and the categorical is universal affirmative, like our saying: (Not at all every what was 'B' 'J' then some 'H' 'R') and (every 'D' 'R') and its result is like the one before it. And an example of the third conjunction of it, which is what its consequent is universal negative and the categorical is universal negative, like our saying: Not at all every what was 'B' 'J' then nothing from 'H' 'R') and (nothing from 'D' 'R') and its result is not at all if was 'B' 'J' then every 'H' 'D'). And an example of the fourth conjunction of it, which is what its consequent is universal affirmative and the categorical is universal affirmative, like our saying: Not at all every what was 'B' 'J' then every 'H' 'R') and (every 'D' 'R') and its result is like the result of the one before it; for the previous explanation, and upon this then the manner of production is not hidden, even if the negative conditional is particular.
Source pages p. 314

Commentary
This passage defines the second figure of conjunctive syllogisms (qiyas iqtirani) where one premise is a conditional (muttasila) and the other a categorical (hamliyya). The author first establishes the condition for the second figure: the consequent of the conditional must be negative, and the categorical can be affirmative or negative. Then four examples (اقترانات) are given. The first example (mentioned implicitly earlier) shows: if every B is J then every H is R, and nothing from D is R, leading to the conclusion that if B is J then nothing from H is D. The second example: not at all if B is J then some H is R, and every D is R, so if B is J then some H is D. The third example: not at all if B is J then nothing from H is R, and nothing from D is R, so if B is J then every H is D. The fourth example: not at all if B is J then every H is R, and every D is R, so if B is J then every H is D (same as third). The author notes that the same method applies even if the conditional is particular. In terms of Islamic logic, one can substitute terms: e.g., if it is intoxicating (B) then it is forbidden (J), and every nabidh (H) is intoxicating (R), and every khamr (D) is forbidden (R), then the conclusion follows.

Conditions and Productive Conjunctions of the Fourth Figure
Author states conditions for the fourth figure and enumerates five productive conjunctions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions for a valid fourth-figure mixed syllogism and list the five productive conjunctions.

Core Idea
In the fourth figure, the consequent's quantity and quality dictate which categorical premises yield a valid conclusion.

In mixed syllogisms, the fourth figure places the middle term as subject of the conditional and predicate of the categorical. This lesson unpacks the exact restrictions on the categorical premise based on the consequent of the conditional, and then presents the five valid moods when the conditional is universal. Understanding these rules ensures you can assess the validity of arguments that combine conditional and categorical statements in Islamic theological and legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the figure
Recognize the fourth figure definition: the middle term is the subject of the conditional premise and the predicate of the categorical premise.
This differs from the first three figures in mixed syllogisms.
2
Parse the conditions on the categorical premise
The author gives four cases based on the quantity and quality of the consequent. Extract them: if consequent is universal negative, categorical cannot be universal affirmative or any particular; if particular affirmative, categorical must be universal affirmative; if particular negative, categorical cannot be particular negative.
The consequent here is the second part of the conditional (the part after 'then').
3
Apply the conditions to a sample
Take a hypothetical conditional with a given consequent and determine which categorical premises are allowed. For example, if the consequent is 'every H is R' (universal affirmative), what categorical premises are allowed?
The condition for universal affirmative consequent is not stated explicitly; only 'consequent not universal affirmative' as a global condition.
4
List the five productive conjunctions
The author enumerates five valid moods using variable notation. Understand each conjunction: the first premise is a negated universal conditional, the second is a categorical, and the conclusion is a negated universal conditional.
The notation uses 'ليس البتة' to negate the conditional.
5
Distinguish between allowed and disallowed moods
Practice recognizing which conjunctions are valid. Compare the conditions to the listed moods to see how they satisfy the restrictions.
All five moods assume the conditional is universal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إن كان الشكل الرابع فشرطه في الإنتاج أن لا يكون التالي كلياً موجباً، وإذا كان التالي كلياً سالباً فلا تكون الحَمْلية كلية موجبة، ولا جزئية لا موجبة ولا سالبة، وإن كان التالي جزئياً موجباً فلا بد وأن تكون الحَمْلية كلية موجبة، وإن كان جزئياً سالباً فلا تكون الحَمْلية جزئية سالبة، وعند ذلك فالشرطية إما أن تكون كلية وإما جزئية، فإن كانت كليّة فالقرائن المنتجة خمسة:
الاقتران الأول: ليس البتة إذا كان "ب" "ج" فلا شيء من "هـ" "ر") و (لا شيء من "د" "هـ") فـ (ليس البتة إذا كان "ب" "ج" فكل "ر" "د").
الاقتران الثاني: ليس البتة إذا كان "ب" "ج" فبعض "ج" فبعض "هـ" "ر") فـ (ليس البتة إذا كان "ب" "ج" فبعض "ر" "د").
الاقتران الثالث: ليس البتة إذا كان "ب" "ج" فليس كل "هـ" "ر") و(كل "د" "هـ") فـ ليس البتة إذا كان "ب" "ج" فلا شيء من "ر" "د").
الاقتران الرابع: (ليس البتة إذا كان "ب" "ج" فليس كل "هـ" "ر") و(لا شيء من "د" "هـ") فـ (ليس البتة إذا كان "ب" "ج" فكل "ر" "د").
الاقتران الخامس: ليس البتة إذا كان "ب" "ج" فليس كل "هـ" "ر") و(بعض "د" "هـ") فـ (ليس البتة إذا كان "ب" "ج" فلا شيء من "ر" "د").
As for the fourth figure, its condition for production is that the consequent not be universal affirmative. If the consequent is universal negative, then the categorical [premise] cannot be universal affirmative, nor particular either affirmative or negative. If the consequent is particular affirmative, then the categorical must be universal affirmative. If it is particular negative, then the categorical cannot be particular negative. Given this, the conditional is either universal or particular. If it is universal, the productive conjunctions are five: First conjunction: 'It is absolutely not the case that if B is C then no H is R' and 'No D is H'; therefore 'It is absolutely not the case that if B is C then every R is D'. Second conjunction: 'It is absolutely not the case that if B is C then some H is R'; therefore 'It is absolutely not the case that if B is C then some R is D'. Third conjunction: 'It is absolutely not the case that if B is C then not every H is R' and 'Every D is H'; therefore 'It is absolutely not the case that if B is C then no R is D'. Fourth conjunction: 'It is absolutely not the case that if B is C then not every H is R' and 'No D is H'; therefore 'It is absolutely not the case that if B is C then every R is D'. Fifth conjunction: 'It is absolutely not the case that if B is C then not every H is R' and 'Some D is H'; therefore 'It is absolutely not the case that if B is C then no R is D'.
Source pages p. 315

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of mixed syllogisms (qiyas iqtirani) where one premise is a conditional (muttasila) and the other is a categorical (hamliyya). Specifically, it treats the fourth figure of such syllogisms. The author first states the condition for a valid conclusion: the consequent of the conditional cannot be universal affirmative. He then lays out the restrictions on the categorical premise depending on the quantity and quality of the consequent. For instance, if the consequent is universal negative, the categorical cannot be universal affirmative or any particular. If the consequent is particular affirmative, the categorical must be universal affirmative. If the consequent is particular negative, the categorical cannot be particular negative. Then, assuming the conditional is universal, five productive conjunctions (iqṭirānāt) are listed. Each conjunction is a valid mixed syllogism of the fourth figure. The notation uses variables: B, C, H, R, D. The examples show the form of the conditional (often a negated universal conditional) and the categorical, leading to a negated universal conditional conclusion. For instance, the first conjunction: the conditional 'It is absolutely not the case that if B is C then no H is R' (i.e., a kind of particular conditional) combined with 'No D is H' yields 'It is absolutely not the case that if B is C then every R is D'. The author does not prove these here but states them as valid moods. The discussion is technical and assumes familiarity with syllogistic figures and the conversion rules that would make these valid.

Particular Conditionals in the Fourth Figure
Rule for particular conditionals in the fourth figure

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the conclusion type when a mixed syllogism in the fourth figure has a particular conditional premise.

Core Idea
A particular conditional in the fourth figure yields only a particular conditional conclusion.

In this passage, the author extends the rules of production to cases where the conditional premise is particular. Understanding this rule is crucial for correctly determining the conclusion in mixed syllogisms, as the quantity of the conclusion depends on the quantity of the conditional premise.

Guided Path
1
Identify the figure
Confirm that the syllogism is in the fourth figure (mixed: categorical and conditional).
The fourth figure is defined by the position of the common term.
2
Check the conditional's quantity
Determine if the conditional premise (shartiyya muttaṣila) is particular (juz'iyya) or universal.
The passage assumes you can identify particular conditionals.
3
Predict the conclusion
If the conditional is particular, the conclusion must be a particular conditional, not a universal one.
The author states: 'al-natīja lā takūn illā juz'iyya muttaṣila'.
4
Compare with the universal case
Recall that when the conditional is universal, the conclusion may be universal or particular depending on other conditions. Here it is restricted.
This lesson focuses only on the particular conditional case.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعلى هذا فلا يخفى الحال في الإنتاج عند كون المتصلة جزئية، إذ هي موازية لها، غير أن النتيجة لا تكون إلا جزئية متصلة. وأما إن كانت الحَمْلية مكان الصغرى والشرطية مكان الكبرى فالاشتراك ما بين مقدم الشرطية والحَمْلية، فإما أن يكون الحد المشترك بينهما محمولاً في الحَمْلية وموضوعاً في المقدم، وإما محمولاً فيهما، وإما موضوعاً فيهما، وإما موضوعاً في الحملية و محمولاً في المقدم، فإن كان محمولاً في الحَمْلية وموضوعاً في المقدم فهو الكائن على نمط الشكل الأول، فشرطه أن يكون المقدم حقاً في نفسه، ولا ينتج إذا كان المقدم كاذباً، فإنا إذا حولنا شرط وجود ("هـ" "ر") مثلاً إلى ("ج" "أ") أو سلب عن "ج" فلا يلزم من حكمنا بـ "أ" على "ب" إيجاباً، أو سلباً تحقق شرط "هـ" ("ر") بتقدير أن يكون الشرط محالاً في نفس الأمر، فلا يلزمه ("هـ" "ر") ولنمثل ذلك إذا كانت الحملية والشرطية كليتين موجبتين والمقدم جزئياً موجباً – وأنت تعرفه في الباقي - فنقول (كل "ب" "ج") و(كل ما كان بعض "ج" ف"هـ" "ر") فإذا استنتجنا من الحَمْلية والمقدم نتيجة وجعلناها مقدمة في النتيجة لا يلزمها ("هـ" "ر") وذلك كما لو قلنا فـ كل ما كان "ب" "أ") أو (بعض "ب" "أ" فـ "هـ" "ر") لم يكن لازماً، بتقدير أن لا يكون "ج" "أ") حقاً، ولا هو لازم عن قولنا: (كل "ب" "أ") ولا عن بعض "ب" "أ") وبتقدير أن يكون المقدم حقاً في نفس الأمر فشرطه أيضاً أن تكون الحَمْلية موجبة، وأن يكون المقدم جزئياً، قال أفضل المتأخرين إذا كان المقدم كلياً أنتج نتيجة متصلة جزئية، وليس بحق، فإنا إذا قلنا مثلاً (كل "ب" "ج") و (كل ما كان كل "ج" "أ" فـ ار") فالمقدم للنتيجة المأخوذة من الحَمْلية ومقدم الشرطية وهو قد يكون إذا كان كل "ب" "أ" لا يلازمه كل "ج" "أ") الذي هو شرط
And based on this, the situation regarding production when the conditional is particular is not hidden, for it is parallel to it, except that the conclusion is only a particular conditional. As for when the categorical takes the place of the minor and the conditional the place of the major, then the commonality is between the antecedent of the conditional and the categorical. Either the common term between them is predicate in the categorical and subject in the antecedent, or predicate in both, or subject in both, or subject in the categorical and predicate in the antecedent. If it is predicate in the categorical and subject in the antecedent, then it is in the pattern of the first figure, and its condition is that the antecedent be true in itself, and it does not produce if the antecedent is false. For if we transform the condition of the existence of ("H" "R") for example to ("J" "A") or negate it from "J", then it does not follow from our judgment of "A" on "B" affirmatively or negatively the realization of the condition of "H" ("R") assuming that the condition is impossible in reality, so "H" "R" does not follow from it. Let us illustrate that when the categorical and conditional are both universal affirmative and the antecedent is particular affirmative – and you know the rest – we say: (Every B is J) and (Everything that is some J is H R). If we infer a conclusion from the categorical and the antecedent and make it a premise in the conclusion, "H" "R" does not follow from it. Thus, as if we say: 'Every B is A' or 'Some B is A then H R', it is not necessary, assuming that 'J is A' is not true, nor does it follow from our statement 'Every B is A' nor from 'Some B is A'. And assuming that the antecedent is true in itself, its condition is also that the categorical be affirmative and the antecedent be particular. The best of the later scholars said: If the antecedent is universal, it produces a particular conditional conclusion, but it is not correct. For if we say for example (Every B is J) and (Everything that is every J is A then H R), the premise for the conclusion taken from the categorical and the antecedent of the conditional may be, if every B is A, it does not entail every J is A, which is the condition.
Source pages p. 316

Commentary
This passage extends the discussion of mixed syllogisms in the fourth figure to cases where the conditional is particular. The author states that when the conditional is particular, the conclusion is also a particular conditional. He then discusses another arrangement: when the categorical premise serves as the minor premise and the conditional as the major. In this case, the common term between the antecedent of the conditional and the categorical determines the figure. There are four possibilities for the common term: it can be predicate in the categorical and subject in the antecedent (which gives the first figure pattern), predicate in both, subject in both, or subject in the categorical and predicate in the antecedent. The author focuses on the first pattern, which resembles the first figure of categorical syllogisms. The condition for validity is that the antecedent must be true in itself; if the antecedent is false, no necessary conclusion follows. He illustrates with examples: if we have 'Every B is J' and 'Everything that is some J is H R', the antecedent 'some J' is particular. If we infer a conclusion from the categorical and the antecedent, it does not guarantee the condition 'H R' if the antecedent is false. The author also critiques a claim by a later scholar that if the antecedent is universal, the conclusion is a particular conditional; he argues this is incorrect, giving the counterexample where 'Every B is J' and 'Everything that is every J is A then H R' does not entail the condition because 'Every B is A' does not imply 'Every J is A'. The passage is dense and assumes knowledge of how to construct conclusions from mixed syllogisms.

Four Patterns of the Common Term
Classification of common term positions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four possible positions of the common term when the categorical is minor and the conditional is major in the fourth figure.

Core Idea
The common term's position determines the figure pattern and validity conditions.

When the categorical premise serves as the minor and the conditional as the major, the arrangement of the common term between them can take four forms. Each form corresponds to a different figure pattern. The author explains these possibilities and focuses on the one that mirrors the first figure.

Guided Path
1
Identify the arrangement
Recognize that the categorical is the minor premise and the conditional is the major premise.
This is a specific case of mixed syllogisms.
2
Locate the common term
The common term is the term shared between the categorical premise (ḥamliyya) and the antecedent (muqaddam) of the conditional.
It is not the full conditional, only the antecedent.
3
Enumerate the four possibilities
The common term can be: predicate in the categorical and subject in the antecedent; predicate in both; subject in both; subject in the categorical and predicate in the antecedent.
These are the only logical combinations.
4
Understand the first pattern
The first pattern (predicate in categorical, subject in antecedent) mirrors the first figure of categorical syllogisms and has specific validity conditions.
Other patterns are not discussed in detail here.

And based on this, the situation regarding production when the conditional is particular is not hidden, for it is parallel to it, except that the conclusion is only a particular conditional. As for when the categorical takes the place of the minor and the conditional the place of the major, then the commonality is between the antecedent of the conditional and the categorical. Either the common term between them is predicate in the categorical and subject in the antecedent, or predicate in both, or subject in both, or subject in the categorical and predicate in the antecedent. If it is predicate in the categorical and subject in the antecedent, then it is in the pattern of the first figure, and its condition is that the antecedent be true in itself, and it does not produce if the antecedent is false. For if we transform the condition of the existence of ("H" "R") for example to ("J" "A") or negate it from "J", then it does not follow from our judgment of "A" on "B" affirmatively or negatively the realization of the condition of "H" ("R") assuming that the condition is impossible in reality, so "H" "R" does not follow from it. Let us illustrate that when the categorical and conditional are both universal affirmative and the antecedent is particular affirmative – and you know the rest – we say: (Every B is J) and (Everything that is some J is H R). If we infer a conclusion from the categorical and the antecedent and make it a premise in the conclusion, "H" "R" does not follow from it. Thus, as if we say: 'Every B is A' or 'Some B is A then H R', it is not necessary, assuming that 'J is A' is not true, nor does it follow from our statement 'Every B is A' nor from 'Some B is A'. And assuming that the antecedent is true in itself, its condition is also that the categorical be affirmative and the antecedent be particular. The best of the later scholars said: If the antecedent is universal, it produces a particular conditional conclusion, but it is not correct. For if we say for example (Every B is J) and (Everything that is every J is A then H R), the premise for the conclusion taken from the categorical and the antecedent of the conditional may be, if every B is A, it does not entail every J is A, which is the condition.
Source pages p. 316

Commentary
This passage extends the discussion of mixed syllogisms in the fourth figure to cases where the conditional is particular. The author states that when the conditional is particular, the conclusion is also a particular conditional. He then discusses another arrangement: when the categorical premise serves as the minor premise and the conditional as the major. In this case, the common term between the antecedent of the conditional and the categorical determines the figure. There are four possibilities for the common term: it can be predicate in the categorical and subject in the antecedent (which gives the first figure pattern), predicate in both, subject in both, or subject in the categorical and predicate in the antecedent. The author focuses on the first pattern, which resembles the first figure of categorical syllogisms. The condition for validity is that the antecedent must be true in itself; if the antecedent is false, no necessary conclusion follows. He illustrates with examples: if we have 'Every B is J' and 'Everything that is some J is H R', the antecedent 'some J' is particular. If we infer a conclusion from the categorical and the antecedent, it does not guarantee the condition 'H R' if the antecedent is false. The author also critiques a claim by a later scholar that if the antecedent is universal, the conclusion is a particular conditional; he argues this is incorrect, giving the counterexample where 'Every B is J' and 'Everything that is every J is A then H R' does not entail the condition because 'Every B is A' does not imply 'Every J is A'. The passage is dense and assumes knowledge of how to construct conclusions from mixed syllogisms.

Condition for Production and Refutation of a Later Scholar
Condition for first pattern and refutation of a later scholar

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the validity condition for a mixed syllogism in the first pattern and critique a later scholar's claim about universal antecedents.

Core Idea
The antecedent must be true for production; a later scholar's claim that a universal antecedent yields a particular conclusion is false.

In this section, the author discusses the condition for production when the common term is predicate in the categorical and subject in the antecedent. He then presents and refutes a claim by a later scholar about the case where the antecedent is universal. Understanding this refutation sharpens our ability to assess syllogistic validity.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the first pattern
Identify that the common term is predicate in the categorical and subject in the antecedent, which is the pattern of the first figure.
This pattern has a specific condition for production.
2
Understand the condition
The antecedent must be true in itself for a valid conclusion. If the antecedent is false, no necessary conclusion follows.
The condition applies regardless of the truth of the categorical.
3
Analyze the example
With 'Every B is J' and 'Everything that is some J is H R', even if we infer something from the categorical and antecedent, H R does not follow if the antecedent is false.
The example illustrates the failure when the antecedent is not true.
4
Examine the later scholar's claim
The 'best of the later scholars' claimed that if the antecedent is universal, the conclusion is a particular conditional.
The author rejects this claim.
5
See the refutation
Counterexample: 'Every B is J' and 'Everything that is every J is A then H R' – from 'Every B is A' one cannot infer 'Every J is A', so the conclusion does not follow.
The refutation shows the claim is incorrect.

Four Arrangements of the Common Term in Mixed Syllogisms
Author explains four arrangements of common term and conditions for valid inference in mixed syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four possible positions of the common term in a mixed conditional-categorical syllogism and determine which corresponds to a valid first-figure form.

Core Idea
The common term's position determines whether the mixed syllogism follows a valid figure.

This passage examines mixed syllogisms where the categorical premise is the minor and the conditional is the major. The key is the common term shared between the categorical and the antecedent of the conditional. The author enumerates four possible arrangements, only one of which mirrors the first figure of categorical syllogisms and can yield a conclusion under specific conditions. Understanding these positions is essential for constructing valid composite arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two premises
Recognize that the first premise is a categorical proposition (e.g., 'Every B is J') and the second is a conditional proposition (e.g., 'If something is J, then ...').
The categorical is the minor, the conditional is the major.
2
Locate the common term (al-ḥadd al-mushtarak)
Find the term that appears in both the categorical and the antecedent of the conditional. This is the term that mediates the inference.
The common term is typically a subject or predicate in each.
3
Determine its position in the categorical
Check whether the common term is the subject or the predicate of the categorical proposition.
4
Determine its position in the antecedent
Check whether it is the subject or the predicate of the antecedent clause in the conditional.
5
Classify the arrangement
The four possibilities are: (1) predicate in cat, subject in ant; (2) predicate in both; (3) subject in both; (4) subject in cat, predicate in ant. Only the first corresponds to the first figure and is productive if the antecedent is true.
The author only develops the first arrangement in detail.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأما إن كانت الحملية مكان الصغرى والشرطية مكان الكبرى فالاشتراك ما بين ومقدم الشرطية، فإما أن يكون الحد المشترك بينهما محمولاً في الحملية وموضوعاً في المقدم، وإما محمولاً فيهما، وإما موضوعاً فيهما، وإما موضوعاً في الحملية ومحمولاً في المقدم، فإن كان محمولاً في الحملية وموضوعاً في المقدم فهو الكائن على نمط الشكل الأول، فشرطه أن يكون المقدم حقاً في نفسه، ولا ينتج إذا كان المقدم كاذباً، فإنا إذا حولنا شرط وجود ("هـ" "ر") مثلاً إلى ("ج" "أ") أو سلب عن "ج" فلا يلزم من فلا يلزم من حكمنا بـ "أ" على "ب" إيجاباً أو سلباً تحقق شرط "هـ" بتقدير أن يكون الشرط محالاً في نفس الأمر، فلا يلزمه ("هـ" "ر") ولنمثل ذلك إذا كانت الحملية والشرطية كليتين موجبتين والمقدم جزئياً موجباً – وأنت تعرفه في الباقي - فنقول (كل "ب" "ج") و(كل ما كان بعض "ج" ف) فإذا استنتجنا من الحملية والمقدم نتيجة وجعلناها مقدمة في النتيجة لا يلزمها ("هـ" "ر") وذلك كما لو قلنا فـ كل ما كان "ب" "أ") أو (بعض "ب" "أ" فـ "هـ" "ر") لم يكن لازماً، بتقدير أن لا يكون "ج" "أ" حقاً، ولا هو لازم عن قولنا: (كل "ب" "أ") ولا عن بعض "ب" "أ" وبتقدير أن يكون المقدم حقاً في نفس الأمر فشرطه أيضاً أن تكون الحملية موجبة، وأن يكون المقدم جزئياً، قال أفضل المتأخرين إذا كان المقدم كلياً أنتج نتيجة متصلة جزئية، وليس بحق، فإنا إذا قلنا مثلاً (كل "ب" "ج") و(كل ما كان كل "ج" "أ" فـ ار) فالمقدم للنتيجة المأخوذة من الحملية ومقدم الشرطية وهو قد يكون إذا كان كل "ب" "أ" لا يلازمه كل "ج" "أ" الذي هو شرط "هـ" "ر") فلا يلزم عنه ("هـ" "ر") إلا بتقدير أن يكون "ب" مساوياً لـ "ج" وبتقدير أن لا يكون مساوياً لـ "ج" فلا يلزم من قولنا كل ما كان كل "ب" "أ" في "هـ" "ر"). فإن قيل: لما احتمل أن يكون "ب" مساوياً لـ "ج" واحتمل أن لا يكون صح أن النتيجة وهي أنه قد يكون إذا كان كل "ب" "أ" فـ "هـ" "ر"). فنقول: وكما احتمل أن يكون "ب" مساوياً لـ "ج" واحتمل أن لا يكون فكذلك احتمل أن يكون المقدم حقاً، واحتمل أن لا يكون وبتقدير أن يكون حقاً فالاقتران منتج، وبتقدير أن لا يكون حقاً فلا إنتاج، فكان ينبغي أن يجزم بكون النتيجة أيضاً جزئية من غير شرط أن يكون المقدم حقاً، وقد شرط أن يكون المقدم حقاً في الإنتاج، كيف وأنه بتقدير أن لا يكون مساوياً لألف فلا تصدق النتيجة أصلاً، ولا في حالة ما والجزئية فلا معنى لها إلا ما صدقها في حالة ما، وخاصة أنه ينتج الكلي وإن كان المقدم والحملية جزئيين
As for when the categorical is in the place of the minor and the conditional is in the place of the major, the commonality is between and the antecedent of the conditional. So either the common term between them is predicate in the categorical and subject in the antecedent, or predicate in both, or subject in both, or subject in the categorical and predicate in the antecedent. If it is predicate in the categorical and subject in the antecedent, it is in the pattern of the first figure. Its condition is that the antecedent is true in itself, and it does not produce if the antecedent is false. For if we transform the condition of existence of ('h', 'r') for example to ('j', 'a') or negation from 'j', then it does not follow from our judgment of 'a' upon 'b', affirmatively or negatively, the realization of the condition of 'h', assuming that the condition is impossible in itself, so ('h', 'r') does not follow. Let us exemplify that when the categorical and conditional are both universal affirmative and the antecedent is particular affirmative—and you know the rest—so we say (Every 'b' is 'j') and (Everything that is some 'j' is...). So if we infer from the categorical and the antecedent a conclusion and make it a premise in the conclusion, ('h', 'r') does not follow. That is as if we say: Every 'b' is 'a' or Some 'b' is 'a' then 'h' is 'r' is not necessary, assuming that 'j' is 'a' is not true, and it is not necessary from our saying (Every 'b' is 'a') nor from (Some 'b' is 'a'). And assuming that the antecedent is true in itself, its condition is also that the categorical be affirmative and that the antecedent be particular. The best of the later scholars said: if the antecedent is universal, it produces a particular conditional conclusion, and it is not correct. For if we say for example (Every 'b' is 'j') and (Everything that is every 'j' is 'a' then ...) the antecedent of the conclusion taken from the categorical and the antecedent of the conditional, it may be that when every 'b' is 'a' it does not entail every 'j' is 'a' which is the condition of ('h' is 'r'), so ('h' is 'r') does not follow except assuming that 'b' is equal to 'j'. And assuming it is not equal to 'j', then from our saying 'everything that is every 'b' is 'a' then 'h' is 'r'' does not follow. If it is said: since it is possible that 'b' is equal to 'j' and possible that it is not, then the conclusion is correct, namely that it may be that when every 'b' is 'a' then 'h' is 'r'. We say: just as it is possible that 'b' is equal to 'j' and possible that it is not, similarly it is possible that the antecedent is true and possible that it is not. Assuming it is true, the conjunction produces; assuming it is not true, there is no production. So one should affirm that the conclusion is also particular without the condition that the antecedent be true, and he conditioned that the antecedent be true for production. How so? Because assuming it is not equal to 'a', the conclusion is not true at all, not in any case, and particularity has no meaning except that it is true in some case. Especially since it produces the universal even if the antecedent and categorical are particular.
Source pages p. 316

Commentary
This passage is a detailed explanation of the rules for combining a categorical premise (as minor) with a conditional premise (as major) in a mixed syllogism. The author begins by stating the four possible relationships between the common term in the categorical and the antecedent of the conditional: (1) predicate in categorical, subject in antecedent; (2) predicate in both; (3) subject in both; (4) subject in categorical, predicate in antecedent. The first corresponds to the first figure of categorical syllogism. The condition for production is that the antecedent must be true in itself; if it is false, no conclusion follows. The author illustrates with an example where both premises are universal affirmative and the antecedent is particular affirmative. He then criticizes a view attributed to 'the best of the later scholars' who claimed that if the antecedent is universal, the conclusion is a particular conditional. The author refutes this with a counterexample showing that unless the middle term is coextensive (musawi) with the subject, the conclusion does not follow universally. He anticipates an objection: since the middle term may or may not be coextensive, the conclusion could be particular. He responds that the same uncertainty applies to the truth of the antecedent; only when the antecedent is actually true does the conjunction produce. In fact, under the assumption of non-coextension, the conclusion is false entirely. Therefore, the conclusion is not merely particular; it can be universal even when both premises are particular, if the antecedent is true. This discussion demonstrates the careful conditions required for valid mixed syllogisms in the tradition.

Key Terms
الحد المشترك (al-ḥadd al-mushtarak): The term shared between the categorical premise and the antecedent of the conditional premise in a mixed syllogism. [Note: No significant shift.]
المقدم (al-muqaddam): The antecedent of the conditional proposition in a mixed syllogism; also used for the minor premise in categorical syllogisms. [Note: In this context, 'muqaddam' refers specifically to the antecedent of the conditional, not the minor premise.]
الاقتران (al-iqtirān): A combination or conjugation of premises in a syllogism, here specifically a mixed conditional-categorical syllogism. [Note: In standard logic, 'iqtirān' often refers to the conjunctive syllogism (qiyās iqtirānī) as opposed to istithnā'ī.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Subject in categorical and predicate in antecedent

Key Takeaway
Only the arrangement where the common term is predicate in the categorical and subject in the antecedent yields a valid first-figure mixed syllogism, provided the antecedent is true.

Refuting the Claim that a Universal Antecedent Yields a Particular Conclusion
Author refutes later scholar's claim about universal antecedent yielding particular conclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the validity of a mixed syllogism when the antecedent is universal, and explain why the author refutes the view that it produces a particular conditional conclusion.

Core Idea
A universal antecedent does not guarantee a particular conclusion unless the middle term is coextensive.

In this passage, the author engages with an opinion attributed to 'the best of the later scholars' who claimed that if the antecedent of the conditional is universal, the mixed syllogism yields a particular conditional conclusion. The author systematically refutes this claim using a counterexample and analyzing the conditions for production. This lesson unpacks that critique and clarifies the correct logical conditions.

Guided Path
1
State the claim under attack
The later scholar held that when the antecedent of the conditional is universal, the conclusion is a particular conditional (e.g., 'It may be that if every B is A then H is R').
This claim is about the modality of the conclusion: particular rather than universal.
2
Follow the author's counterexample
The author gives: (Every B is J) and (Everything that is every J is A then ...). The antecedent of the conclusion would be 'Every B is A', but this does not imply 'Every J is A' unless B and J are coextensive.
The author uses variables: ب (B), ج (J), أ (A), هـ (H), ر (R).
3
Understand the objection
An objector might say: since B may or may not be equal to J, the conclusion could be particular (i.e., true in some cases).
The objection is that the conclusion is possible particular.
4
Analyze the author's response
The author counters: the same uncertainty applies to the truth of the antecedent. Only when the antecedent is actually true does the syllogism produce a conclusion. If B is not equal to J, the conclusion is false entirely, not even particularly true.
Therefore, the conclusion cannot be called particular; it is either universally true or false.
5
Conclude the correct condition
The correct condition is that the antecedent must be true in itself. When it is true, the conclusion can be universal even if both premises are particular. This refutes the later scholar's view.
The author emphasizes that the later scholar's view is 'not correct' (ليس بحق).

فأما إن كانت الحملية مكان الصغرى والشرطية مكان الكبرى فالاشتراك ما بين ومقدم الشرطية، فإما أن يكون الحد المشترك بينهما محمولاً في الحملية وموضوعاً في المقدم، وإما محمولاً فيهما، وإما موضوعاً فيهما، وإما موضوعاً في الحملية ومحمولاً في المقدم، فإن كان محمولاً في الحملية وموضوعاً في المقدم فهو الكائن على نمط الشكل الأول، فشرطه أن يكون المقدم حقاً في نفسه، ولا ينتج إذا كان المقدم كاذباً، فإنا إذا حولنا شرط وجود ("هـ" "ر") مثلاً إلى ("ج" "أ") أو سلب عن "ج" فلا يلزم من فلا يلزم من حكمنا بـ "أ" على "ب" إيجاباً أو سلباً تحقق شرط "هـ" بتقدير أن يكون الشرط محالاً في نفس الأمر، فلا يلزمه ("هـ" "ر") ولنمثل ذلك إذا كانت الحملية والشرطية كليتين موجبتين والمقدم جزئياً موجباً – وأنت تعرفه في الباقي - فنقول (كل "ب" "ج") و(كل ما كان بعض "ج" ف) فإذا استنتجنا من الحملية والمقدم نتيجة وجعلناها مقدمة في النتيجة لا يلزمها ("هـ" "ر") وذلك كما لو قلنا فـ كل ما كان "ب" "أ") أو (بعض "ب" "أ" فـ "هـ" "ر") لم يكن لازماً، بتقدير أن لا يكون "ج" "أ" حقاً، ولا هو لازم عن قولنا: (كل "ب" "أ") ولا عن بعض "ب" "أ" وبتقدير أن يكون المقدم حقاً في نفس الأمر فشرطه أيضاً أن تكون الحملية موجبة، وأن يكون المقدم جزئياً، قال أفضل المتأخرين إذا كان المقدم كلياً أنتج نتيجة متصلة جزئية، وليس بحق، فإنا إذا قلنا مثلاً (كل "ب" "ج") و(كل ما كان كل "ج" "أ" فـ ار) فالمقدم للنتيجة المأخوذة من الحملية ومقدم الشرطية وهو قد يكون إذا كان كل "ب" "أ" لا يلازمه كل "ج" "أ" الذي هو شرط "هـ" "ر") فلا يلزم عنه ("هـ" "ر") إلا بتقدير أن يكون "ب" مساوياً لـ "ج" وبتقدير أن لا يكون مساوياً لـ "ج" فلا يلزم من قولنا كل ما كان كل "ب" "أ" في "هـ" "ر"). فإن قيل: لما احتمل أن يكون "ب" مساوياً لـ "ج" واحتمل أن لا يكون صح أن النتيجة وهي أنه قد يكون إذا كان كل "ب" "أ" فـ "هـ" "ر"). فنقول: وكما احتمل أن يكون "ب" مساوياً لـ "ج" واحتمل أن لا يكون فكذلك احتمل أن يكون المقدم حقاً، واحتمل أن لا يكون وبتقدير أن يكون حقاً فالاقتران منتج، وبتقدير أن لا يكون حقاً فلا إنتاج، فكان ينبغي أن يجزم بكون النتيجة أيضاً جزئية من غير شرط أن يكون المقدم حقاً، وقد شرط أن يكون المقدم حقاً في الإنتاج، كيف وأنه بتقدير أن لا يكون مساوياً لألف فلا تصدق النتيجة أصلاً، ولا في حالة ما والجزئية فلا معنى لها إلا ما صدقها في حالة ما، وخاصة أنه ينتج الكلي وإن كان المقدم والحملية جزئيين
As for when the categorical is in the place of the minor and the conditional is in the place of the major, the commonality is between and the antecedent of the conditional. So either the common term between them is predicate in the categorical and subject in the antecedent, or predicate in both, or subject in both, or subject in the categorical and predicate in the antecedent. If it is predicate in the categorical and subject in the antecedent, it is in the pattern of the first figure. Its condition is that the antecedent is true in itself, and it does not produce if the antecedent is false. For if we transform the condition of existence of ('h', 'r') for example to ('j', 'a') or negation from 'j', then it does not follow from our judgment of 'a' upon 'b', affirmatively or negatively, the realization of the condition of 'h', assuming that the condition is impossible in itself, so ('h', 'r') does not follow. Let us exemplify that when the categorical and conditional are both universal affirmative and the antecedent is particular affirmative—and you know the rest—so we say (Every 'b' is 'j') and (Everything that is some 'j' is...). So if we infer from the categorical and the antecedent a conclusion and make it a premise in the conclusion, ('h', 'r') does not follow. That is as if we say: Every 'b' is 'a' or Some 'b' is 'a' then 'h' is 'r' is not necessary, assuming that 'j' is 'a' is not true, and it is not necessary from our saying (Every 'b' is 'a') nor from (Some 'b' is 'a'). And assuming that the antecedent is true in itself, its condition is also that the categorical be affirmative and that the antecedent be particular. The best of the later scholars said: if the antecedent is universal, it produces a particular conditional conclusion, and it is not correct. For if we say for example (Every 'b' is 'j') and (Everything that is every 'j' is 'a' then ...) the antecedent of the conclusion taken from the categorical and the antecedent of the conditional, it may be that when every 'b' is 'a' it does not entail every 'j' is 'a' which is the condition of ('h' is 'r'), so ('h' is 'r') does not follow except assuming that 'b' is equal to 'j'. And assuming it is not equal to 'j', then from our saying 'everything that is every 'b' is 'a' then 'h' is 'r'' does not follow. If it is said: since it is possible that 'b' is equal to 'j' and possible that it is not, then the conclusion is correct, namely that it may be that when every 'b' is 'a' then 'h' is 'r'. We say: just as it is possible that 'b' is equal to 'j' and possible that it is not, similarly it is possible that the antecedent is true and possible that it is not. Assuming it is true, the conjunction produces; assuming it is not true, there is no production. So one should affirm that the conclusion is also particular without the condition that the antecedent be true, and he conditioned that the antecedent be true for production. How so? Because assuming it is not equal to 'a', the conclusion is not true at all, not in any case, and particularity has no meaning except that it is true in some case. Especially since it produces the universal even if the antecedent and categorical are particular.
Source pages p. 316

Commentary
This passage is a detailed explanation of the rules for combining a categorical premise (as minor) with a conditional premise (as major) in a mixed syllogism. The author begins by stating the four possible relationships between the common term in the categorical and the antecedent of the conditional: (1) predicate in categorical, subject in antecedent; (2) predicate in both; (3) subject in both; (4) subject in categorical, predicate in antecedent. The first corresponds to the first figure of categorical syllogism. The condition for production is that the antecedent must be true in itself; if it is false, no conclusion follows. The author illustrates with an example where both premises are universal affirmative and the antecedent is particular affirmative. He then criticizes a view attributed to 'the best of the later scholars' who claimed that if the antecedent is universal, the conclusion is a particular conditional. The author refutes this with a counterexample showing that unless the middle term is coextensive (musawi) with the subject, the conclusion does not follow universally. He anticipates an objection: since the middle term may or may not be coextensive, the conclusion could be particular. He responds that the same uncertainty applies to the truth of the antecedent; only when the antecedent is actually true does the conjunction produce. In fact, under the assumption of non-coextension, the conclusion is false entirely. Therefore, the conclusion is not merely particular; it can be universal even when both premises are particular, if the antecedent is true. This discussion demonstrates the careful conditions required for valid mixed syllogisms in the tradition.

Key Terms
الحد المشترك (al-ḥadd al-mushtarak): The term shared between the categorical premise and the antecedent of the conditional premise in a mixed syllogism. [Note: No significant shift.]
المقدم (al-muqaddam): The antecedent of the conditional proposition in a mixed syllogism; also used for the minor premise in categorical syllogisms. [Note: In this context, 'muqaddam' refers specifically to the antecedent of the conditional, not the minor premise.]
الاقتران (al-iqtirān): A combination or conjugation of premises in a syllogism, here specifically a mixed conditional-categorical syllogism. [Note: In standard logic, 'iqtirān' often refers to the conjunctive syllogism (qiyās iqtirānī) as opposed to istithnā'ī.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

The author refutes the later scholar by showing that a universal antecedent does not guarantee a particular conclusion; the conclusion's truth depends on the antecedent's truth and coextension of the middle term.

First Two Conjugations of Mixed Syllogisms
Author enumerates the first two valid combinations of categorical and conditional premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the structure and conclusion of the first two mixed-syllogism conjugations combining a universal affirmative categorical premise with a conditional premise.

Core Idea
Mixed syllogisms combine categorical and conditional premises; the first two conjugations show how universality transfers from categorical to conditional conclusion.

In this passage, the author systematically enumerates the sixteen valid conjugations of mixed syllogisms. We focus on the first two, which both start with two universal affirmative premises: a categorical 'Every B is J' and a conditional with either a particular affirmative or particular negative antecedent. The conclusion is always a universal affirmative conditional, demonstrating a key pattern in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises
Note the categorical premise: a universal affirmative (e.g., Every B is J). The conditional premise is a universal affirmative conditional with particular antecedent (e.g., Whenever some J is A, then D is R).
The variables are placeholders for any terms.
2
Trace the conclusion
For the first conjugation, the conclusion is: Whenever every B is A, then D is R. Notice that the antecedent becomes universal (every B is A) and the consequent remains D is R.
The second conjugation yields: Whenever every B is not A, then D is R.
3
Understand the proof by inclusion
Since every B is J (from categorical), if every B is A, then some J is A. This triggers the conditional's antecedent, so D is R follows. The author also provides a proof by contradiction.
The logical relationship depends on the fact that B is a subset of J.
4
Compare the two conjugations
The first has a particular affirmative antecedent (some J is A); the second has a particular negative antecedent (some J is not A). Their conclusions have universal affirmative antecedents differing in quality: every B is A vs. every B is not A.
Both conclusions are universal affirmative conditionals.

And its productive conjunctions are sixteen conjugations. The first conjugation: from two universal affirmatives, and the antecedent in the conditional is particular affirmative. Its example is our saying: (Every 'b' is 'j') and (Whenever some 'j' is 'a', then 'd' is 'r'). Its conclusion is a universal affirmative conditional, and its antecedent is universal. Its composition is from the subject of the categorical and the predicate of the antecedent in the conditional, and its consequent is the consequent of the conditional. It is: Whenever every 'b' is 'a', then 'd' is 'r'. This is necessary because whenever some 'j' is 'a', then 'd' is 'r'. If (every 'b' is 'a') and 'b' falls under 'j', then the existence of (some 'j' is 'a') is realized, so ('d' is 'r') follows. Its conclusion is a universal affirmative conditional, its antecedent is universal negative, composed from the subject of the categorical.

Commentary
This passage enumerates the first two of the sixteen valid conjugations (iqtirānāt) of mixed syllogisms combining a categorical premise with a conditional premise. The author first states that there are sixteen productive conjugations. The first conjugation consists of two universal affirmative premises: a categorical (Every B is J) and a conditional with a particular affirmative antecedent (Whenever some J is A, then D is R). The conclusion is a universal affirmative conditional: Whenever every B is A, then D is R. The reasoning is that since B falls under J, if every B is A, then some J is A, which triggers the conditional's antecedent, yielding D is R. The author also provides a proof by contradiction: if the conclusion were false, its opposite would be true, leading to a situation where some J is A exists but D is not R, contradicting the original conditional. The second conjugation is similar but the conditional's antecedent is particular negative (some J is not A), and the conclusion has a universal negative antecedent (every B is not A). The structure uses variables common in logic texts. This enumeration is a systematic classification of all possible mixed syllogism forms, providing a complete tool for analysis in the Islamic logical tradition.
