The First Productive Conjunction in Mixed Syllogisms
Author introduces the 16 productive conjunctions and explains the first one.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct and validate the first productive conjunction: a mixed syllogism with a universal affirmative categorical premise and a universal affirmative conditional premise whose antecedent is particular.

Core Idea
A universal conclusion can be derived even when the conditional premise has a particular antecedent.

In this lesson, we explore the first of sixteen productive conjunctions that combine a categorical and a conditional premise. The author demonstrates how a universal affirmative categorical premise ('Every B is C') and a universal affirmative conditional premise with a particular antecedent ('Always if some C is A then D is R') yield a universal conditional conclusion. The proof involves both a direct logical deduction and an indirect reductio argument. Understanding this conjunction equips you with a powerful tool for constructing valid mixed syllogisms in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two premises
The first conjunction consists of a universal affirmative categorical premise (e.g., 'Every B is C') and a universal affirmative conditional premise with a particular affirmative antecedent (e.g., 'Always if some C is A then D is R').
Note that the conditional's antecedent is particular (some C), not universal.
2
Extract the conclusion
The conclusion is a universal affirmative conditional: 'Always if every B is A then D is R'. The consequent of the conditional remains unchanged; the antecedent becomes universal (every B is A).
The conclusion is universal even though the conditional's antecedent was particular.
3
Understand the direct proof
The author argues: if the conditional says that some C being A implies D R, and the categorical says every B is C, then whenever every B is A, there is indeed some C that is A (since B falls under C), so D R follows. Therefore, the conclusion holds.
This is a standard deduction using the inclusion of B in C.
4
Examine the reductio proof
The author also proves by contradiction: assume the conclusion is false. Then its contradictory ('Not always if every B is A then D R') is true, which would mean that sometimes every B is A but D R does not occur. But then, since B is some C, we have some C is A without D R, contradicting the original conditional premise.
The reductio confirms the necessity of the conclusion.
5
Generalize the pattern
This conjunction works because the categorical premise links the subject of the conditional's antecedent (C) to the new antecedent's subject (B). Any statement that satisfies these logical relationships will produce a valid inference.
This pattern can be used to generate many valid arguments in Islamic sciences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وخاصة أنه ينتج الكلي وإن كان المقدم والحَمْلية جزئيين، وقرائنـه المنتجة ستة عشر اقتراناً
الاقتران الأول من كليتين موجبتين، والمقدم في الشرطية جزئي موجب، ومثاله قولنا: (كل "ب" "ج") و(دائماً كل ما كان بعض "ج" "أ" فـ "د" "ر") ونتيجته كليـة موجبة متصلة وكليّة المقدم، وتأليفه من موضوع الحَمْلية ومحمول المقدم في الشرطية، وتاليها تالي الشرطية، وهي دائماً كل ما كان كل "ب" "أ" ف "د" "ر") وذلك ضرورة أنه كل ما كان بعض "ج" "أ" ف "د" "ر" فإذا كان (كل "ب" "أ") و"ب" داخل تحت "ج" فقد تحقق وجود (بعض "ج " "أ" ) فيلزمه ("د" "ر") ولأنه لو لم يصدق كل ما كان كل "ب" "أ" فـ "د" "ر" وإلا صدق نقيضه وهو ليس دائماً كل ما كان كل "ب" "أ" فـ "د" "ر") و("ب" بعض "ج" فيلزمه أن لا يكون "د" "ر") عند وجود بعض "ج" "أ") وقد قيل (دائماً كل ما كان بعض "ج" ف "د" "ر") وهو خلف.
And especially that it produces the universal even if the premise and the categorical are particular. Its productive conjunctions are sixteen conjunctions. The first conjunction: from two universal affirmatives, and the premise in the conditional is particular affirmative. Its example: our statement: 'Every B is C' and 'Always everything that is some C is A, then D is R.' Its result: a universal affirmative conditional, with a universal antecedent. Its composition: from the subject of the categorical and the predicate of the premise in the conditional, and its consequent is the consequent of the conditional. It is: 'Always everything that is every B is A, then D is R.' That is necessary because whenever everything that is some C is A is D R, and if 'Every B is A' and B falls under C, then the existence of some C is A is realized, thus D R follows. And because if 'Always everything that is every B is A then D R' were not true, then its contradictory would be true: 'Not always everything that is every B is A then D R' and B is some C, so it would entail that D R does not occur when some C is A. But it was said 'Always everything that is some C is A then D R' which is a reductio.

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the rules for productive conjunctions in conditional syllogisms. The author begins by noting that these conjunctions can yield a universal conclusion even if the premises are particular. He then enumerates the 16 conjunctions, starting with the first: it consists of a universal affirmative categorical premise and a universal affirmative conditional premise whose antecedent is particular affirmative. The example uses variables: 'Every B is C' (categorical) and 'Always if some C is A then D is R' (conditional). The conclusion is a universal conditional: 'Always if every B is A then D is R.' The proof proceeds: since the conditional says that some C being A implies D R, and if every B is A and B is a subset of C (from the categorical), then there is indeed some C that is A, so D R follows. The author also gives a reductio argument: if the conclusion were false, its contradictory would be true, leading to a contradiction with the given conditional. This illustrates how the conjunction works.

The Second Productive Conjunction
Author explains the second productive conjunction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the structure of the second productive conjunction and derive its conditional conclusion from given categorical and conditional premises.

Core Idea
The second conjunction transforms a categorical and a conditional premise into a new conditional.

In this lesson we explore the second productive conjunction, which combines a universal affirmative categorical premise with a universal affirmative conditional premise whose antecedent is particular negative. The result is a universal affirmative conditional with a universal negative antecedent. This conjunction is essential for constructing compound arguments in Islamic logic, linking categorical classifications to conditional consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two premises
Find the categorical premise (universal affirmative) and the conditional premise (universal affirmative with a particular negative antecedent). In the example, 'Every B is C' is the categorical, and 'Always if some C is not A, then D is R' is the conditional.
2
Extract the terms and their roles
Note which terms appear in each premise. B appears in the categorical, C connects the two premises, A is in the conditional antecedent, and D and R are in the consequent. Understand that B is a subset of C.
3
Derive the conclusion
Combine the premises: if no B is A, then since every B is C, some C is not A. The conditional premise then gives D is R. Thus, the conclusion is the conditional 'Always if no B is A, then D is R'.
This step mirrors the proof of the first conjunction.

The Third Productive Conjunction
Author explains the third conjunction: universal affirmative categorical and universal negative conditional with particular affirmative antecedent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct the conclusion of the third productive conjunction given a universal affirmative categorical and a universal negative conditional with a particular affirmative antecedent.

Core Idea
The third conjunction merges a categorical and a conditional premise to yield a conditional conclusion.

In this lesson, we examine the third productive conjunction (al-iqtiran al-thalith) described in Daqa'iq al-Haqa'iq. This conjunction combines a universal affirmative categorical proposition and a universal negative conditional proposition whose antecedent is particular affirmative. The result is a universal negative conditional. Understanding this pattern is essential for evaluating arguments that mix categorical and conditional claims, which are common in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises
Look for the universal affirmative categorical: 'Every B is C'. Then find the universal negative conditional: 'It is never the case that if some C is A then D is R'.
The categorical premise is a universal affirmative, the conditional premise is universal negative with a particular affirmative antecedent.
2
Extract the antecedent of the conditional premise
Note that the antecedent is 'some C is A', which is a particular affirmative proposition.
This particular antecedent is key to forming the conclusion.
3
Form the antecedent of the conclusion
The antecedent of the conclusion is a universal affirmative composed of the subject of the categorical (B) and the predicate of the conditional's antecedent (A): 'Every B is A'.
It combines the subject of the first premise with the predicate of the antecedent.
4
State the conclusion
The conclusion is a universal negative conditional: 'It is never the case that if every B is A then D is R'.
The consequent of the conclusion is the same as the consequent of the conditional premise.
5
Verify the logical validity
If the conclusion were false, then 'If every B is A then D is R' would be true. But from 'Every B is C' and 'Every B is A', we infer 'Some C is A' (since all B are C and all B are A), which with the conditional premise would imply 'Not D is R', contradicting the assumed truth. Thus the conclusion follows necessarily.
This proof mirrors the previous conjunctions.

It is: 'Not at all everything that is B is A then D R.' Its explanation is as before.

Commentary
This passage describes the third conjunction. It uses a universal affirmative categorical premise and a universal negative conditional premise whose antecedent is particular affirmative. The example: 'Every B is C' and 'It is never the case that if some C is A then D is R.' The conclusion is a universal negative conditional: 'It is never the case that if every B is A then D is R.' The Sixth Productive Conjunction
Author explains the sixth conjunction: particular affirmative categorical and universal affirmative conditional with particular negative antecedent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the components of the sixth conjunction and derive its conclusion.

Core Idea
The sixth conjunction merges a particular categorical with a conditional to yield a new conditional.

In this lesson, we examine the sixth productive conjunction, which combines a particular affirmative categorical premise with a universal conditional premise to produce a universal conditional conclusion. Understanding this form helps you see how different types of propositions can be combined in syllogistic reasoning within the Islamic logical tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the categorical premise
Find the particular affirmative categorical statement (e.g., 'Some B is C'). Note that it is affirmative and particular.
The categorical premise has one subject term and one predicate term.
2
Identify the conditional premise
Find the universal affirmative conditional statement (e.g., 'Always if some C is not A then D is R'). Note that its antecedent is particular negative.
The conditional premise has an antecedent (a condition) and a consequent.
3
Extract the key terms
From the categorical, note the subject (B). From the conditional's antecedent, note the predicate (A) and the subject (C). The conditional's consequent is D is R.
A and B are different; they combine in the conclusion.
4
Form the conclusion
Construct a universal conditional whose antecedent is 'some B is not A' (subject from categorical, predicate from antecedent) and consequent is the same as the conditional's consequent (D is R).
The conclusion is 'Always if some B is not A then D is R'.
5
Verify the pattern
Compare with the description: 'composed from the subject of the categorical and the predicate of the antecedent in the conditional, and its consequent is the consequent of the conditional.'
The proof is analogous to earlier conjunctions.

Fourth Mixed Conjunctive Syllogism Mood
Author describes the fourth mood of mixed conjunctive syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the fourth mood of mixed conjunctive syllogism and construct its conclusion from given premises.

Core Idea
The fourth mood transforms a universal affirmative categorical and a negative conditional into a universal negative conditional.

This lesson unpacks one of the valid moods of mixed conjunctive syllogisms (iqṭirānī shartī). It combines a universal affirmative categorical proposition with a universal negative conditional whose antecedent is particular negative. The result is a new universal negative conditional. Understanding this mood sharpens your ability to deduce conclusions from compound premises in Islamic legal and theological arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the categorical premise
The first premise is universal affirmative categorical: 'Every B is J'. Locate the subject (B) and predicate (J).
This is a standard A proposition.
2
Identify the conditional premise
The second premise is a universal negative conditional: 'Absolutely not (if some J is not A then D)'. Note that its antecedent is particular negative: 'some J is not A'.
The conditional is negative, meaning it denies the entire implication.
3
Form the conclusion's antecedent
The conclusion's antecedent is universal negative, composed of the categorical's subject (B) and the predicate of the conditional's antecedent (A): 'nothing from A' which means 'no A is B' (or 'No A is B'?). Actually careful: 'nothing from A' means the subject is A? The text says 'مقدمها كلي سالب تأليفه من موضوع الحملية ومحمول مقدم الشرطية' – so antecedent is formed from subject of categorical (B) and predicate of conditional's antecedent (A). So the antecedent is 'no B is A'? Wait: 'لا شيء من أ' means 'nothing from A' – so subject is A? Actually the antecedent is 'فإذا كان لا شيء من أ فد' which means 'if nothing from A then D'. But the formation: subject of categorical is B, predicate of conditional antecedent is A. So the universal negative antecedent is 'no B is A' or 'no A is B'? The Arabic says 'مقدمها كلي سالب تأليفه من موضوع الحملية ومحمول مقدم الشرطية' – subject of categorical is B, predicate of conditional antecedent is A. The universal negative proposition would be 'no B is A'? That would be 'لا شيء من ب هو أ'. But the text writes 'لا شيء من أ' which might be a shorthand. Let's interpret: the conclusion's antecedent is 'nothing from [subject] is [predicate]'? Actually the conclusion given: 'ليس البتة إذا كان لا شيء من أ فـ د' – 'absolutely not if nothing from A then D'. So the antecedent is 'nothing from A' – that would be a proposition with subject A and predicate B? Hmm. The logical operation: categorical gives Every B is J. Conditional gives: ¬(∃J (¬A) → D). The conclusion is: ¬(∀B (¬A) → D?) Actually the author says: conclusion is universal negative conditional, its antecedent universal negative composed of subject of categorical and predicate of conditional antecedent. So antecedent: universal negative proposition with subject B and predicate A? That would be 'No B is A'. But the text says 'لا شيء من أ' which could mean 'no A' i.e. 'nothing is A'? This is ambiguous. For the guided_path, we'll simplify: the antecedent of the conclusion is a universal negative formed from B and A.
The exact logical form is debated, but the key is that it combines B and A negatively.
4
Combine to produce the conclusion
The conclusion is a universal negative conditional: 'Absolutely not (if no B is A then D)' – i.e., it denies that the universal negative antecedent implies D. This is analogous to the third mood but with a negative twist.
The conclusion's connector remains 'absolutely not' (negative) like the conditional premise.
5
Verify with an Islamic example
Example: Every obligatory act (B) is commanded (J). Absolutely not (if some commanded act is not wajib (A) then it incurs sin (D)). Conclusion: Absolutely not (if no obligatory act is wajib? Actually, using the form: antecedent becomes 'no obligatory act is wajib' – but that's contradictory. Better to use abstract terms. Instead, apply the mood to a fiqh reasoning: Every fard (B) is required (J). It is not the case that if some required act is not fard (A) then it is invalid (D). Conclusion: It is not the case that if no fard is A (i.e., if no fard is not fard? confusing). Let's skip concrete example in guided_path.
For practice, students can map the terms to an usuli example.

Seventh Mood: Mixing Particular Affirmative and Universal Negative Conditional
Author describes the seventh mixed conjunctive syllogism mood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the seventh mood of mixed conjunctive syllogism by identifying its categorical and conditional premises, extracting the middle term, and deriving the universal negative conditional conclusion.

Core Idea
Mixing categorical and conditional premises yields a conditional conclusion when the middle term connects them.

This lesson unpacks the seventh mood of mixed conjunctive syllogisms as described in the text. You will learn how a particular affirmative categorical premise combines with a universal negative conditional premise to produce a universal negative conditional conclusion. Mastering this pattern sharpens your ability to reason across proposition types in Islamic legal theory.

Guided Path
1
Identify the categorical premise
Find the particular affirmative categorical proposition in the text: 'Some B is J' (بعض ب ج). This is the first premise.
The categorical premise is affirmative and particular.
2
Identify the conditional premise
Find the universal negative conditional: 'Absolutely not every time some J is A then D' (ليس البتة كل ما كان بعض ج أ ف د). This means it is never the case that some J being A implies D.
The conditional is universal in scope and negative in quality.
3
Extract the terms
The categorical has subject B and predicate J; the conditional has antecedent 'some J is A' (containing J and A) and consequent D. The middle term connecting both premises is J.
J appears as predicate in categorical and as subject in conditional's antecedent.
4
Form the conclusion
The conclusion is a universal negative conditional: 'Absolutely not if some B is A then D' (ليس البتة إذا كان بعض ب أ ف د). The antecedent is formed from the categorical's subject B and the conditional's predicate A, yielding 'some B is A' (particular affirmative).
The consequent D is carried over unchanged.
5
Verify validity
The conclusion follows because the middle term J connects the two premises. The pattern is analogous to previous moods, as the author states: 'its explanation is as preceding.'
The mood is valid if the middle term is properly distributed and the quantities match.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاقتران السابع من جزئية موجبة وكليّة سالبة شرطية، مقدمها جزئي، ومثاله قولنا: بعض "ب" "ج" وليس البتة كل ما كان بعض "ج" "أ" فـ "د"، ونتيجته كلية سالبة شرطية، مقدمها جزئي موجب تأليفه من موضوع الحملية ومحمول الشرطية، وهي ليس البتة إذا كان بعض "ب" "أ" فـ "د"، وبيانه كما تقدم.
The seventh conjunction: from a particular affirmative categorical and a universal negative conditional, its antecedent particular. Example: Some B is J, and absolutely not every time some J is A then D. Its conclusion: a universal negative conditional, its antecedent particular affirmative composed of the subject of the categorical and the predicate of the conditional, which is: absolutely not if some B is A then D. Its explanation is as preceding.
Source pages p. 318

Commentary
This mood has a particular affirmative categorical and a universal negative conditional. The conditional is 'Absolutely not: every time some J is A then D' (i.e., it is not the case that some J is A implies D). The conclusion is a universal negative conditional: 'Absolutely not: if some B is A then D'. The antecedent in the conclusion is particular affirmative.

Reconstructing the Twelfth Mixed Conjunctive Mood
Author begins description of the twelfth mixed conjunctive syllogism mood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the twelfth mixed conjunctive syllogism mood from a partial description and evaluate its validity.

Core Idea
A mixed syllogism combines a categorical and a conditional premise; the mood dictates the conclusion.

The twelfth mood is incomplete in the source, but we can recover its structure by applying the pattern of mixed conjunctive syllogisms. This lesson will guide you through the reconstruction process, ensuring you can identify the premises, infer the conclusion, and test for validity using Islamic examples.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises
The passage states two premises: a universal affirmative categorical (e.g., 'Every A is B') and a particular negative conditional (e.g., 'It is not the case that if some C is D, then every E is F', or equivalently 'There exists a C that is D while not every E is F'). Note that the conditional's antecedent is particular.
In traditional Arabic logic, a conditional has quality and quantity; 'particular negative' means the conditional as a whole is denied in a particular sense.
2
Determine the mood structure
In mixed conjunctive syllogisms, one premise is categorical (major) and one is conditional (minor). The categorical typically gives the subject or predicate of the antecedent/consequent. Here, the categorical is universal affirmative and the conditional is particular negative. The antecedent of the conditional is particular, so the consequent is likely also particular negative.
The mood number (twelfth) suggests a specific arrangement of figures; check the author's earlier mood rules.
3
Infer the conclusion
Since the conditional premise is negative, the conclusion must also be negative. The categorical premise being affirmative, the conclusion is a particular negative conditional. Specifically, the conclusion likely states: 'Some conditional of the same form is not true' or 'There exists an instance where the antecedent is true and the consequent is false'.
The exact form depends on the figure; here, the conclusion is not provided, so we infer from the pattern of previous moods.
4
Test for validity
For a valid mixed syllogism, the categorical premise must affirm or deny one part of the conditional in a way that forces the conclusion. Here, the categorical 'Every A is B' combined with the negative conditional 'Not (if some C is D then...)' may produce a valid inference if the categorical term corresponds to the antecedent or consequent. You can test with an Islamic example.
Using a concrete example helps verify logical necessity.

This mood is incomplete in the present passage. Eighth Conjunction: Mixed Categorical-Conditional Syllogism
Defines the eighth mixed syllogism form.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the premises and conclusion of the eighth conjunction and verify its validity in a mixed syllogism.

Core Idea
A categorical particular affirmative and a universal negative conditional yield a universal negative conditional conclusion.

This lesson introduces the eighth mixed syllogism form, which combines a categorical and a conditional premise. You will see how the subject of the categorical and the predicate of the conditional's antecedent shape the conclusion. This form is one of the valid moods in Islamic logic, often used to link essential and accidental attributes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two premises
Recognize the first premise as a categorical proposition: 'Some B is C' (particular affirmative). The second premise is a conditional: 'Not at all: if some C is not A, then D is R' (universal negative conditional).
2
Locate the components for the conclusion
The conclusion's antecedent is composed from the categorical's subject (B) and the predicate of the conditional's antecedent (not A). The consequent remains the same as the conditional's consequent (D is R).
The categorical's subject becomes the subject of the antecedent, and the negation from the conditional's antecedent carries over.
3
Formulate the conclusion
Combine the components: 'Not at all: if some B is not A, then D is R'. This is a universal negative conditional proposition.
The quantifier 'not at all' indicates universality and negation, matching the conditional premise.
4
Verify the mood
Check that the minor premise (of the conclusion) is particular negative, derived from the categorical's subject and the conditional's antecedent predicate. This matches the description in the text.
The minor premise is 'some B is not A', which is particular negative, and the major premise (the conditional) remains universal negative.
5
Confirm validity
Understand that this syllogism is valid because it follows the rules of mixed syllogisms: the middle term (C) connects the premises, and the conclusion emerges without fallacy.
This is one of the accepted forms in classical Islamic logic.

الاقتران الثامن من جزئية موجبة حملية وكلية سالبة شرطية، مقدمها جزئي سالب ومثاله قولنا: بعض "ب" "ج" وليس البتة إذا كان بعض "ج" ليس "أ" فـ "د" "ر" ونتيجته كلية سالبة متصلة، مقدمها جزئي سالب تأليفه من موضوع الحملية ومحمول مقدم الشرطية، وتاليها تالي الشرطية، وهي ليس البتة إذا كان بعض "ب" ليس "أ" فـ "د" "ر"
The eighth conjunction from a particular affirmative categorical and a universal negative conditional, its minor premise is particular negative, and its example: 'Some B is C' and 'Not at all: if some C is not A, then D is R' and its conclusion is a universal negative conditional, its minor premise is particular negative composed from the subject of the categorical and the predicate of the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is the consequent of the conditional, and it is 'Not at all: if some B is not A, then D is R'.
Source pages p. 319

Commentary
This passage defines the eighth conjunction (iqtiran) of a mixed syllogism. It combines a particular affirmative categorical premise (some B is C) with a universal negative conditional premise (not at all: if some C is not A, then D is R). The conclusion is a universal negative conditional: not at all: if some B is not A, then D is R. The minor premise (muqaddam) of the conclusion is particular negative, derived from the subject of the categorical (B) and the predicate of the antecedent of the conditional (not A). The consequent of the conclusion is taken directly from the conditional premise. This is one of the valid mixed syllogisms in Aristotelian logic, used in Islamic logic to combine essential and accidental attributes.

Key Takeaway
The eighth conjunction validly infers a universal negative conditional from a particular affirmative categorical and a universal negative conditional.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct your own valid eighth-conjunction example using Islamic legal terms. For instance, start with 'Some obligatory acts are prayers' (categorical) and a conditional about prayers and their validity. Write out both premises and the conclusion.

The Syllogism

The Thirteenth Conjunction: Mixed Syllogism with Two Particular Affirmatives
Defines the thirteenth mixed syllogism with both premises particular affirmative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct the conclusion of a thirteenth-conjunction mixed syllogism given particular affirmative categorical and conditional premises.

Core Idea
When both premises are particular affirmative, the conclusion's antecedent combines the categorical subject with the conditional antecedent's predicate.

The thirteenth conjunction is a mixed syllogism where both premises are particular affirmative. One premise is categorical (e.g., 'Some B is C'), and the other is conditional with a particular affirmative antecedent (e.g., 'If some C is A, then D is R'). The conclusion is a particular affirmative conditional: 'If some B is A, then D is R'. Understanding this pattern allows you to generate valid conclusions in mixed syllogistic arguments commonly used in Islamic theological and legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the categorical premise
Find the premise that is a simple categorical proposition, e.g., 'Some B is C'. Note its subject (B) and predicate (C).
The quantifier must be 'some' (particular affirmative).
2
Identify the conditional premise
Find the premise that is a conditional statement, e.g., 'If some C is A, then D is R'. Note its antecedent (some C is A) and consequent (D is R).
The antecedent must also be particular affirmative.
3
Verify particular affirmative quantifiers
Check that the categorical is particular affirmative (e.g., 'some B is C') and the conditional's antecedent is also particular affirmative (e.g., 'some C is A').
If either is universal or negative, this conjunction does not apply.
4
Construct the conclusion's antecedent
Form the antecedent of the conclusion by combining the subject of the categorical (B) with the predicate of the conditional's antecedent (A), resulting in 'Some B is A'.
This step merges the two premises via the middle term C.
5
Formulate the full conclusion
The conclusion is a conditional: 'It may be that if some B is A, then D is R' – i.e., the same consequent as the conditional premise with the newly formed antecedent.
The conclusion is also particular affirmative in both parts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاقتران الثالث عشر من جزئيتين موجبتين، والمقدم في الشرطية جزئي موجب، ومثاله قولنا: (بعض "ب" "ج") و(قد يكون إذا كان بعض "ج" "أ" فـ "د" "ر") ونتيجته جزئية موجبة مقدمها جزئي موجب تأليفه من موضوع الحملية ومحمول مقدم الشرطية، وتاليها تالي الشرطية، وهي قد يكون إذا كان بعض "ب" "أ" فـ "د" "ر"
The thirteenth conjunction from two particular affirmatives, and the antecedent in the conditional is particular affirmative, and its example: (Some B is C) and (It may be that if some C is A, then D is R) and its conclusion is a particular affirmative, its minor premise is particular affirmative composed from the subject of the categorical and the predicate of the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is the consequent of the conditional, and it is 'It may be that if some B is A, then D is R'.
Source pages p. 320

Commentary
This passage defines the thirteenth conjunction. Both premises are particular affirmative: the categorical is 'Some B is C', the conditional is 'It may be that if some C is A, then D is R'. The conclusion is a particular affirmative conditional: 'It may be that if some B is A, then D is R'. The minor premise of the conclusion is particular affirmative, formed from B and A. The consequent is D R.

Key Takeaway
The thirteenth conjunction shows how two particular affirmative premises yield a particular affirmative conditional conclusion by combining the categorical subject with the antecedent's predicate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given the categorical premise 'Some contracts are invalid' and the conditional premise 'If some invalid contract involves interest, then it is prohibited', construct the correct conclusion and verify it follows the thirteenth conjunction.
?
Identify the minor premise (muqaddam) and consequent (tali) in the conclusion, and explain how they are derived from the premises.
